801 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bais


Crim. No. 10638


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


31 Cal. App. 3d 663; 107 Cal. Rptr. 519; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1260



April 17, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 13, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 51192, Gordon L. Minder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Alameda County (California), which, after granting the prosecution's motion to discover extra-judicial alibi witness statements over defense objections, convicted appellant of robbery in the first degree in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested after he was identified as having committed a robbery with an accomplice. The state charged appellant with robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. During the trial, appellant presented an alibi defense through four witnesses. Following the direct examination of one witness, and prior to cross-examination, the prosecutor filed a motion to discover extra-judicial statements made by the witness and another witness. Appellant's counsel objected, but the lower court allowed the discovery. Appellant was subsequently convicted of robbery in the first degree. Appellant contested the conviction. On appeal, the conviction was reversed. The court applied the test under Prudhomme v. Superior Court, 2 Cal.3d 320, 85 Cal. Rptr. 129, 466 P.2d 673 (1970), and held that the lower court's discovery was erroneous, because the matters disclosed could have conceivably lighten the burden which the prosecution bore in bringing about appellant's conviction. The discovery order violated appellant's Fifth Amendment right to be free from compulsory self-incrimination. It was therefore void and unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. Appellant was improperly convicted for robbery in the first degree, because the lower court's error in allowing the prosecution to discover extra-judicial alibi witness statements violated appellant's constitutional privilege against self-incrimination, and such error was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.

 

802 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. McNaught


Civ. No. 22540


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


31 Cal. App. 3d 599; 107 Cal. Rptr. 566; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1098



April 12, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 27, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 7, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Santa Barbara-Goleta Judicial District of Santa Barbara County, Nos. D-25965, D-26101, Frank P. Kearney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In case number D-26101 in the same court, the judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Santa Barbara-Goleta Judicial District of Santa Barbara County (California), which revoked defendant's probation and convicted him of being "drunk in public."


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from the municipal court's order revoking his probation for a previous conviction and from its final judgment of conviction for being "drunk in public." Defendant contended that he did not commit a public offense. The court ordered the matter transferred to it on certification from the superior court's appellate department. The court found that the effect of Cal. Penal Code § 647(ff) was that in counties that operated detoxification centers, simple violations of Cal. Penal Code § 647(f) were no longer crimes, but the county in which defendant was arrested did not operate such a center. Defendant argued that to treat him as a criminal for a condition that in counties having a detoxification center would cause him to become a patient discriminated against him, as a denial of equal protection. The court affirmed, finding no violation of equal protection, because territorial uniformity was not a constitutional prerequisite. The court concluded that the problem was political, not legal.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the municipal court that revoked defendant's probation and convicted him of being "drunk in public" was affirmed, as no violation of defendant's equal protection rights existed because the county in which he was arrested did not have a detoxification facility to which he could have been diverted.

 

803 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Orser


Crim. Nos. 10030, 10318


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


31 Cal. App. 3d 528; 107 Cal. Rptr. 458; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1259



April 11, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, Nos. 145801, 139038, Robert F. Kane and W. Howard Hartley, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The respective judgments are reversed with directions to the trial court to dismiss the respective informations.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant abortion advocates sought review of two orders from the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California), which entered judgments convicting defendants of violating Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 601 by publishing notices that advertised services and means for producing or facilitating a miscarriage or abortion.


OVERVIEW: Defendants provided numerous women with birth control and information about abortion services. Defendants were arrested and charged in separate actions with violating Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 601, which restricted the content of abortion information published in notices and advertisements. Defendants contended that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 610 was unconstitutional. The lower court entered judgments conviction defendants. On appeal, the convictions were reversed. The court held that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 601 was unconstitutional, because it was overbroad. Abortions performed pursuant to the Therapeutic Abortion Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 25950- 25954, shorn of its invalid provisions, were permitted. Because Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 601, however, did not distinguish between abortions, which were permitted, and those, which were not, its broad language, encompassed activity which was legal and activity which was illegal.


OUTCOME: The lower court's convictions of defendant abortion advocates were reversed with instructions for the informations against them to be dismissed. The appellate court held that the relevant statute restricting publications of notices advertising abortion services was overbroad and thus violated defendants' First Amendment right to freedom of speech.

 

804 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Leonard v. Occidental Life Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 29349


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


31 Cal. App. 3d 117; 106 Cal. Rptr. 899; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1057



March 14, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 9, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 609354, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of defendant in plaintiff's suit to recover alleged community property interest in life insurance policies on the life of her deceased husband. The court found under Cal. Ins. Code § 10172, defendant discharged its obligations under the policies by making payment to beneficiaries before receiving notice of claim by plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the widow of decedent. At the time of their marriage, decedent had three adult children from a prior marriage. At the time of his death, decedent left two life insurance policies with defendant, insurance company. One policy listed the children only as beneficiaries. The second policy listed the children and appellant as beneficiaries. The benefits were paid to the respective beneficiaries. Approximately one week later, appellant gave written notice to defendant of her claim to community interest in these policies. Plaintiff then brought action to recover of her alleged community property interest. Defendant set up the defense that, pursuant to the terms of the policies, it had paid the designated beneficiaries. The lower court granted summary judgment for defendant, finding that under Cal. Ins. Code § 10172, defendant had discharged its obligations under the policies by making payment to the beneficiaries before having received notice of a claim by plaintiff, widow. The court affirmed, holding defendant was not required to investigate the relationship, whether cordial or hostile, of those persons to whom, by the terms of the policies, it must make payment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the policy called for immediate payment, and defendant was not obligated to withhold payment in order to investigate the relationships of those persons to whom, by the terms of the policy, it must make payment.

 

805 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hirst


Crim. No. 5323


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


31 Cal. App. 3d 75; 106 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1051



March 9, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County, Nos. M-100065, M-100074 and M-097571, Frank H. Nottbush, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The evidence in this case shows that all defendants were upon the school ground for the purpose of distributing literature, and in the case of Hirst, also to talk to a student, neither of which is itself for an unlawful purpose; since the statute under which they were prosecuted does not penalize their presence unless it was for an unlawful purpose, the judgments are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision from the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County (California), which convicted them in a nonjury trial for violations of Cal. Penal Code § 653g.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that the section under which they were prosecuted, Cal. Penal Code § 653g, was unconstitutional, that if otherwise constitutional it was unconstitutional as applied to the conduct of defendants, which was protected under the U.S. Const. amend. I, and that the complaint was deficient to meet due process requirements that the defendants be informed of the charges against them. On appeal the court held that § 653g, as amended, was constitutional and that an accusatory pleading intended to charge a violation of § 653g was sufficient as against a demurrer if it was in the language of the statute. However, the court held that the evidence in the case showed that all defendants were upon a school ground for the purpose of distributing literature, and in the case of one of the defendants, also to talk to a student. The court held that neither of these reasons is itself an unlawful purpose. The court held that the statute under which defendants were prosecuted did not penalize their presence unless it was for an unlawful purpose. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgments of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the trial court, which convicted defendants for loitering about any school or public place at or near which children attend or normally congregate. The court held that defendants' reasons for being at the school were not unlawful, and that the statute under which defendants were prosecuted did not penalize their presence unless it was for an unlawful purpose.

 

806 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Taschner v. City Council


Civ. No. 12090


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


31 Cal. App. 3d 48; 107 Cal. Rptr. 214; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1049



March 8, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 27, 1973, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1973. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. M-1838, William S. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed with directions to overrule respondent's demurrer and to take further proceedings in this matter consistent with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which sustained respondent City's general demurrer without leave to amend and entered a judgment of dismissal. In the court below, appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate and sought to challenge the validity of an initiative building height limit ordinance of respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner contended a building height ordinance was a zoning measure and that its enactment by the initiative process was proscribed by the State Planning and Zoning Law, Cal. Gov't Code § 65000 et seq. (State Zoning Law). Respondent city argued that the judgment of dismissal was justified on the procedural grounds that appellant lacked standing to maintain the action, and that mandate was not a proper remedy. On the merits respondent argued that the ordinance was not a zoning regulation and, considered as a zoning measure, its enactment constituted a valid exercise of the people's constitutional power to legislate by initiative. On appeal, the court concluded that the judgment of dismissal could not be sustained on the procedural grounds advanced by respondent, and that the ordinance was a zoning regulation and as such its enactment by the initiative process was in violation of the State Zoning Law and the due process clause of the U.S. Const. amend XIV. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which sustained respondent City's general demurrer without leave to amend and entered a judgment of dismissal. The court held that the ordinance was a zoning regulation, its enactment by the initiative process was in violation of due process and statutory zoning laws, and the judgment of dismissal could not be sustained on the procedural grounds advanced by respondent.

 

807 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ladd v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 40193


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


31 Cal. App. 3d 35; 106 Cal. Rptr. 885; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1047



March 8, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 924269, Steven S. Weisman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying recovery in a suit for refund of sales and use taxes paid by appellant pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6006. Appellant contended that § 6006 unconstitutionally discriminated upon lessees and renters of personal property who lease or rent personal property in a different form than that in which it was acquired by the lessor.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was engaged in the construction and rental of powered houseboats. Beginning August 1, 1965, appellant followed the mandate of amended Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6006 (§ 6006 ) and paid a "use" tax measured by his receipts from the rental of houseboats. He did not, as required by the statute, collect use tax from his customers. From August 1, 1965, onward, appellant purchased materials for the construction, repair, and maintenance of houseboats for resale, paying no sales tax reimbursement or use tax as a result of those purchases. The following year, appellant filed a claim for refund for use taxes paid by him subsequent to August 1, 1965. Appellee board and the trial court denied the claims. On review, appellant contended that § 6006 unconstitutionally discriminated upon lessees and renters of personal property who lease or rent personal property in a different form than that in which it was acquired by the lessor. The appeals court affirmed because there was a rational basis for the distinction. The court reasoned that where the lessor changes the form of the property, he, of necessity, adds to its value the cost of labor involved in the change.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed the judgment denying recovery in appellant's suit for refund of sales and use taxes because the use tax statute passed constitutional muster. The court ruled that the distinction between parties in the tax statute did not violate the equal protection clause because it rested upon a rational basis.

 

808 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Henry's Restaurants of Pomona, Inc. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 39970


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


30 Cal. App. 3d 1009; 106 Cal. Rptr. 867; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1227



March 5, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 925358-925362, Steven S. Weisman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the trial court is directed to enter judgment for defendant State Board of Equalization.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State Board of Equalization sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) awarding judgment to plaintiff restaurant owners in their action to recover sales taxes paid under protest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff restaurant owners filed an action against defendant State Board of Equalization to recover sales taxes paid under protest. Plaintiffs claimed that the sales were exempted by Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 6359. The trial court awarded judgment to plaintiffs, finding § 6359, together with defendant's manner of administration thereof, to be unconstitutional. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, finding that neither the statute nor the various formulae used to identify "bulk sales" could be characterized as arbitrary, capricious, or patently unreasonable. The statute in question was a permissible exercise of administrative discretion in carrying out the intent of the legislature. It merely served as a guide to taxpayers by describing, in an intelligible fashion, the exemption from taxation which the statute provided for sales of food products not ordinarily sold for immediate consumption. Accordingly, the court held that the weight of the authorities enjoined any further incursion by it into defendant's exercise of the authority delegated to it by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The order awarding judgment to plaintiff restaurant owners in their action against defendant State Board of Equalization to recover sales taxes paid under protest was reversed and remanded because neither the statute in question nor defendant's manner of administration thereof were unconstitutional.

 

809 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bradley v. Doherty


Civ. Nos. 31151, 30953


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


30 Cal. App. 3d 991; 106 Cal. Rptr. 725; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1226



March 1, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 158376, Frank B. Blum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owner challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sustained respondent tenants' demurrer with respect to appellant's cause of action seeking to recover money that he lost while gambling on respondents' "fixed" games. Appellant also sought review of the trial court's decision denying his request to appoint a receiver and his motion for a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Respondent tenants operated a cocktail bar on appellant owner's property. Appellant filed an action against respondents, alleging that they operated their business in a negligent manner and that respondents' negligence resulted in a substantial lost to appellant. Appellant further contended that respondents fraudulently induced him to gamble on "fixed" games, and, as a result, appellant lost a substantial amount of money. Respondents filed general and special demurrers to appellant's complaint. The trial court overruled all of respondents' demurrers except for the cause of action that asserted that appellant was entitled to recover the money that he lost gambling. The trial court also denied appellant's motion to have a receiver appointed and appellant's motion for preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that the doctrine of pari delicto was not unconstitutional when applied to an action to recover money lost in "fixed" gambling. The court also affirmed the trial court's decision denying appellant's motion to appoint a receiver and its motion for preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision sustaining respondent tenants' demurrer regarding the gambling claim, holding that the doctrine of pair delicto was not unconstitutionally applied to appellant's cause of action which sought to recover the money he lost on "fixed" gambling. The court also affirmed the trial court's decision denying appellant's motion to appoint a receiver and his motion for preliminary injunction.

 

810 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Joseph A.


Crim. No. 21955


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


30 Cal. App. 3d 880; 106 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1216



February 27, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 15, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 23, 1973. Tobriner, J., Mosk, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 415615, Newell Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained a petition that alleged that defendant committed voluntary manslaughter or murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 192(1). Defendant contended that the juvenile court erred in admitting into evidence a tape-recorded oral conversation between him and his uncle in which he admitted stabbing the victim.


OVERVIEW: Defendant minor was arrested for murder. Later that day, defendant's uncle went to the police station and told an officer that he wanted to talk to his nephew "by himself." Defendant then told his uncle that he had stabbed the victim. Without the knowledge or consent of either defendant or his uncle, their conversation was recorded. A petition was filed alleging that defendant committed murder. The juvenile court ordered that the petition be amended adding an allegation of voluntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192(1). The court then sustained the petition as amended and ordered that defendant be committed to the California Youth Authority. On appeal, defendant contended that the juvenile court erred in admitting into evidence over his objection a surreptitiously monitored and tape-recorded oral conversation between him and his uncle. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that the monitored conversation was properly admitted into evidence under the general rule that persons within a jail or police facility have no reasonable expectation of privacy as to conversations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the juvenile court's decision sustaining the petition that alleged that defendant minor committed murder or voluntary manslaughter. The court held that the juvenile court did not err when it admitted into evidence a tape recording of defendant admitting to his uncle that he stabbed the victim because persons in a jail facility have no reasonable expectation of privacy.

 

811 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bauman v. Islay Invs.


Civ. No. 40429


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


30 Cal. App. 3d 752; 106 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1262



February 22, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1973, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 94266, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which sustained defendant landlord's demurrer without leave to amend in plaintiff's action to determine her rights in cleaning fees.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tenant paid a nonrefundable cleaning fee pursuant to a uniform rental agreement with defendant landlord, which required payment prior to occupancy. Plaintiff brought a class action against defendant to determine the tenants' rights in fees collected on or after January 1, 1971, and not returned. Plaintiff alleged that the fees violated Cal. Civ. Code § 1951 (1971), because they exceeded the reasonable cost for cleaning apartments that were vacated on termination of each tenancy. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend. Plaintiff appealed. The court held that the fees were not liquidated damages or security deposits for maintaining the apartment under the agreement. The court reversed because plaintiff's complaint alleged facts that, if neither denied nor explained, entitled her to relief. The court held that plaintiff did not waive her rights by agreeing to the "nonrefundable" provision. The court rejected defendant's argument that § 1951 was vague or violative of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that sustained defendant landlord's demurrer in plaintiff tenant's action involving cleaning fees. The court held that plaintiff's complaint alleged facts that entitled her to statutory relief from unreasonable, nonrefundable cleaning costs.

 

812 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bauman v. Islay Investments


Civ. No. 40429


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


30 Cal. App. 3d 752; 106 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1202



February 22, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1973, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 94266, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which sustained defendant landlord's demurrer without leave to amend, in plaintiff's class action alleging that a nonrefundable "cleaning fee" lease provision violated Cal. Civ. Code § 1951.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tenant filed a class action on behalf of past and present tenants of defendant landlord's buildings, contending that the lease agreement violated Cal. Civ. Code § 1951. The lease agreement required that tenants pay a nonrefundable "cleaning fee." Defendants argued that the "cleaning fee" provision was not covered under § 1951 and that § 1951 did not apply because the tenants waived the protection of the statute by signing the agreement. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend and plaintiff appealed. The court reversed, holding that validity of the "cleaning fee" provision was properly determined under Cal. Civ. Code § 1951(a) and that plaintiff had alleged sufficient facts to warrant statutory relief. Further, the face of the agreement was not legally effective to establish a waiver. The agreement made no reference to § 1951, but merely included the word "nonrefundable." Thus, it could not be said from the face of the agreement that plaintiff was aware of her statutory rights and intended to waive the statute's benefits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, finding that it was error to sustain a demurrer to plaintiff tenant's class action challenging the validity of defendant landlord's nonrefundable "cleaning fee" lease provision where the state law determined the validity of the "fee" and plaintiff had pled facts warranting relief. Also, the lease itself did not establish an effective legal waiver of the statutory protections.

 

813 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


County of Orange v. Heim


Civ. No. 11832


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


30 Cal. App. 3d 694; 106 Cal. Rptr. 825; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1199



February 21, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied March 12, 1973, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. M-1105, Claude M. Owens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The trial court is directed to enter judgment denying the peremptory writ of mandate, discharging the alternative writ heretofore issued by it and awarding costs in accordance with the law bearing in mind that the recover of costs against the State in this action is prohibited by Public Resources Code, section 6308. In view of this prohibition against the recovery of costs against the State, it is hereby ordered in the interest of justice that Interveners shall recover their costs on appeal from respondent Irvine and that appellant Heim, as Auditor of Orange County, shall bear his own costs on appeal. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, county auditor and residents, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which ordered appellant auditor to issue a warrant for payment of respondent county's share of engineering costs incurred in conjunction with agreements for the exchange of tidelands, dredging, and landfill agreements for the purpose of creating a recreational harbor and declared those agreements valid.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, county and landowner, entered into agreements for the exchange of tidelands, dredging, and landfill for the development of the bay as a harbor. Respondent state land commission acted to free the tidelands from a constitutional trust. Appellant county auditor refused to release funds for payment of respondent county's agreed share of engineering costs incurred in a soils investigation study made pursuant to the agreements, claiming that the agreements were invalid. Respondents sought a writ of mandate to compel appellant to issue payment and declarations that the several agreements were valid and in compliance with the enabling legislation and the California Constitution. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondents. The court reversed, holding the land exchange agreement, and the action purporting to free from the constitutional tidelands trust the lands to be conveyed, violated the prohibition of Cal. Const. art. XV, § 3 against alienation of tidelands into private ownership. The court held that the tidelands to be conveyed under land exchange agreement were not a relatively small parcel to apply the exemption to the rule against alienation of tidelands.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgement, holding that the agreements were an unconstitutional attempt to alienate tidelands into private ownership and finding that the tidelands to be conveyed under agreements did not constitute a relatively small parcel to qualify for an exemption to the rule against the alienation of tidelands.

 

814 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Field Research Corp. v. Patrick


Civ. No. 29816


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 603; 106 Cal. Rptr. 473; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1192



February 16, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 16, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 12, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 564926, Melvyn I. Cronin, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant political candidate sought review of the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) entering judgment for plaintiffs, a research company and its owner, in the libel suit against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, research company and its owner, sued defendant political candidate for damages for libel after defendant allegedly made statements that a poll conducted by plaintiffs was corrupt, dishonest, and rigged and that another candidate bought the poll. The trial court's judgment was in favor of plaintiffs. Defendant appealed the trial court's decision and asserted that the trial court erred by instructing the jury that plaintiffs only had to prove actual malice by a preponderance of the evidence, and by refusing to instruct that clear and convincing evidence was the standard. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that juries in libel actions must be instructed that actual malice was to be proved by clear and convincing evidence. However, the trial court's preponderance of the evidence instructions constituted harmless error. The court found that there was no factual issue on actual malice for the jury's determination because the uncontroverted evidence showed that defendant made statements with actual malice, and that the jury should have been so instructed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for plaintiffs, a research company and its owner, in their libel suit against defendant political candidate. Even though the burden of proof for finding actual malice in libel actions was by clear and convincing evidence, the trial court's instruction to the jury that actual malice was to be proved by a preponderance of the evidence instructions was harmless.

 

815 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Statewide Homeowners, Inc. v. Williams


Civ. No. 11357


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 567; 106 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1187



February 15, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 320630, Franklin B. Orfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners requested review of a decision by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) denying their request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent assessor to produce his workpapers. The trial court concluded that the workpapers were not public records subject to inspection pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 408(a).


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellant homeowners' request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent assessor to release workpapers used in the tax assessment process. The trial court concluded that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 408(a) exempted the workpapers from disclosure because respondent was not required to prepare or keep the workpapers. Appellant asserted that the workpapers were essential to respondent's statutory duties and did not contain confidential information. The court affirmed the judgment denying the writ and held that respondent had no duty to disclose the workpapers. Respondent had no duty to maintain the records for appellant's benefit and they were exempt from disclosure under the Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 408(a), and the California Public Records Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 6250 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying appellant homeowners' request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent assessor to produce its workpapers for inspection. Respondent assessor had no legal duty to prepare or keep the workpapers and they were therefore exempt from disclosure.

 

816 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kelsey v. Colwell


Civ. No. 1745


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


30 Cal. App. 3d 590; 106 Cal. Rptr. 420; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1190



February 15, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 12, 1973, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 43369, Nathaniel O. Bradley, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying appellants' petition for a writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Merced County (California) denying appellant's petition for a writ of mandate, holding that the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 51200 et seq., was permissive, and that respondent board of supervisors was not required to implement the act in Merced County in appellant's action to compel respondent to do so.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners petitioned for a writ of mandamus to compel respondent board of supervisors to implement the Williamson Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 51200 et seq., in Merced County. The trial judge held that the act was permissive, not mandatory, and that respondent was not committed irrevocably to implement the act in Merced County. The court affirmed the judgment denying appellants' petition for a writ of mandate because the act was permissive legislation, and mandamus did not lie to compel respondent, a public body, to perform a discretionary function. The court stated that ordinary doctrines of statutory construction compelled the conclusion that the term "may" was used in Cal. Gov't Code §§ 51230, 51240 in its permissive sense within the meaning of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 5, 14. The court also stated that respondent directed certain county officers and departments to make studies and to prepare forms and procedures, but it did not approve the studies or adopt the forms and procedures; neither did it call a public hearing as required by Cal. Gov't Code § 51230. Respondent's action did not progress beyond the investigative stage.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying appellant landowners' petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent board of supervisors to implement an act in Merced County because the act was permissive, not mandatory, legislation, and respondent, a public body, could not have been compelled to perform a discretionary function.

 

817 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Cook v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 12140


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


33 Cal. App. 3d 265; 109 Cal. Rptr. 104; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 890



February 8, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 189054, Lester Van Tatenhove, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver appealed the dismissal of a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Orange County (California). Plaintiff requested the writ to compel defendant Department of Motor Vehicle to reinstate his driving privileges after defendant suspended plaintiff's license based on two out of state judgments pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant Department of Motor Vehicle suspended plaintiff driver's license to drive because of two out of state convictions for driving while intoxicated. Plaintiff contended that the judgments were constitutionally infirm and requested a writ of mandate to vacate the suspension order. The trial court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the writ of mandate. Defendant was required to suspend plaintiff's license pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13352 (c), upon receipt of the abstracts of judgment. The appellate court affirmed dismissal of the writ of mandate, and held that defendant was not empowered to determine the validity of the out of state judgments, and that it was plaintiff's responsibility to attack the judgments in the states that issued them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the writ of mandate. The suspension of appellant driver's operating license was proper, because it was appellant's responsibility to attack the out of state judgments, that required the suspension, in the states that issued the judgments. Defendant DMV was not empowered to determine the validity of the judgments.

 

818 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Roberts v. Brian


Civ. No. 40361


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 427; 106 Cal. Rptr. 360; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1171



February 5, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 988 782, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That portion of the judgment from which this appeal has been taken is affirmed; defendant is ordered to pay for and on behalf of attorneys' fees on this appeal the sum of $ 1,000.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant director sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded attorney's fees to respondent indigent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant director sought review of an order granting attorney's fees to respondent indigent. Appellant argued that inasmuch as the underlying action was remanded specifically for modification of the lower court's findings and conclusion of law and judgment, the lower court had no power to do anything more than give effect to the appellate court's directions and therefore was without jurisdiction to make an award of attorneys' fees for services rendered on appeal. The court affirmed the judgment holding that implicit in the opinion of the appellate court was the intention to give respondent complete relief which necessarily included not only the individualized nursing care he so desperately needed but reasonable attorney's fees and to enable the trial court, by its remand of the cause with directions, to take all necessary steps to modify the findings of fact and conclusions of law. The court ordered appellant to pay additional attorneys' fees on the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that portion of the judgment from which the appeal had been taken and further ordered appellant director to pay additional attorney's fees on current appeal, holding that implicit in the opinion to remand was the intention of the appellate court to give respondent indigent complete relief.

 

819 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bohannan v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 11322


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 416; 106 Cal. Rptr. 333; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1170



February 2, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 324075, William H. Macomber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which rejected his action to declare defendant city's zoning ordinance invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city adopted an ordinance regulating the architectural design of buildings and signs within public view in an area surrounding a state park. Plaintiff owner brought a class action on behalf of retail businesses in the zoned area, seeking to declare the ordinance invalid. The trial court rejected the claim. Plaintiff appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in finding that the regulation of the control district's boundaries was within defendant's police power. The general public welfare was promoted by preserving the image of the "old town" as it existed before 1871 and by an educational exhibit of the state's birthplace. The court held that the style requirements on remodeling and repairing were not a taking without compensation and did not render the existing buildings valueless. The court held that the classification between signs was reasonably related to the historical purpose. The court found that the ordinance did not violate free speech or invalidly delegate legislative authority to the board, but prescribed ascertainable criteria for approval and a right of review by the council.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of declaratory relief to plaintiff owner because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in finding that the zoning ordinance was a valid exercise of defendant city's police power and did not violate free speech or taking principles or invalidly delegate legislative power to the architectural compliance board.

 

820 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Amundsen v. Public Employees' Retirement System


Civ. No. 31381


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


30 Cal. App. 3d 856; 106 Cal. Rptr. 759; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1212



January 31, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 640406, Leland J. Lazarus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that changes in retirement laws were constitutional and did not impair appellant's pension rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee challenged a trial court decision that held that a change in retirement laws, requiring five years of service before appellant could retire, was constitutional. Appellant alleged that he was deprived of the right to retire as he intended, and that this was an unconstitutional impairment of his pension rights. In considering whether disadvantages to appellant were accompanied by comparable new advantages, the court noted that appellant could have retired immediately prior to the effective date of the amendments, whereupon he would have been entitled to a pension based upon his own contributions. The court held that by failing to do so, appellant was precluded from retiring on a pension until December 31, 1973, which was concededly a disadvantage. But the amendments also provided for a decreased percentage of employee contributions and for a substantially higher pension upon retirement after five years of service. The court found that the disadvantage was thus accompanied by comparable new advantages and affirmed, holding that the mere fact that appellant did not desire to share in the advantages of the new law did not affect the validity of the amendments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the amendments were constitutional. The court held that comparable new advantages accompanied any disadvantage of the new retirement law and that appellant employee's individual desire not to share in the advantages did not affect the validity of the amendments to the retirement law.

 

821 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Vienne


Crim. No. 10507


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


30 Cal. App. 3d 266; 105 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1156



January 30, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 1, 1973, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-1933, Louis B. Dematteis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are each affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for robbery rendered by the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), challenging the validity of a prior conviction used to convict him and the constitutionality of the habitual criminal statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for robbery in part by reliance on a prior conviction. At the robbery trial, defendant's counsel challenged the validity of the prior conviction but the trial judge admitted it. The court affirmed admission of the prior conviction and set out a five-part procedure for how the conviction could have been challenged at trial: 1) if defendant raised the issue, by motion to strike or by denial, the court should have held a hearing outside the presence of the jury; 2) the prosecutor then had the burden of producing evidence of the prior conviction; 3) after the prosecutor's prima facie showing, the burden then shifted to the defendant to show his right to counsel was infringed; 4) the prosecutor could then introduce rebuttal evidence; and 5) the court would then be required to make an evidentiary finding and strike an invalid conviction. Defendant failed to meet the burden of producing evidence that his right to counsel had been violated. The court summarily affirmed the constitutionality of the habitual criminal statute, Cal. Penal Code § 644. The court also affirmed the validity of the robbery conviction and briefly addressed other alleged trial errors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's robbery conviction. At trial, defendant failed to present any evidence that his right to counsel had been infringed in the prior proceedings; hence, the prior conviction was properly admitted. The court summarily affirmed the constitutionality of the habitual criminal statute.

 

822 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ataka America, Inc. v. Crateo, Inc.


Civ. Nos. 39802, 41013


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


30 Cal. App. 3d 315; 106 Cal. Rptr. 280; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1161



January 30, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 15, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 972703, Robert H. Patton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: A petition for a rehearing was denied February 15, 1973.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Third-party claimant corporation sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which precluded transfer of attached stock pending the determination of an appeal, unless a bond was filed.


OVERVIEW: Third-party claimant corporation appealed a judgment which prohibited transfer by third-party claimant of attached stock pending the determination of an appeal in another action. One effect of that judgment was to restore to plaintiff guarantee the right of attachment of the property involved. It was that right which plaintiff sought to enforce, but the court held that plaintiff no longer had the attachment right. To permit enforcement of the portion of the judgment that ordered the officer to levy the property by way of garnishment under the writ of attachment, if the writ were still in his possession, or under a new writ, was in violation of the current state of the law. The court held that it could not permit the writ to continue, holding that the proceeding was held on full notice to the only parties therein concerned -- namely plaintiff and third-party claimant; it was fully litigated; it resulted in an elaborate factual and legal determination and every requirement of due process was observed. The court concluded that third-party claimant failed to set forth evidence for grounds of reversal and thus, the trial court did not err in finding that the transfer was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that third-party claimant corporation failed to set forth evidence for grounds of reversal and thus, the trial court did not err in finding that the transfer was invalid.

 

823 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Purifoy v. State Board of Education


Civ. No. 30109


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 187; 106 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1149



January 26, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 626-247, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order and judgment are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher appealed from an order and judgment of defendant Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate ordering the California State Board of Education to afford him a fair hearing on his fitness to teach and, pending the outcome of such hearing, to rescind all action taken against his teaching credential and expunge all records of any such action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff teacher had been the certified holder of a life credential, required for teaching in the public schools of California. After he was convicted of violations of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a), (d), the California State Board of Education suspended plaintiff's teaching credential. The trial court denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate ordering the Board to afford him a fair hearing on his fitness to teach and, pending the outcome of such hearing, to rescind all action taken against his teaching credential and expunge all records of any such action. The court affirmed the trial court. The court reasoned that the discipline sought to be imposed on plaintiff did not depend on a specific finding of unfitness to teach, but, rather, on the fact of the conviction of a crime. That conviction could only be obtained after a judicial hearing about whether plaintiff was guilty of the offense charged. Plaintiff was accorded such a hearing and, accordingly, was afforded due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment of the trial court, which denied plaintiff teacher's petition for a writ of mandate ordering respondent California State Board of Education to afford him a fair hearing on his fitness to teach. The court did so because the judicial hearing in which plaintiff was convicted of a criminal offense afforded him due process on the matter of suspension of his teaching credential.

 

824 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Drolet


Crim. No. 10226


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


30 Cal. App. 3d 207; 105 Cal. Rptr. 824; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1151



January 26, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 79389, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction of appellant Downton of violation of Penal Code section 288a as charged in count one, and of appellants Downton and Drolet of violation of section 182, is reversed. The judgment of conviction of appellant Drolet of violation of Penal Code section 288a as charged in count one, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, actress and writer, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found appellant actress guilty of committing an act of oral copulation and conspiracy to commit such an act under, Cal. Penal Code §§ 288a, 182, and found appellant writer guilty of conspiracy and aiding and abetting an act of oral copulation under §§ 288a, 182.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, actress and writer, were found guilty of violations of Cal. Penal Code §§ 288a, 182. During a performance appellant actress engaged in oral copulation of a male actor. Appellant actress was convicted of oral copulation and conspiracy to commit such an act. Appellant writer was convicted of aiding and abetting oral copulation and conspiracy to commit such an act under the same statutes. The court affirmed appellant actress's conviction for oral copulation and found that the state had a legitimate interest in protecting the public from such acts because its only value was to incite the audience. The court also found that the act committed was not protected U.S. Const. amend. I speech any more than actually killing a villain on stage would have been. The court reversed the §288a conviction of appellant writer and found that aiding and abetting required an actual instigation of the act and appellant writer made it clear that he only wanted a simulation of oral copulation, which was not a crime. The court reversed the conspiracy conviction as to both appellants and found that there was no evidence of an unlawful agreement between the appellants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant writer's convictions for committing and conspiracy to commit oral copulation because there was no evidence to support the convictions. The conspiracy to commit an act of oral copulation was also reversed for appellant actress, but her conviction for oral copulation was affirmed.

 

825 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taylor


Crim. No. 21538


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


30 Cal. App. 3d 117; 106 Cal. Rptr. 216; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1143



January 23, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 265777, Michael Berg, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article IV, section 21; see People v. Tijerina, 1 Cal.3d 41 [81 Cal.Rptr. 264, 459 P.2d 680].



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25540, and of grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1). Defendant had sold worthless certificates of interest in an Oklahoma oil lease without first having secured a qualification from the California Commissioner of Corporations pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 25110.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sold certificates of interest and participation in an Oklahoma oil lease without first obtaining a qualification from the California Commissioner of Corporations pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 25110. An agent had told investors that oil had been found and they received invoices soliciting more money for a completion fund; the "well" was a dry hole. Defendant was tried for violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25540 and for grand theft in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487(1); he was found guilty. On appeal, the court affirmed the ruling because he obtained property by false pretenses; use of an innocent agent was no defense nor was failure to benefit personally. The court held no error in failure to strike the testimony of a senior special investigator, who had testified that there was no record that defendant was authorized to sell the interests, because the testimony was developed by defendant on cross-examination and further explored on redirect. The court found that Cal. Corp. Code § 25540 was not void in forcing defendant to prove that he had no knowledge of the rule because defendant was convicted of violating the provisions of the law itself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for selling certificates of interest in a "dry" oil field absent a qualification from the California Commissioner of Corporations and for grand theft by selling the interests under false pretenses because use of an innocent agent was no defense nor was failure to benefit, and the damaging testimony of a state official was properly admitted because it was developed on cross-examination.

 

826 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 31803


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


30 Cal. App. 3d 8; 106 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1133



January 17, 1973 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ issue, directing the trial court to dismiss the conspiracy charge (count I of the information) and to remand the misdemeanor charge to the municipal court for appropriate action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prostitute sought a writ of prohibition from the court restraining respondent Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), from all further proceedings against her in a criminal action in which petitioner was charged with felony conspiracy to commit prostitution under Cal. Penal Code § 182, and misdemeanor disorderly conduct, prostitution, under Cal. Penal Code § 647(b).


OVERVIEW: An undercover police officer asked a cab driver on two occasions to obtain a prostitute for him. The driver directed the officer to petitioner prostitute who allegedly disrobed and received money for an act of prostitution. Petitioner and the cab driver were subsequently charged with conspiracy to commit prostitution and disorderly conduct. Petitioner thereafter sought a writ of prohibition dismissing the felony conspiracy charge. The court granted the writ because there was a recognized distinction between the offense of prostitution, which was punishable as a misdemeanor, and the felony offenses of pimping and pandering under Cal. Penal Code §§ 266h and 266i. The distinction reflected the policy that the woman who was exploited by a male in violation of § 266h was not to be considered an accomplice of the man who exploited her. The court remanded the misdemeanor charge to the lower court for further action.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a peremptory writ issue, directing the trial court to dismiss the conspiracy charge against petitioner prostitute, and remanded the misdemeanor charge to the lower court because there was a recognized distinction between a misdemeanor prostitution charge and felony pimping and pandering charges based on a legislative determination about the seriousness of those offenses.

 

827 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Earl


Crim. No. 10991


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


29 Cal. App. 3d 894; 105 Cal. Rptr. 831; 1973 Cal. App. LEXIS 1243



January 10, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 8, 1973. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 6691, Raymond J. Sherwin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment instead of death for the murder in accordance with People v. Anderson, supra, 6 Cal.3d 628, and as so modified is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder, and burglary in the first degree and attempted kidnapping, on the grounds that there was insufficient evidence, or diminished capacity, that the felony-murder rule was not applicable to a burglary, that he should not have been restrained in chains during trial, and he was denied a hearing to discharge his attorney.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder, pursuant to the felony-murder rule under Cal. Penal Code § 189, for killing a private security guard who had stopped him for suspicion of shoplifting. Appellant was sentenced to death, which the court modified to life because of an intervening case holding the death penalty unconstitutional. As modified, the court affirmed because, based on the evidence, the jury could infer that appellant had the intent to steal when he entered the store, and that he had the undiminished capacity to so. The felony-murder rule was correctly applied in connection with the burglary because the dangerousness of the § 189 felony had to be assessed in the abstract, and not on a case by case basis. Additionally, there was sufficient evidence of a second burglary to steal a gun, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in using leg-irons to restrain appellant because of the nature of the crime and because of his uncivil conduct, and he was given an adequate hearing to explain his request to discharge his attorney.


OUTCOME: The court modified judgment of the trial court to provide for life in prison instead of death for the felony murder of a security guard, and as modified, affirmed the judgment. The court held that there was sufficient evidence from which the jury could infer the fact of and requisite capacity and intent to burglarize, which in the abstract supported a conviction of felony murder, and that any procedural errors were not an abuse of discretion.

 

828 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Chrysler Credit Corp. v. Waegele


Civ. No. 38662


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


29 Cal. App. 3d 681; 105 Cal. Rptr. 914; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 721



December 27, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 22, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 996942, Parks Stillwell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, two corporations and their vice president, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied defendants' motion to quash service of a temporary restraining order preventing the disposal by sale or otherwise of automobiles financed by plaintiff creditor.


OVERVIEW: The trial court issued a temporary restraining order to prevent defendants, two corporations and their vice president, who were nonresidents, from disposing of collateral for plaintiff creditor's loan to one defendant corporation. The court affirmed the denial of defendants' motion to quash service of the order because it involved a situation requiring special protection of plaintiff's interest. The court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527, authorizing the use of a temporary restraining order, was narrowly drawn, satisfied the requirements of due process of law, and was not offensive to either constitutional or equitable principles on the facts in this case. The court found that defendants had adequate notice of the command to appear and show cause why a preliminary injunction should not have been issued. The court found that the haste with which defendants' transaction was consummated led to the conclusion that defendants and defendants' attorney were willing to accept the consequences of a contempt proceeding in exchange for putting the collateral beyond the easy reach of plaintiff and dispelled any reasonable inference that defendants' acts were not willful and deliberate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the motion by defendants, two corporations and their vice president, to quash service of a temporary restraining order because the California Civil Procedure Code allowed a temporary restraining order as special protection of plaintiff creditor's interest in case of a nonresident defendant. Restraint of property held by nonresidents was not likely to impose an economic hardship on them.

 

829 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Eugene W.


Civ. No. 31136


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


29 Cal. App. 3d 623; 105 Cal. Rptr. 736; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 717



December 26, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Napa County, No. 27891, William L. Blanckenburg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Napa County (California), which freed her four minor children from parental custody and control pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g). Proceedings were initiated by respondent child welfare worker. A motion to compel appellant to submit to a mental examination was granted.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother's main contention in challenging the freeing of her children was that Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g) was unconstitutional and violative of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court pointed out that the language of the statute demonstrated on its face that the statutory classification was based solely on mental deficiency or mental illness, not on poverty. Mental deficiency or mental illness, by its very definition, inherently affected the sick parent's ability to control. The court found that the purpose of the statute was to secure the welfare and best interest of the child by freeing him from parental care when adoptive homes would provide stability and security otherwise missing from his life. The record revealed that although respondent child welfare worker filed the action in her individual capacity, she expressly stated in her petition that she worked for the Napa County Welfare Department. That allegation on its face indicated that she had a direct interest in the adjudication of the case. Consequently, the court agreed with the finding of the trial court that she was a proper person to bring the action. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant mother's mental illness made it impossible for her to care for her children. Freeing them would serve their best interests and would provide stability and security in a more reliable setting.

 

830 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Anderson


Crim. No. 10014


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


29 Cal. App. 3d 551; 105 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 711



December 21, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 14, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 43649, Charles S. Franich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting probation is affirmed, and the purported appeal from the final judgment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict in the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which convicted him of six counts of improperly issuing prescriptions to narcotic addicts in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11162.5 and sentenced him to probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant physician issued prescriptions to ambulatory narcotic addicts in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11162.5. On appeal, defendant argued that the last sentence of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11391 was unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the judgment and dismissed the appeal, holding that defendant's contention on certain phrases being vague, uncertain, and indefinite to give notice was without merit. The court held that the statute was read in context with the intent element of the offense it described. The court held that defendant was not erroneously charged under § 11162.5 instead of the more specific § 11391 that carried only a misdemeanor penalty because § 11162.5 could be either a misdemeanor or a felony. The court also found that the trial court did not err in modifying the proposed instructions on "customary use" and the clarification in no way harmed defendant. As to defendant's argument that the evidence was insufficient to sustain the judgment, the court found that the jury could infer that defendant had the requisite specific intent to keep the patients comfortable by maintaining customary use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and probation of defendant for issuing prescriptions to narcotic addicts because it found the statute was not worded in a vague way and was not uncertain or indefinite in terms of giving notice. The court found the trial court behaved properly in its instructions and clarifications and in judging that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the final verdict.

 

831 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court of Monterey County


Civ. No. 31915


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


29 Cal. App. 3d 397; 105 Cal. Rptr. 695; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 699



December 14, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 12, 1973, and the petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 8, 1973.



DISPOSITION: The petitioner's application for peremptory writ of mandate is granted and the respondent court is directed to set aside its order suppressing the evidence which we find to have been obtained in a lawful search incident to a lawful arrest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Monterey County (California) to set aside its order granting the motion of defendant to suppress evidence of the booking search on the grounds that taking him to jail instead of taking him to a 72-hour detention facility, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 647(ff), violated equal protection of the law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and booked at the police department for being in a public place under the influence of intoxicating liquor, and, as a result of the booking search, he was also charged with unlawful possession of dangerous restricted drugs. Respondent superior court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence of the booking search on the grounds that taking him to jail instead of taking him to a 72-hour detention facility, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 647(ff), violated equal protection of the law, and the people sought a writ on mandate. The court granted the peremptory writ and directed respondent to set aside its order suppressing the evidence of the booking search, holding that the evidence was obtained in a lawful search incident to a lawful arrest. The court found that defendant did not have a fundamental to be treated for his drinking problem in identically the same manner in each county. Therefore, any rational basis would uphold § 647(ff), and the state interest in finding a more effective way to deal with inebriates qualified as a rational basis for allowing counties to vary their approach.


OUTCOME: The court granted the people's application for a peremptory writ of mandate and directed respondent superior court to set aside its order suppressing the evidence of the booking search, because the evidence was obtained in a lawful search incident to a lawful arrest of defendant.

 

832 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Whitmire v. City of Eureka


Civ. No. 30052


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


29 Cal. App. 3d 28; 105 Cal. Rptr. 185; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 672



November 30, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Humboldt County, No. 48947, William G. Watson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal from the judgment (order) denying motion for summary judgment is dismissed. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two city police and fire department members, sought relief from a decision of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California), which granted declaratory relief upon respondent city's cross-claim in favor of respondent and dismissed appellants' claim challenging the validity of ordinances passed by respondent affecting appellants' retirement system.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city sought declaratory relief and judicial determination that ordinances it passed were valid, constitutional legislative enactments and that it had unrestricted power and authority to amend provisions of the fire and police department retirement system codified in Eureka, Cal., Ordinance 2262 (1943) in an action brought by appellants, members of the respondent city's fire and police departments who challenged the validity of the ordinances. The court affirmed the decision of the superior court to grant declaratory relief for respondent because it held that § 16 of Ordinance 2262 which gave fire and police department members the right to enact laws regarding the retirement system was an invalid and unconstitutional delegation of legislative power because it gave private individuals an ultimate right to veto or approve city council actions, but had no safeguards against abuse and was not an exclusive means of enactment of laws regarding the retirement system. Moreover, § 20 of Ordinance 2262 provided that the provisions of the ordinance were to be interpreted in conformity with the city's charter and in the event of conflict, the city prevailed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of declaratory relief to respondent city by the superior court and held that ordinances passed by respondent were valid because respondent had unrestricted power and authority to amend provisions of the fire and police department retirement system and to allow members of the departments exclusive means to enact laws regarding the retirement system was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power.

 

833 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Penney v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 1835


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


28 Cal. App. 3d 941; 105 Cal. Rptr. 162; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 811



November 22, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The matter is remanded to the superior court with instructions to reopen the evidentiary hearing on petitioners' motion to dismiss, to receive such additional evidence as the People can produce relevant to a legitimate justification for the delay, and in rebuttal of the petitioners' showing of prejudice, and to exercise the required judicial discretion by balancing the public interest in prosecuting petitioners against each petitioner's right to a fair trial. The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied without prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, accused murderers, appealed the ruling of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), denying petitioners' motion to dismiss the indictment because of pre-indictment delay.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, accused murderers, sought to dismiss the indictment against them because of unconstitutional pre-indictment delay. The murder had been committed in 1966 and an investigation was performed shortly afterward. At that time, the district attorney decided not to indict. In 1972, the district attorney brought charges against petitioners with substantially the same evidence. The court reversed the denial of the motion to dismiss the indictment because of pre-indictment delay, requiring that the trial court conduct an additional evidentiary hearing on the matter. The court held that the proper test was to balance the justification for delay versus the prejudice to petitioners, and the trial court was required to consider the facts and circumstance surrounding the decision not to prosecute, the length of the delay, and the reasons for the subsequent re-evaluations and prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of the motion to dismiss the indictment against petitioners, accused murderers, and remanded to the trial court for further evidentiary hearings to determine whether there was justification for delay in prosecution of the matter and whether prejudice to petitioners outweighed the people's right to prosecute.

 

834 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Sobiek v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 31149


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


28 Cal. App. 3d 846; 106 Cal. Rptr. 516; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 802



November 21, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged, through writ of prohibition, the decision of respondent, the Superior Court of San Mateo County, which denied his motion to quash, on statute of limitations grounds, his indictment for forgery, Cal. Penal Code § 470.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with four counts of grand theft, Cal. Penal Code § 487, and one count of forgery, Cal. Penal Code § 470, in connection with his appropriation of funds from an investment club. Petitioner brought a motion to quash the entire indictment. The trial court quashed grand theft counts of the indictment on the ground of lack of probable cause, but it retained the forgery count.
