Petitioner challenged, through writ of prohibition, the forgery count on limitations grounds. On review the court granted the writ, holding that an amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 800, that provided that the limitation did not start to run until the discovery of the offense, was not adopted until some months after the existing time in which to file the forgery charge had expired, so that application of the amendment to petitioner's situation would constitute application of ex post facto legislation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ to petitioner because an amendment to the statute of limitations that provided that it did not start to run until the discovery of the offense, was not adopted until some months after the existing time in which to file the forgery charge had expired, so that it would be an application of ex post facto legislation to apply the amendment to petitioner's situation.

 

835 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Strutt v. Ontario Sav. & Loan Assn.


Civ. No. 11967


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


28 Cal. App. 3d 866; 105 Cal. Rptr. 395; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 803



November 21, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 7, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 159329, Robert L. Corfman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: No reversible error appearing, the judgment is affirmed.

In the interests of justice, the parties shall bear their own costs on appeal. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff debtor appealed the verdict of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) in favor of defendant creditor in plaintiff's action for fraud, negligence, and unjust enrichment surrounding a foreclosure.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff debtor brought an action against defendant creditor surrounding the repossession and foreclosure of plaintiff's real estate. Defendant was the holder of a first deed of trust on the property. Defendant mailed notice of default under Cal. Civ. Code § 2924 to plaintiff, who was out of town without notice to defendant. Plaintiff was then hospitalized according to Cal. Penal Code § 1368 because he was unable to understand bad-check charges against him and to effectively assist his attorney. Plaintiff alleged that defendant converted his personal property when it paid $ 100 to the mental health department for the property. The court found that conversion did occur because the department of mental health was unauthorized to sell personalty in such manner; however, it was non-prejudicial error. The court affirmed the lower court's ruling, holding that the lack of a preliminary hearing before a foreclosure under Cal. Civ. Code § 2924 was constitutional because it was action by a private party, not the state. The court held that plaintiff was not adjudicated incompetent by reason of his commitment under Cal. Penal Code § 1368.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's verdict, holding that while it was error for defendant creditor to buy plaintiff debtor's personalty from the state department of mental health, it was not prejudicial. In addition, there was no constitutional violation when a private creditor sold real estate without prior notice to the owner when the owner could not be found.

 

836 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Nancy C.


Civ. No. 30485


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


28 Cal. App. 3d 747; 105 Cal. Rptr. 113; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 790



November 15, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Solano County, No. 8638, Richard J. Swan, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile offender challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which found that she violated Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 by leaving home without consent, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, by possessing marijuana, and the local curfew ordinance, which appellant alleged was unconstitutional. The juvenile court adjudicated appellant a ward of the court, committing her to the California Youth Authority.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a supplemental petition filed in the juvenile court, appellant juvenile offender was found to have violated Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, for leaving her foster parents' home without their consent, and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, for possession of marijuana. When she was found wandering the streets, the officers placed appellant under arrest for violation of the city's curfew ordinance. The juvenile court ordered her to continue as a ward of the court, and committed her to the youth authority. On appeal, appellant argued that the officers lacked probable cause to arrest her under the ordinance, which was unconstitutionally broad. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that once the officers ascertained that appellant was a juvenile, they had probable cause to arrest her and perform a search incident to the arrest. Further, the court found the ordinance constitutional, holding that to forbid juveniles from loitering in the streets during nighttime hours had a real and substantial relationship to the dual goal of protection of children and the community, and the ordinance did not unduly restrict the rights of minors in view of these interests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the juvenile court, finding that under the totality of the circumstances there was probable cause for arresting and searching appellant juvenile offender for violating the curfew ordinance, and that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally broad because juveniles could be treated differently from adults, for their protection.

 

837 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 12087, Crim. No. 5616


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


28 Cal. App. 3d 600; 104 Cal. Rptr. 876; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 779



November 9, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 27, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The People's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 3, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-26732, Robert L. Corfman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The People's petition for writ of prohibition and writ of review is denied and the order to show cause heretofore issued is discharged. The stay order is dissolved provided, however, that the trial court is directed to defer imposition of sentence as to Chief Scott until 60 days after this opinion becomes final. In the event the chief purges himself of contempt by complying with the order to return within that period, no punishment shall be imposed. It is further ordered that the People's appeal in People v. Frederick Arnold Loar and Kay Nancy Loar, 4 Crim. 5616, be dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people filed a writ of prohibition and review against the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which ordered the return to defendant film producers of all films seized by the people to prepare the ultimately unsuccessful case against defendants for conspiring to distribute obscene matter in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2.


OVERVIEW: Vast quantities of films were seized from defendant film producers by the people in connection with the trial of defendants for conspiring to distribute obscene matter in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2. After defendants were acquitted, the trial court sustained their motion for the return of the evidence. The people, however, sought a writ of prohibition and a writ of review for the police chief's subsequent contempt charge for failing to return the films. The court held the trial court had jurisdiction to order the return of the films and materials under Cal. Penal Code § 1536 because the evidence was taken in connection with an offense that was triable in that court and it was irrelevant that the trial was over. Further, the police had no right to the property beyond that derived from the search warrant, and official retention of seized materials not found to be obscene denied defendants' due process rights. Finally, because the evidence fell under U.S. Const. amend. I, it was entitled to additional protections that mandated its return to defendants. The court affirmed the contempt charge against the police chief despite contentions that he acted in good faith.


OUTCOME: The court denied the people's petition for writ for prohibition of the trial court's order to return allegedly obscene materials to defendant film producers and found that the police chief was in contempt for failing to return the items. The court held that defendants were entitled to a return of the confiscated films as a matter of course because, unlike contraband or dangerous weapons, the films were entitled to First Amendment protection.

People v. Witzerman


Civ. No. 38700


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


29 Cal. App. 3d 169; 105 Cal. Rptr. 284; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 684



November 8, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 1, 1972, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 3, 1973.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, cattle care contract salesmen, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that enjoined them from offering and selling cattle care contracts and imposed a $ 50,000 civil penalty.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, cattle care contract salesmen, began promoting and selling cattle care contracts, although most did not have knowledge of the cattle industry. After serving several cease and desist orders on appellants for false and misleading advertising, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 17535, 17536, the state brought an action against them for a permanent injunction. The trial court issued the injunction along with a $ 50,000 civil penalty with joint and several liability. Appellants challenged the fine. The court affirmed the ruling, holding that appellants did not have a right to a jury trial because the injunction issue was an equitable one and it could not be severed from the penalty issue. The court found that the cattle care contracts were securities under Cal. Corp. Code § 25008 and that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§17500, 17536 were not unconstitutionally vague. The court also found that the penalties imposed were reasonable under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§17500, 17536.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court, holding that appellants, cattle care contract salesmen, did not have a constitutional right to a jury trial, the contracts were securities under California law, and that the findings of the trial court were supported by substantial evidence.

Tip Top Foods, Inc. v. Lyng


Civ. No. 30639


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


28 Cal. App. 3d 533; 104 Cal. Rptr. 718; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 777



November 6, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 6, 1972. On November 24, 1972, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of the plaintiff and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 3, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 386846, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to modify the findings of fact, conclusions of law, and judgment in conformity with the views expressed herein. Each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Review was sought of an order by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which declared unconstitutional certain portions of Cal. Agric. Code § 38901 et seq., in a suit by plaintiff, a manufacturer of food products, for injunctive and declaratory relief against defendants, agriculture department officials.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a manufacturer of food products, filed suit against defendants, agriculture department officials, seeking to restrain defendants from enforcing a cease-and-desist order, and to have certain parts of the California Agricultural Code declared unconstitutional. Both parties sought review of the trial court's decision. The court affirmed in part, and reversed in part. Cal. Agric. Code § 38904, which provided that no products resembling milk products could be used in certain institutions receiving state assistance, was held invalid, because prohibiting the use of such products bore no reasonable relation to the statutory purposes of preventing deceptive marketing or protecting public health. Section 38905, which forbade offering any product resembling milk products to the public without a warning sign, was also deemed unconstitutional, because the size of the sign required was unreasonably large, and the warning statement, too broadly-worded to be informative. Section 35191, a record-keeping provision, §§ 38952 and 38956, labelling restrictions, §§ 38903(b) and (c), exemptions to the statute, and § 38941, a registration requirement, were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part, declaring unconstitutional a law forbidding the use of products resembling milk products in institutions receiving state assistance, and a law requiring a warning sign where such products were offered the public, because they bore no reasonable relation to the statutory goals of preventing public deception and protecting public health.

 

840 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rappard


Crim. No. 21247


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


28 Cal. App. 3d 302; 104 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 758



October 26, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 20, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-055411, William A. Caldecott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, Penal Code section 12021, as it applies to aliens, is declared unconstitutional. The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted and sentenced him for being an alien in possession of a concealable firearm in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a judgment pronounced upon the revocation of the probation previously granted him following his conviction (without judgment thereupon) of being an alien in possession of a concealable firearm and sentencing him for the offense, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12021. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment because § 12021, as it applied to aliens, was unconstitutional. The court found that the state did not sustain its burden of establishing that the classification, based upon the suspect factor of alienage, not only promoted a compelling interest which justified the law but that the distinctions drawn by the law were necessary to further its purpose, in this case preventing a threat to public safety. Therefore under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 12021 as it applied to defendant was unconstitutional because it denied equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that convicted defendant of being an alien in possession of a concealable firearm because statute was unconstitutional as it applied to aliens. The court found that the alien classification was suspect under the equal protection of the Fourteenth Amendment, and it had no reasonable relationship to the threat to public safety which the statute was designed to prevent.

In re Marriage of Walton


Civ. No. 11952


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


28 Cal. App. 3d 108; 104 Cal. Rptr. 472; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 741



October 13, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 7, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. D-41190, Claude M. Owens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged the interlocutory judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted respondent husband's petition for dissolution of marriage and denied appellant's request for legal separation.


OVERVIEW: Respondent husband filed a petition for dissolution of his marriage to appellant wife. The trial court rendered an interlocutory judgment, granting respondent's motion and denying appellant's request for legal separation. Appellant challenged the judgment of the trial court, and the court affirmed on appeal. The court held that the impact of the Family Law Act on appellant was not unfair or unjust, as marital rights and obligations were not contractual rights and obligations within the meaning of the California or United States Constitutions. The court also dismissed appellant's allegation that granting respondent's motion for dissolution of marriage was a deprivation of appellant's property without due process of law. The court held that appellant's interest in remaining a married woman was not properly within the meaning of the due process clauses of U.S. Const. amend XIV and Cal. Const. art 1, § 13. Whatever interest appellant had was subject to the reserve power of the state to amend the law or to enact additional laws for the public good and in pursuance of public policy.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court granting respondent husband's petition for dissolution of marriage was affirmed because the impact of the Family Law Act on appellant wife was not unfair or unjust as marital rights and obligations were not contractual rights and obligations within the meaning of the California or United States Constitutions.

Yarish v. Nelson


Civ. No. 30188


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 893; 104 Cal. Rptr. 205; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 901



September 22, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 16, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 59145, Samuel W. Gardiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of March 10, 1971, is affirmed. The appeal from the order of March 22, 1971, having been withdrawn and no filing fee having been paid thereon, is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant journalist and publisher sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which denied appellants' amended petition for a writ of mandate asserting that respondent prison warden's restrictions on interviews of inmates were unconstitutionally vague, and that respondent improperly limited appellants' use of a tape recorded interview and improperly refused them access to an inmate's prison files.


OVERVIEW: Appellant journalist and publisher filed an amended petition for a writ of mandate asserting that respondent prison warden's restrictions on interviews of inmates were unconstitutionally vague, and that respondent improperly limited appellants' use of a tape recorded interview and improperly refused them access to an inmate's prison files. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the writ of mandate, holding that respondent's conditions regarding interviews of inmates were constitutional because prisoners did not possess full rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. and because the state could reasonably regulate freedom of expression if the regulations furthered an important governmental interest. The court held that respondent's restriction requiring the consent of prisoners' counsel to use tape recorded interviews was constitutional because it was reasonably designed to protect a legitimate end within the state's police power, namely, the right of inmates to a fair trial. The court also found that respondent lawfully denied appellants access to a prisoner's prison files because Cal. Govt. Code § 6253 and public policy concerns protected such records from disclosure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying appellant journalist and publisher's amended petition for a writ of mandate, holding that respondent prison warden's restrictions on interviews of inmates, use of tape recorded interviews of inmates, and access to inmates' prison records were proper.

People v. Ozene


Crim. No. 9962


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 905; 104 Cal. Rptr. 170; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 902



September 22, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 15, 1972. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 78222, Joseph Karesh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the finding of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found him guilty of rape by force and violence, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of rape by force and violence under Cal. Penal Code §261(3). On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellant's contention that the judgment was not supported by sufficient evidence was without merit. The court noted that the prosecutrix's testimony was corroborated by police testimony, and was not so improbable that it warranted the rejection of the reviewing court when the jury believed it. Appellant alleged that the Allen Charge, which was given to the jury as a jury instruction, was coercive as a matter of law because it urged the jury to reach a verdict, encouraging the minority of jurors to surrender their convictions and follow the majority. The court held that there was no evidence of coercion, and that the jury instruction was proper. The court found that the trial court's use of an allegedly biased diagnostic report prepared pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.03 did not deprive appellant of due process. The court also found that the denial of probation was proper and that there was no evidence that the trial court was unduly influenced by the report of the probation officer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of rape by force and violence because the judgment was supported by substantial evidence, there was no evidence of coercion in the jury instructions or of undue influence by the parole officer on the trial court.

In re Marriage of Stuart

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One

DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss appellant's appeal insofar as it purports to be from that portion of the interlocutory judgment of dissolution of marriage which orders that an interlocutory judgment be entered declaring that the parties are entitled to have their marriage dissolved, and that a final judgment of dissolution be entered upon proper application of either party or on the court's own motion after the expiration of six months from the date the court acquired jurisdiction of the respondent (appellant herein) is granted, and a remittitur shall issue directing the trial court to proceed on that issue as provided by law and in accordance with the views set forth in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent wife filed a motion to dismiss appellant husband's appeal of the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which ordered that an interlocutory judgment be entered declaring that the parties were entitled to have their marriage dissolved, and sought a remittitur directing the trial court to enter final judgment of dissolution of marriage.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband filed a notice of appeal of the trial court's entire interlocutory judgment of dissolution of marriage, primarily seeking review of the child custody decision. OUTCOME: The court granted respondent wife's motion to dismiss appellant husband's notice of appeal on the issue of dissolution of marriage, ordered that the parties were entitled to have their marriage dissolved, and issued a remittitur directing the trial court to enter a final judgment of dissolution of marriage.

People v. Cleveland


Crim. No. 20130


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


27 Cal. App. 3d 820; 104 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 894



September 19, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On October 6, 1972, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 15, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 238-632, Alfred J. McCourtney, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment and sentence are vacated; the case is remanded to the trial court with directions to rearraign defendant for sentence and thereafter to proceed in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a jury verdict finding him guilty of kidnapping for robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 209 where the victim was subjected to bodily harm and robbery. The trial court held a sanity hearing and found that defendant was insane during commission of the robbery. Defendant's sentence for kidnapping for robbery was life without parole.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of numerous criminal offenses, including kidnapping for purposes of robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 209. The jury sentenced defendant to life without parole for his kidnapping for purposes of robbery conviction. Defendant appealed the judgment and the court vacated the judgment and sentence, remanding the case to the trial court with directions to re-arraign defendant for sentence and to proceed in accordance with its opinion. The court found that defendant was properly convicted under § 209, holding that the jury instruction allowed the jury to find defendant guilty of merely kidnapping and that a simple abduction could have multiple purposes, one of which was the commission of robbery. The court also held that giving the Monk jury instruction was proper for several reasons, including because it was read with the Daniels jury instruction. The court held that the aggravated punishment under Cal. Penal Code § 209 for infliction of bodily harm on the victim was proper because defendant was sane at the time he committed the kidnapping. Under Cal. Penal Code § 1026, the court remanded the case for disposition of the conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 209.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's judgment and sentence finding defendant guilty of numerous offenses, including kidnapping for robbery and sentencing him to life without parole. The court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to re-arraign defendant for sentence and to proceed in accordance with its opinion.

Coffee-Rich, Inc. v. Fielder


Civ. No. 38748


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 792; 104 Cal. Rptr. 252; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 893; 1972 Trade Cas. (CCH) P74,186



September 19, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 16, 1972, and the petition of the plaintiffs and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 15, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 943548, Samuel L. Kurland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and remanded with directions to modify the findings of fact, conclusions of law and the judgment (permanent injunction) in terms consistent with this opinion. Each side to bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations and defendants, officials of the State Department of Agriculture, appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which permanently enjoined defendants from enforcing, in part, the provisions of Cal. Agric. Code §§ 38901 - 38987 in a suit to declare that certain portions of the statute did not apply to plaintiffs' products or that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporations filed a suit against defendants, officials of the State Department of Agriculture, seeking a declaration that provisions relating to "products resembling milk products," Cal. Agric. Code §§ 38901-38987 did not apply to their products or were unconstitutional. Plaintiffs and defendants appealed the trial courts decision, which permanently enjoined defendants from enforcing certain portions of the statute. The court reversed the trial court's decision and found that the injunction was improper because the trial court's factual findings conflicted and plaintiffs' products were governed by the statute because, when unpackaged, they could be confused with milk products. Although the court found Cal. Agric. Code § 38904, which prohibited the use of products resembling milk products in charitable or penal institutions, and Cal. Agric. Code § 38905, which required restaurants to post signs or notices that non-milk products were served, were unreasonable, the court found that the rest of the legislation was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision, which permanently enjoined defendants, officials of the State Department of Agriculture, and held that the injunction was improper because plaintiff corporations' products could be confused with milk products and therefore fell within contested statutes. Furthermore the court found, with the exception of two sections, that the challenged legislation was constitutional.

 

847 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Adams v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 40086


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


27 Cal. App. 3d 719; 104 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 887



September 13, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 10, 1972, and the opinion was modified on October 10 and 11, 1972, to read as printed above. Cobey, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 9, 1972. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 276392, Kathleen Parker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of mandate is discharged. A peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandamus to compel the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate its order denying his motion to quash the petit jury panel and to make an order granting that motion. Petitioner argued that because of the resort to a county-wide draw for the Central District, as contrasted with a district-wide draw for the other districts, he was subjected to a denial of due process and equal protection of the law.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged in a 17-count information with the commission of various felonies. Petitioner filed a motion to quash the petit jury panel, challenging the manner in which jurors were selected for the central district of the court pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 206, as a denial of due process and equal protection of the law. After the trial court denied the motion, petitioner sought a writ of mandamus to compel the trial court to vacate its order denying his motion to quash the petit jury panel and to make an order granting the motion. The court denied the writ. The court reasoned that 8 percent of the jurors selected by means of a county-wide draw were African-Americans, while the proportion of African-American residents in the county was 11 percent. Also, there was no evidence of improper standards. Thus, the representation of African-Americans was of the substantial nature required under the cross-sectional concept and satisfied the constitutional mandate. The court also ruled that the classification withstood petitioner's equal protection challenge since it was necessary to promote the compelling state interest in efficient and speedy administration of justice.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of mandate to compel the trial court to vacate its order denying his motion to quash the petit jury panel because petitioner did not establish that the manner in which jurors were selected for the central district operated as a denial of due process and equal protection of the law. The court ruled that the law was related to the efficient administration of justice.

 

848 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Alvarado


Crim. No. 10575


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


27 Cal. App. 3d 610; 103 Cal. Rptr. 845; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 878



September 5, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 1, 1972. Mosk, J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION: We issue the writ of habeas corpus, directing modification of the judgment only to the extent of ordering the superior court to strike therefrom reference to conviction under section 209 of the Penal Code and to substitute therefor conviction of section 207 of the Penal Code. Since the conviction of rape has produced a sentence of life imprisonment, and other sentences merge therein, it will be unnecessary to resentence petitioner for conviction under section 207 of the Penal Code.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for writ of habeas corpus, in an action involving petitioner's conviction for kidnaping for the purpose of robbery, under Cal. Penal Code § 209.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of rape by force, under Cal. Penal Code § 261(3), with a sentence of life imprisonment and was convicted of kidnaping for the purpose of robbery, under Cal. Penal Code § 209, with a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. For relief from the latter sentence, he filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, which the trial court denied. On appeal, the court issued the writ, directing modification of the judgment only to the extent of striking reference to conviction under § 209 and substituting conviction for simple kidnaping, under Cal. Penal Code § 207. The court held that the jury was incorrectly instructed that even though the intent to rob was an afterthought to the objective of rape, petitioner could be convicted of kidnaping for the purpose of robbery. Only after petitioner's conviction was a definitive ruling established: the specific intention to commit that crime was required to be present at the time of the original asportation of the victim. The court held that the ruling was retroactive and applied to petitioner because the evidence of intent to rob as contemporaneous with original asportation was exceedingly tenuous.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of habeas corpus, because a conviction for kidnaping for the purpose of robbery required that the carrying away of the person was for the purpose of committing robbery. This rule was retroactively applied and the court held that petitioner's conduct of asportation for the purpose of rape was not prohibited by the aforementioned crime, thus, jurisdiction was exceeded. Conviction was for simple kidnaping instead.

 

849 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Medina


Crim. No. 9710


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


27 Cal. App. 3d 473; 103 Cal. Rptr. 721; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 863



August 28, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 25, 1972. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 50083, Albert F. DeMarco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) entered after defendant was found guilty by a jury of violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11503, which made it unlawful to offer to sell a narcotic and to deliver some substance in lieu of a narcotic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sold five capsules, which he represented to be mescaline, to an undercover narcotics agent. Upon analysis none of these capsules contained a narcotic. The criminologists who made the analysis testified without objection to the composition of mescaline, and stated that the law classifies mescaline as a narcotic. Defendant was found guilty by a jury of violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11503 that made it unlawful to offer to sell a narcotic and to deliver some substance in lieu of a narcotic. Defendant appealed, contending that § 11503 required a specific intent to substitute a non-narcotic for the promised narcotic and that it was error for the trial court to have refused to so instruct the jury. The court affirmed, holding that § 11503 was not a specific intent crime as the aim of § 11503 was to prohibit anyone from appearing to engage in narcotics traffic, and the offense was complete at the time of delivery regardless of the intent with which it was done. The court also found that the trial court properly instructed the jury on mescaline because it was a narcotic as a matter of law and not within the jury's province to determine.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because violation for which defendant was found guilty was not a specific intent crime, and the offense was complete at the time of delivery of the fake narcotics regardless of the intent with which it was done. The jury was also properly instructed on mescaline because it was a narcotic as a matter of law.

 

850 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Cosgrove


Civ. No. 39361


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 424; 103 Cal. Rptr. 733; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 858



August 24, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 18, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D-769605, Jack T. Ryburn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The portion of the judgment relating to child support is reversed. On remand the trial court will redetermine the amount to be awarded appellant for the support of the minor children and also determine whatever controversy may exist with respect to the income tax liabilities of the parties. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. The costs on this appeal will be borne by the parties equally.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In her divorce from respondent husband, appellant wife challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), arguing that the Family Law Act was unconstitutional, disputing the rulings on community property and spousal and child support, and asking that the trial court determine the parties' income tax liabilities.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the child support award in a divorce action between appellant wife and respondent husband, finding that the trial court erred in taking into account the children's trust funds. Concluding that the resulting award was too low, it ordered the trial court to recalculate respondent's child support obligation on remand. It affirmed the order denying spousal support, noting that appellant's securities income was adequate to pay her and the children's living expenses, as well as the finding that respondent was not obligated to reimburse appellant for gifts of securities made by her parents because there had not been an agreement by the parties to that effect. It rejected appellant's argument that the Family Law Act was unconstitutional, noting that she had not articulated any specific basis for her assertion and that the act demonstrated the legislature's concern that marriages be preserved whenever possible. Finally, it ordered the trial court to determine the income tax liabilities of the parties, writing that although appellant's objections had prevented such a determination at trial, it was equitable that the matter be resolved on remand.


OUTCOME: Finding that the trial court erred in considering the children's trust funds when setting respondent husband's child support obligation, the court reversed the child support award. It affirmed the judgments on community property and spousal support, rejecting appellant wife's argument that the Family Law Act was unconstitutional and remanding the case for recalculation of child support and a determination of the parties' income tax liability.

Estate of Weaver


Civ. No. 30740


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 312; 103 Cal. Rptr. 664; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 849



August 18, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 185697, Carl H. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant estate executrix sought review of the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied a motion to confirm the report of the probate commissioner, overruled objections to an inheritance tax report, and fixed the amount of inheritance tax due respondent state controller.


OVERVIEW: Decedent was granted power of appointment to distribute the assets of two trusts at the time of her death. When decedent died, her will purported to exercise the power of appointment over the first trust, which no longer existed. Decedent also left the residue of her estate, including any property over which she had power of appointment with the exception of the assets of the first trust, to her surviving children. Appellant estate executrix filed objections to the inheritance tax appraisal. The probate commissioner admitted extrinsic evidence which showed that decedent was not aware of the second trust, and therefore concluded that no taxable transfer took place by reason of decedent's failure to exercise the power of appointment. The trial court sustained respondent state controller's objections to the commissioner's report. The court affirmed, holding that decedent's will did operate to exercise the general power of appointment of the second trust, whether or not decedent was aware she possessed that power. Therefore, the assets of the second trust had been transferred by decedent to her children and thus were subject to inheritance tax under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13696.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court sustaining respondent state controller's objections to the probate commissioner's report was affirmed. There was no ambiguity in decedent's will justifying the admission of extrinsic evidence. Whether or not decedent knew she held power of appointment over the distribution of the assets of the second trust, her will exercised any powers of appointment she may have had and effected a taxable transfer of property.

 

852 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jacobs


Crim. No. 9594


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


27 Cal. App. 3d 246; 103 Cal. Rptr. 536; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 844



August 16, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 1, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 3650, Thomas F. Keating, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court of Marin County (California) for battery by a prisoner upon a non-prisoner.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his conviction for battery by a prisoner upon a non-prisoner, defendant claimed that he was denied a speedy trial pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1382 and, because of trial strategy differences of opinion between himself and court-appointed counsel, the trial court erred in denying his motion to dismiss the public defender and permit him to represent himself. The court affirmed the conviction. The court found that the 90-day limitation period found in Cal. Penal Code § 1381 applied, not the 60-day period in § 1382, because defendant was a prisoner when the offense was committed. The court held that defendant was not denied a speedy trial, and that § 1381 was not unconstitutional because it treated persons differently than § 1382. The court found that the differences between the sections were rationally based, that they protected the interests of different classifications of persons, and that all persons governed by § 1381 were treated alike. The court held that the trial court was not required to remove court-appointed counsel because of a disagreement as to the method of handling the defense, and defendant did not have a constitutional right to self-representation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for battery by a prisoner upon a non-prisoner. The court held that defendant was not denied a speedy trial because filing period for inmates applied, and that the delays were occasioned by defendant's motions to dismiss counsel, and counsel's motion to withdraw. The court also held that the trial court was not required to dismiss counsel, and defendant did not have a right of self-representation.

MCA, Inc. v. Universal Diversified Enterprises Corp.


Civ. No. 38189


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


27 Cal. App. 3d 170; 103 Cal. Rptr. 522; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 838



August 14, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 6, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 934984, 938009, Robert W. Kenny and Richard C. Fildew, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant debtor sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff purchaser partial summary judgment for possession of the real estate and damages for unlawful detention of the property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant debtor defaulted on its loan, plaintiff owner purchased the property at a trustee's sale, defendant failed to vacate the premises, and plaintiff filed an action for unlawful detainer. The trial court granted plaintiff partial summary judgment, possession, and monetary damages. On appeal the court affirmed. The court held defendant's Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 right to due process was waived by the contract provision for a nonjudicial trustee's sale. The court held the partial summary judgment was proper because defendant did not raise triable issues of fact by its challenge to plaintiff's title for noncompliance with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2924 or by its alleged defenses regarding plaintiff's right of possession where defendant had not stipulated to plaintiff's right of possession under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1166a. The court held the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to strike the testimony of plaintiff's expert witness, and the award of damages was proper under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1161a because the showing of reasonable rental value established plaintiff 's detriment from defendant's unlawful detention of the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting plaintiff owner summary judgment for possession and damages from defendant debtor's unlawful detention of the property because defendant waived due process rights by the contract provision for non-judicial foreclosure sale, defendant did not raise triable issues of fact, the expert witness testimony was properly before the court, and the damage award established the value of defendant's unlawful detention.

Handlery v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 30084


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


26 Cal. App. 3d 970; 103 Cal. Rptr. 465; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1001



July 31, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 29, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 5, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 614828, Clayton W. Horn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which upheld a ruling of defendant State of California Franchise Tax Board. Defendant denied plaintiffs' claims for a refund of income taxes. Plaintiffs contended that they were entitled to have been treated as a unitary business.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers conducted all of their activities in California and derived all of their income from sources within California. All corporate plaintiffs were California corporations. Contending that they were entitled to have been treated as a unitary business, plaintiffs filed claims for a refund of income taxes with defendant State of California Franchise Tax Board. The claims were denied and the trial court upheld the ruling. Plaintiffs sought further review and the court affirmed the order, holding that any uncertainty between Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 25102 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 2403 was resolved in favor of defendant. The court concluded that the administrative construction was not clearly erroneous or unauthorized. California's taxation statutes and regulations did not deny plaintiffs equal protection of the laws. Plaintiffs doing business wholly within the state had no right to be taxed as a unitary business.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the trial court, which upheld a decision of defendant tax board that denied plaintiff taxpayers' claims for a refund of income taxes. Plaintiffs contended that they were entitled to have been treated as a unitary business, but the court held that plaintiffs doing business wholly within the state had no right to be taxed as a unitary business. The court held that plaintiffs' equal protection rights were not violated.

Franklin v. Municipal Court


Civ. Nos. 29013, 29803


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


26 Cal. App. 3d 884; 103 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 993



July 26, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 618575, Byron Arnold and Robert W. Merrill, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in 1 Civil 29013 denying Franklin's petition for a writ of mandate is affirmed. That portion of the judgment in 1 Civil 29803 in favor of the Municipal Court and Judge O'Kane in his official capacity as a municipal judge is affirmed. That portion of the judgment in 1 Civil 29803 based on the order dismissing the "third cause of action" against Judge O'Kane as an individual is affirmed. That portion of the judgment in 1 Civil 29803 based on the order dismissing the "second cause of action" against Judge O'Kane as an individual is reversed. Each of the parties shall bear his own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that denied his petition for a writ of mandate to compel defendants municipal court and judge to return his property to him and that granted summary judgment in defendants' favor on plaintiff's conversion claim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was arrested at his residence, and a revolver was taken from him at that time and was used as an exhibit at the preliminary examination, at the conclusion of which the charge was dismissed and plaintiff was discharged. After the judge denied plaintiff's subsequent request for the return of his revolver, plaintiff filed suit against the municipal court and the judge, seeking a writ of mandate to compel the return of his firearm and alleging conversion. The trial court denied the petition for a writ of mandate and granted summary judgment in favor of defendants. Plaintiff appealed the adverse decision. In affirming the denial of plaintiff's petition, the appellate court concluded that denial was proper because the defendants lacked the power to issue an order releasing plaintiff's revolver where the revolver was not in their custody or within their jurisdiction when demand for its return was made. In reversing in part, however, the appellate court concluded that, although the municipal court was immune from liability, defendant judge was not immune where plaintiff alleged conversion against him in his individual, rather than official, capacity.


OUTCOME: The denial of plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate was affirmed, as the defendants lacked authority to order the release of plaintiff's property when it was not within their control or jurisdiction when plaintiff requested his property's release. Summary judgment in favor of defendant judge was reversed, however, as judicial immunity did not preclude a conversion claim asserted against him in his individual capacity.

Allen v. Flournoy


Civ. No. 39117


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


26 Cal. App. 3d 774; 103 Cal. Rptr. 275; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 985



July 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 11, 1972, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 13, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 987585, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein. Each party shall bear his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, husband and wife, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed their action seeking a declaration that an order confirming an inheritance tax report was invalid. Plaintiffs wanted to contest their liability for the inheritance taxes, which were based on plaintiffs' joint tenancy with the decedent in bank accounts.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, husband and wife, sought a declaration that an order confirming an inheritance tax report was invalid. The taxes were imposed on plaintiffs' joint tenancy with the decedent in bank accounts. The trial court dismissed their action and plaintiffs sought review. On appeal, the court held that if the probate court had the power to fix the tax and if plaintiffs were afforded an opportunity to urge their claim, then plaintiffs could not question their liability after the order became final. As to plaintiff husband individually, the probate court had no power to fix the tax. As to plaintiff wife, the probate court was empowered as to her probate property, despite a lack of personal notice, because she shared in the estate. However, notice to plaintiff husband could not be construed as notice to plaintiff wife as to her non-probate property, which was the only subject of the tax. She had to be given the same opportunity to amend her complaint as plaintiff husband, and the sufficiency had to be determined by the same standards. The court held that the statutory scheme, which did not require notice to plaintiff wife, was unconstitutional. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the action by plaintiffs, husband and wife, to contest their liability for inheritance taxes was reversed and the case was remanded. The statutory scheme under which the tax was imposed on plaintiff wife was unconstitutional where it did not require notice to plaintiff wife, an indispensable party to the proceeding in which the taxes were fixed.

People v. Poulin


Crim. No. 9883


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


27 Cal. App. 3d 54; 103 Cal. Rptr. 623; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 829



July 12, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C 1052, Louis B. Dematteis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) which convicted him of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12310, explosion of a destructive device causing great bodily injury.


OVERVIEW: When appellant was convicted of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12310, explosion of a destructive device causing great bodily injury, after his ex-girlfriend's stepfather was injured by a bomb in a milk carton, appellant sought review. The court affirmed. Sufficient evidence sustained the jury's determination that victim suffered great bodily injury. The injuries suffered by victim were greater than slight, trivial or minor. The jury instruction on great bodily injury was proper. Evidence that appellant admitted one bombing and planned another, together with the similarity of the bombs used and the common motive involved, supported logical inference that appellant committed the crime charged. The record showed that defense counsel, rather than showing a lack of diligence or competence or of reducing the trial to a farce or sham, which was necessary to constitute denial of effective assistance of counsel, showed great diligence and competence, especially considering the overwhelming weight of the evidence against appellant. Cal. Penal Code § 12310 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction because sufficient evidence sustained determination that victim suffered great bodily injury and evidence that appellant admitted one bombing and planned another, together with the similarity of the bombs used and the common motive involved, supported logical inference that appellant committed the crime charged.

Griffin v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 1735


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


26 Cal. App. 3d 672; 103 Cal. Rptr. 379; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 977



July 11, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 7, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue:

1. Directing the Superior Court of Stanislaus County to grant the motion for change of venue, hold a hearing to determine a place where a fair and impartial trial can be had and transfer the cause to that place, all in compliance with California Rules of Court numbered 840-844 (adopted March 4, 1972).

2. Directing the superior court to hold further hearings on petitioner's motion to exclude certain witnesses consistent with this opinion.

3. Directing the superior court to suppress as evidence and return to petitioner items 1, 2, 3, 4, 6, 7, 8, 9 and 10 listed on the return to the search warrant of June 2, 1969.

Except as hereinabove granted, the petition for writ of mandate is denied and the alternate writ discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought writs of mandamus and prohibition directed to the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), after denial by that court of multiple pretrial motions on the eve of petitioner's second trial on a murder charge, particularly a motion for a change of venue, a motion to suppress evidence, and a motion to exclude certain witnesses. He also sought reversal of the amount of bond set by the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's first conviction for first degree murder was reversed, and at retrial petitioner filed motions to change venue, to exclude prosecution witnesses disclosed to the prosecution by petitioner's former counsel, to suppress certain incriminatory statements taken in violation of his Miranda rights, and a motion pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1538.5 and 1540 to suppress evidence obtained pursuant to search warrants. The trial court denied each motion and petitioner sought a writ of mandate. The court issued the writ in part. The size of the county, the gravity of the offense, the persistent and repeated reference in the various newspaper articles to petitioner's prior criminal record, including a murder, resulted in a reasonable likelihood of unfairness without a change of venue. The trial court should have reopened the hearing on the motion to exclude witnesses for a Prudhomme hearing. The search warrant's description of certain items was overbroad, and certain allegations were non-factual and conclusory, and therefore the warrant violated U.S. Const. amend. IV and Cal. Const. art I, § 19, as well as Cal. Penal Code § 1525.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the trial court to grant the motion for change of venue, hold further hearings on petitioner's motion to exclude witnesses, and suppress as evidence and return to petitioner certain items listed on the return to one search warrant. The court denied the petition for writ in all other respects.

People v. McManis


Crim. No. 4734


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


26 Cal. App. 3d 608; 102 Cal. Rptr. 889; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 971



June 29, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 24091, Margaret J. Morris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to sentence, and the case is remanded to the Superior Court of San Bernardino County for that court to exercise its independent judicial discretion as to the eligibility of appellant for probation. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) convicting him of voluntary manslaughter, denying his motions for a new trial and reduction of the verdict, and denying his application for probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with murder. After his plea of not guilty, a jury convicted him of voluntary manslaughter, a lesser and necessarily included offense within the crime charged. The trial court denied appellant's motions for a new trial and for reduction of the verdict. His application for probation was also denied. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment as to appellant's sentence, but affirmed the judgment in all other respects. The court found that the trial court erred in requiring the concurrence of the district attorney before considering probation as an appropriate disposition of the case. The court further found however, that the trial court's error in failing to define "misdemeanor" in the context of the involuntary manslaughter instructions was not prejudicial to appellant. Finally, the court found that under the circumstances, the jury was justified in concluding that appellant intended to fire the fatal shot, and in rejecting his testimony that the gun inadvertently fired during a struggle with the victim over a tire iron.


OUTCOME: The order convicting appellant of voluntary manslaughter and denying his motions for a new trial and reduction of the verdict was affirmed. However, appellant's sentence was reversed and remanded because the trial court erred in requiring the concurrence of the district attorney before considering probation as an appropriate disposition of the case.

John Tennant Memorial Homes, Inc. v. Pacific Grove


Civ. Nos. 29424, 29425


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


27 Cal. App. 3d 372; 103 Cal. Rptr. 215; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 855



June 22, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 21, 1972, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 16, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, Nos. M 4925 and M 4926, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two companion cases, appellants, the city and its tax administrator, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which held that a city ordinance that imposed a special tax on occupants of respondent nonprofit retirement homes was void and enjoined enforcement of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, the city and its tax administrator, sought to enforce an ordinance that imposed a special tax on occupants of respondent nonprofit retirement homes. Respondents filed suit opposing the tax, and the trial court declared the ordinance void and enjoined its enforcement. Appellants sought review, contending generally that the ordinance was necessary to raise revenues to pay for the extra city services provided to the elderly occupants. Affirming the trial court's judgment, the court ruled that pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 1c and Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 214, the state conferred tax exempt status on nonprofit retirement homes, and that appellants' tax ordinance nullified the state tax exemption. The court further ruled that where a conflict between state law and a municipal ordinance existed, the local law was invalid unless the subject matter was exclusively of municipal concern. The court held that the retirement home tax exemption was a matter of statewide concern, rendering appellants' tax ordinance void and unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which held that the special tax imposed on occupants of respondent nonprofit retirement homes was void. The tax was in conflict with the state constitution and tax code, which granted tax exempt status to nonprofit retirement homes based on the benefits conferred by them on the public.

 

861 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Alford v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 12951


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


26 Cal. App. 3d 244; 102 Cal. Rptr. 667; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 938



June 21, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 16, 1972. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 205058, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the writ is reversed with directions to the superior court to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner flag burner sought review from an order entered by respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his request for a writ of prohibition to order respondent to dismiss the case against him on the ground that the ordinance under which he was charged was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner flag burner was charged with a violation of Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code. § 611, and he filed a demurrer to the complaint. The trial court overruled the demurrer, and petitioner sought a writ of prohibition in respondent trial court. The trial court denied the writ. Petitioner contended that the statute was unconstitutional. The court agreed with petitioner and found that the statute was unconstitutional because it was void for overbreadth. The court noted that the definition of "flag" contained in the statute could result in someone being charged with a violation of the statute if he or she used a picnic napkin decorated with a flag. The court also noted that loyalty to the state of one's residence was a usual an desirable social behavior but, any person has the right to dislike the state within which he lives and to say so.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying the writ of prohibition requested by petitioner flag burner and directed the trial court to issue a writ of peremptory prohibition. The court found that the statute was unconstitutional because it was void for overbreadth.

 

862 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kanovitz v. Bloodgood


Civ. No. 39309


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


26 Cal. App. 3d 205; 103 Cal. Rptr. 27; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 933



June 20, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 4367, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant court reporter sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a demurrer of respondent, auditor-controller and county treasurer, without leave to amend, from appellant's petition for writ of mandate regarding court reporter compensation from the state under Cal. Penal Code § 869.


OVERVIEW: Appellant court reporter transcribed a preliminary hearing record from a municipal court, then properly certified and delivered copies. Respondents, auditor-controller and county treasurer, authorized compensation for two copies, but not for three. Appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate and respondents entered a demurrer that was granted without leave to amend. Appellant challenged whether Cal. Penal Code § 869, requiring a court reporter to furnish a copy of a felony preliminary hearing "without charge" to the county, was constitutional. The appellate court affirmed the judgment finding that absent any overriding constitutional infirmity, appellant could only receive that compensation from the state that was expressly authorized by law. Fees could not be awarded in excess of statutory rates, even when counsel for the state agreed to higher compensation. Only when a statute effectively omitted to set fees was payment on a quantum meruit basis allowed (not found here). Equal protection was not violated where no "invidious discrimination" was found in the statute's classification scheme.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding that no constitutional infirmity existed (equal protection was not violated in the statute's classification scheme), and appellant court reporter could only receive that compensation from the state that was expressly authorized by statute. The demurrer of respondents, auditor-controller and county treasurer, without leave to amend was proper.

Banks v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 31218


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


26 Cal. App. 3d 143; 102 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 927



June 19, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the respondent court to annul its order of March 10, 1972, and upon petitioners' compliance with Code of Civil Procedure, section 539, to permit the issuance of a writ of attachment against the described real property of the nonresident defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff travel agency partners filed a petition for writ of mandamus from a decision of respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), seeking to compel respondent to vacate its order denying a motion for a writ of attachment of real property of a nonresident defendant (real party former partner), made pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(3), in a fraud action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff travel agency partners, filed a lawsuit against defendant former partner, for damages caused by defendant's fraudulent misappropriation of partnership funds. Plaintiffs also filed a declaration for attachment of real property of the nonresident defendant under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(3), which authorized a prejudgment attachment. After a hearing, the trial court denied plaintiffs' motion for writ of attachment and dissolved a temporary restraining order regarding property disposal. Plaintiffs filed a petition for writ of mandamus seeking to compel respondent trial court to vacate its order denying the motion for writ of attachment. The appellate court let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent to annul its order and upon plaintiffs' compliance with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537, to permit the issuance of a writ of attachment against the described real property of the nonresident defendant. A "situation requiring special protection to a state or creditor interest" was present in a nonresident attachment, and consequently, § 537 authorizing its use, was constitutionally valid.


OUTCOME: The court let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent trial court to annul its order, and upon plaintiff travel agency partners' statutory compliance, to permit a writ of attachment issuance against the real property of a nonresident defendant former partner. A "situation requiring special protection to a state or creditor interest" was present, and therefore constitutional, in a fraud action.

 

864 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gordon H. Ball, Inc. v. State of Califonria ex rel. Dept. Pub. Wks.


Civ. Nos. 13032, 13039, 13126


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


26 Cal. App. 3d 162; 102 Cal. Rptr. 637; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 929



June 19, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 206631, 206632 and 206630, B. Abbott Goldberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In three consolidated appeals, plaintiff contractors sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which declared that Cal. Gov't Code § 14402.5 could not be applied to their construction contracts entered into before the effective date of the statute, and denied their petitions for writs of mandate by which they sought application of the statute to their contracts.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractors entered into contracts with co-defendant State of California (the state) for construction of portions of the state highway system. As required by Cal. Gov't Code § 14402, certain monies earned under the contracts were withheld by the state. On November 10, 1969, Cal. Gov't Code § 14402.5 became effective which implemented an escrow procedure for the retained funds. After the state refused to apply the escrow procedure to contracts executed prior to the effective date of the statute, plaintiffs filed a petition for writ of mandate and complaint for declaratory relief seeking to have § 14402.5 declared applicable to their contracts. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the state, and plaintiffs sought review. Affirming the trial court's judgment, the court held that no statute was to be given retroactive effect unless the legislature expressly so declared. The court concluded that from the legislative history of the bill, there was no intent on the part of the legislature to permit retroactive application of the escrow procedure; therefore, § 14402.5 applied only to contracts entered into after its effective date.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment declaring that the escrow procedure statute did not apply to the contracts in question and denying plaintiff contractors' petitions for a writ of mandamus. The legislative intent was that the benefits of the statute would apply only to public contracts executed after its effective date.

Damazo v. MacIntyre


Civ. No. 38958


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


26 Cal. App. 3d 18; 102 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 915



June 13, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 9, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 53067, Richard C. Heaton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment dismissing the action is reversed, each side to bear its own costs. The trial court is instructed to take such action as is not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed the grant by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) of defendant marshal's demurrer to plaintiff's action challenging the constitutionality of the provisions of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537 for prejudgment attachment of property without notice or hearing.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers brought action against defendant marshal challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537, which provided for prejudgment attachment of property without notice or hearing in certain categories of civil actions. The trial court granted defendant's demurrer. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court. The court held that subdivisions 3 and 6 of § 537 concerning personal injury actions against nonresident defendants and actions for recovery of public funds paid to illegal drug sellers in undercover drug buys were constitutional. The court held that subdivision 4, permitting prejudgment attachment in an unlawful detainer action was an unconstitutional denial of due process, because no extraordinary circumstance justified the deprivation of a civil defendant's property interest without notice or a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of defendant marshal's demurrer to plaintiff taxpayers' constitutional challenge. The provision for attachment of property without notice or hearing in unlawful detainer actions was unconstitutional.

Allen v. California Board of Barber Examiners


Civ. No. 11651


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


25 Cal. App. 3d 1014; 102 Cal. Rptr. 368; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1095; 54 A.L.R.3d 910



May 30, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 15, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 26, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. M-1614, Bruce W. Sumner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of barber examiners sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandate. The trial court found that the minimum price schedules for barbers were invalid and that the respondent barbers' certificates should be reinstated.


OVERVIEW: Respondent barbers' certificates were revoked by appellant board of barber examiners for furnishing barbering services at prices below those prescribed in the minimum price schedule established by appellant. Respondents sought a writ of mandate directed to compel appellant to vacate and set aside its orders revoking respondents' certificates, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1084 et seq. The trial court issued the writ on the basis that the statutory provisions authorizing appellant to establish minimum schedules for the barbering industry, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 6551- 6557, violated the due process clauses of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I. In addition, the trial court held that the statutes constitute an unlawful delegation of legislative power to an administrative agency. The court affirmed the judgment and held that when the delegation of power to fix prices is to an administrative agency made up of interested members of the industry, the courts have insisted on insist upon stringent standards to contain and guide the exercise of the delegated power.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court which had issued a peremptory writ directing the board of barber examiners to vacate and set aside its order revoking respondents' barber certificates was affirmed. The delegation of legislative power to an administrative agency without stringent standards was illegal.
