People v. Jones


Crim. No. 18237


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


25 Cal. App. 3d 776; 102 Cal. Rptr. 277; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1073



May 19, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 2, 1972, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-003164, Roy J. Brown, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of murder. Defendant alleged that the trial court should have granted his motion to quash the complete jury venire for the county there was such an exclusion of black people and Mexican-Americans that it would violate his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder. On appeal, he argued that the trial court should have granted his motion to quash the complete jury venire for the county there was such an exclusion of black people and Mexican-Americans that it would violate his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Defendant's attorneys filed an extensive memorandum of points and authorities in support of the motion, but it was not drafted as a formal offer of proof, and the motion was denied. The court reversed the conviction, holding that defendant was entitled to a hearing on his motion to show proof. The court also held that, even though the selection procedures were designed to screen out potentially unqualified jurors, when this was done by discriminating against large segments of the population using a test designed to measure something other than ability to make good jurors, the procedures were constitutionally invalid if the defendant who challenged them was a member of one of the discriminated segments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the defendant's conviction for murder, holding that he had been entitled to a hearing to offer evidence on his motion to quash the jury venire for the county on the basis of discrimination that violated his due process rights under the Fourteenth Amendment.

Forte v. Nolfi


Civ. No. 28623


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


25 Cal. App. 3d 656; 102 Cal. Rptr. 455; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1064



May 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 12, 1972, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 532688, Edward F. O'Day, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, except as to the defendant notary, with directions to reassess the compensatory and exemplary damages due from the defendant Nolfi, and to revise the findings of fact, conclusions of law, and judgment with respect to the remaining defendants in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant financiers sought review from Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted judgment in favor of plaintiff debtors and held that the note and deed of trust executed by plaintiffs were null and void and created no lien upon plaintiffs' property. The trial court further awarded damages to plaintiffs for the false, fraudulent, oppressive, and malicious acts of defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff debtors initiated action against defendant financiers for the false, fraudulent, oppressive, and malicious acts of defendants regarding note and deed of trust executed by plaintiffs. The trial court held that such note and deed were null and void and created no lien upon plaintiffs' property. The trial court also awarded compensatory and punitive damages to plaintiffs for defendants' malicious acts. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the finding that defendant individual secured plaintiffs' signatures through fraudulent misrepresentations but that the evidence was insufficient to find that remaining defendants were liable. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to prove that defendants conspired to attempt to exact usurious interest from plaintiffs, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22. The court held that the evidence was insufficient to sustain the punitive damages award, which was the result of passion or prejudice on behalf of the trier of fact. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court with directions to reassess the compensatory and exemplary damages due from defendant individual and to revise the findings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which held that malicious acts of defendant financiers caused note and deed executed by plaintiff debtors to be null and void. The court held that defendant individual did secure plaintiffs' signatures through fraudulent misrepresentations and attempted to exact usurious interest but that remaining defendants were not liable. The court held that an award of punitive damages was not appropriate.

People v. McDaniels


Crim. No. 5959


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


25 Cal. App. 3d 708; 102 Cal. Rptr. 444; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1067



May 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 5, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 12, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 36903, Lloyd A. Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his convictions from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), for two counts of first degree robbery, rape by threats, and the use of a firearm in the commission of the three offenses.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that due to the circumstances of being the only man in the lineup wearing a blue shirt, he was singularly marked for identification by the victims; that the lineup was unreasonably and unnecessarily suggestive and conducive to irreparable mistaken identification and, therefore, violative of his right to due process. The court noted that prudence suggested that the officer conducting the lineup should have advised defendant not to wear a blue shirt. However, the criterion was whether the lineup was unnecessarily suggestive and conducive to irreparable mistaken identity. The court held that the application of criterion in a given case depended upon the totality of the circumstances. The court found that the evidence established that the several victim-witnesses had ample opportunity to observe defendant's facial and other physical characteristics during the commission of the crimes, and that the in-court identifications were based on characteristics other than the blue shirt. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant was identified by several victim-witnesses who had ample opportunity to observe his facial and other physical characteristics during the commission of the crimes.

Fuchs v. Western Oil Fields Supply


Civ. No. 1635


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


25 Cal. App. 3d 728; 102 Cal. Rptr. 74; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1069; 37 Cal. Comp. Cases 953



May 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 16, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 26, 1972. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 142139, Joseph L. Joy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), that denied a motion for an award of attorney's fees from appellee lien claimant's recovery in appellant's suit against defendant below and others resulting from an automobile accident, which culminated in a settlement in favor of appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was an employee of appellee lien claimant. Appellant was involved in an accident with an employee of defendant below. Appellant sued defendant and others. The lawsuit resulted in a settlement for appellant. Appellant sought review of the denial of an award of attorney's fees from appellee's award. The award was in repayment of temporary disability benefits and medical benefits that had been advanced to appellant. Any discussion of settlement agreements had to refer also to the statutes governing judgments. Cal. Lab. Code § 3856 was applicable to judgments in workmen's compensation cases involving third-party tortfeasors. The comparable statute concerning settlement agreements was § 3860. The court found there was no question that §§ 3856 and 3860 established a priority system. The first priority was the attorney's right to a reasonable fee, the second was complete satisfaction of appellee's lien claim, and the residue went to appellant. Appellant's arguments of denial of equal protection of the law and due process both failed. The statutes were constitutional, and the application of them by the trial court was correct. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied the motion for an award of attorney's fees from appellee lien claimant's recovery. Appellant's proposed construction of the California Labor Code section concerning settlement agreements was not maintainable. The code sections in question were constitutional, and the application of them by the trial court was correct.

People v. Snipe


Crim. No. 1085


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


25 Cal. App. 3d 742; 102 Cal. Rptr. 6; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1070; 60 A.L.R.3d 1316



May 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 19, 1972. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 110466, William Zeff, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which held that the application of Cal. Penal Code § 194 (amended) to the beating death of respondents' child 21 months after the beating occurred was ex post facto. Section 194's amendment had increased the time span between beating and death, for the purposes of charging murder, from one year and a day to three years and a day.


OVERVIEW: A child died from beating injuries 21 months after the beating. Respondents were charged with the child's murder, because the child had died within three years and a day of the beating as provided by Cal. Penal Code § 194 (amended). Respondents claimed that application of amended § 194 was ex post facto because at the time of the beating, it was required that a person die within a year and a day. The trial court found the application of amended § 194 had been ex post facto, and the people sought review. The court reversed and found the amendment's application had not been unconstitutional because such amendments, which allowed the admission of evidence upon an issue of fact which was not admissible under the rules of evidence in effect at the time an offense was committed, were not ex post facto. Furthermore, the court found Cal. Penal Code § 3's presumption against retroactivity had not been violated because the amendment had not affected an element of the offense, but instead, had affected a rule of evidence. Also, the court found the issue of double punishment under Cal. Penal Code § 654 was premature because respondents had yet to be convicted and sentenced for murder.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment holding the application of an amended statute, which increased the time between beating and death from one year and a day to three years and a day, was ex post facto because the amendment was a constitutional change in the rules of evidence in effect when respondents committed the beating. The court also held the evidentiary change had not violated the statutory presumption against retroactive penal statutes.

 

872 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Omori


Crim. No. 20642


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


25 Cal. App. 3d 616; 102 Cal. Rptr. 64; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1061



May 17, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-262761, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted appellant of possession of restricted dangerous drugs under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910 after 31 capsules of secobarbital were found in appellant's possession.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who had been in possession of 31 capsules of secobarbital, was convicted of possession of restricted dangerous drugs under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed. Appellant alleged that drug addict status entitled appellant to immunity from criminal prosecution under § 11910. In addressing appellant's allegation, the court found appellant's criminal conviction under § 11910 had not subjected appellant to cruel and unusual punishment in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court held that appellant had been convicted under § 11910 not because of appellant's status as an addict but because of appellant's criminal act. Furthermore, the court found that § 11910, which made no distinction between addicts and nonaddicts, had not denied appellant equal protection because in classifying all possessors of illicit drugs as subject to criminal sanctions, § 11910 was substantially related to the legitimate object of controlling drug traffic and protecting the public welfare. Moreover, the court held that by exempting an addict, who possessed the object of habituation, from § 11910, nonaddicts would be encouraged toward addiction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment convicting appellant of possession of restricted dangerous drugs because appellant's conviction had not subjected appellant, as a drug addict, to cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the Eighth Amendment, nor had it denied appellant equal protection of the law by subjecting defendant along with nonaddicts to criminal sanctions.

In re Marriage of Leff


Civ. Nos. 36775, 37404


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


25 Cal. App. 3d 630; 102 Cal. Rptr. 195; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1062



May 17, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 7, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 714 329, Parks Stillwell and Lester E. Olson, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in part and reversed in part with directions to conduct further proceedings consistent with this opinion; the order denying motion for fees and costs on appeal is reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that found in respondent husband's favor in appellant's divorce action holding that the Nevada divorce decree obtained by respondent was entitled to full faith and credit in California.


OVERVIEW: Respondent husband was awarded a judgment of divorce by a Nevada court. The trial court found that the divorce was entitled to full faith and credit in California and rendered judgment in respondent's favor, ordering that appellant wife took nothing by reason of her complaint. On appeal, the court held that respondent was a bona fide domiciliary of Nevada, noting that respondent maintained a permanent home in Nevada, paid Nevada taxes, and was registered to vote there. However, the court held that because appellant was domiciled in California, at the time she sought the divorce, California law determined her right to adjudicate her claim to alimony and a division of the parties' alleged community property estate in California. The court reversed and remanded to the trial court directing it to make specific findings on the issues of alimony and the existence of and division of alleged California community property. The court held that Nevada had jurisdiction to decide issues regarding appellant's rights in respondent's Nevada separate property. The court remanded the issue of appellant's request for attorney's fees because the trial court's denial was not clearly made on the merits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the lower court's judgment in favor of respondent husband in appellant wife's divorce action. The court held that despite the valid Nevada divorce decree appellant had the right to have her alimony claims and division of California community property adjudicated in California because she was domiciled in California. The court remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

874 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lawrence


Crim. No. 4863


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


25 Cal. App. 3d 498; 102 Cal. Rptr. 16; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1049



May 12, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 8243, E. Scott Dales, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted defendant of conspiracy to murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182(1).


OVERVIEW: Prior to his indictment for conspiracy to murder, defendant was granted immunity for his testimony at another trial. On appeal from the conspiracy conviction, defendant argued that certain testimony violated the immunity agreement and that the admission of hearsay statements of a co-conspirator were erroneously admitted in violation of the confrontation rights. The court affirmed. The court noted that immunity granted to a federal witness protected against state incrimination as well, and that the immunized testimony could not be used at a later trial, nor could it be used to search out evidence which could later be used against the witness. This was a principal known as "use-plus-fruits" immunity. However, because the testimony introduced at trial was derived independently of the immunized testimony. The people did not abridge defendant's immunity from use of the compelled testimony or its fruits. As to the co-conspirator hearsay statements, because the admitted evidence was not "crucial" or "devastating," and did not otherwise amount to a "wholesale denial" of defendant's right to cross-examine the witnesses against him, the evidence was properly admitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of conspiracy to commit murder, and held that defendant's confrontation rights were not violated because no immunized testimony was used, either directly or indirectly, at trial, and co-conspirator hearsay testimony did not abridge defendant's right to cross-examine witnesses against him.

 

875 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kirby v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board


Civ. No. 39841


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


25 Cal. App. 3d 331; 101 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1034



May 4, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 29, 1972.



DISPOSITION: The order of the board is reversed with directions to affirm the decision of the department.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, sought relief from a decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Board (California), which reversed petitioner's decision to revoke the liquor license of real party in interest licensee for violation of Cal. Const. art XX, § 22, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24200(a), and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25601.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, the director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control revoked the liquor license of real party in interest, licensee, for violations of Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24200(a)(b), and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25601. Licensee appealed the department's decision to respondent, the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, which found that evidence was sufficient to support findings but that real party had not violated both statutes and reversed the department's decision to revoke the license from which outcome petitioner sought relief. The court reversed, holding that considerations for determining whether a disorderly house existed because of illegal immoral acts on the premises under § 25601 was identical to considerations in determining whether conduct on a licensed premises was contrary to public welfare under Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22 and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code 24200(a). Being identical, it was unnecessary for petitioner to make any conclusion as to a violation of these sections, since the findings and conclusions of the department that licensee violated § 25601, were supported by the evidence. Petitioner's decision was thus reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board with directions to affirm the decision of petitioner, the director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control to revoke the license of real party in interest licensee because the findings and conclusions of petitioner that licensee had violated the Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code was supported by evidence.

 

876 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gayle v. Hamm


Civ. No. 39089


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


25 Cal. App. 3d 250; 101 Cal. Rptr. 628; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1026



April 28, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP 46619, Howard E. Crandall, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) directing issuance of the peremptory writ of mandamus is affirmed. The attempted appeal from all other orders is dismissed. Petitioners shall recover their costs on appeal against the defendant County of Ventura.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county clerk and real party in interest county appealed from an order entered by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandamus that commanded defendant to ascertain if an initiative petition filed by petitioner proponents had the requisite number of qualified signatures.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner proponents filed an initiative with defendant county clerk that involved restrictions on the ability of a county, here the real party in interest, to own, lease, or maintain an airport within its boundaries. After being advised by county counsel that the initiative would be invalid even if carried at the polls, defendant refused to examine the initiative petition for requisite signatures as required by Cal. Elec. Code § 3707. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court order, which issued a writ of mandamus in favor of petitioners that commanded defendant to determine if the petition was signed by the requisite number of voters, and if so, to submit the petition to the board of supervisors of the county. The court concluded that there was no mandatory duty upon the trial court to determine the validity of the petition because it was not "clear beyond question" that the proposed initiative would be invalid even if enacted. The court found that the position taken by defendant would have required every proponent of an initiative measure to first seek the advisory opinion of the courts as to its validity before getting the measure to the electorate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which issued a writ of mandamus in favor of petitioner proponents that required defendant county clerk to ascertain if an initiative petition had the requisite number of signatures, and if so, to submit it to the board of supervisors of the county, real party in interest here. The petition was not clearly invalid beyond question, and therefore the court was not obliged to rule on its validity.

Blinder v. Division of Narcotic Enforcement


Civ. No. 29434


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


25 Cal. App. 3d 174; 101 Cal. Rptr. 635; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1020



April 26, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 24, 1972, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 22, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 615-800, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, physicians and addicts, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted defendant Division of Narcotic Enforcement's motion for judgment on the pleadings in an action alleging that Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11391- 11395 relating to treatment of drug addicts were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, physicians and addicts, filed a complaint against defendant Division of Narcotic Enforcement seeking a declaration that Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11391- 11395, providing for the treatment of addicts for addiction, were unconstitutional and void, and requesting that defendant be enjoined from further enforcement of the statutes. The lower court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court affirmed holding that the statutes did not deprive plaintiff physicians of their right to practice medicine because physicians are granted certain privileges respecting the administration of drugs, but this privilege was not absolute. The court found that plaintiff addicts had not been deprived of their right to proper medical treatment because treatment was available at county hospitals. The court ruled that plaintiff addicts had not been deprived of their liberty without due process because the statutes did not provide for commitment but for treatment. The court found that the statutes were not unduly oppressive, arbitrary, or capricious, and they did not deprive the narcotic addict of effective methadone treatment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting defendant Division of Narcotic Enforcement's motion for judgment on the pleadings because the challenged statutes relating to treatment for drug addicts were enactments which had for their purpose the interest of public health and welfare and were reasonably necessary for the accomplishment of that purpose. The court held that the statutes were not unduly oppressive, arbitrary, or capricious.

People ex rel. Younger v. Allstate Leasing Corp.


Civ. No. 28898


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


24 Cal. App. 3d 973; 101 Cal. Rptr. 470; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1182



April 18, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 18, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 397284, William H. Brailsford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed and the matter remanded to the trial court with directions to quash the writ of attachment levied upon Allstate's bank accounts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant leasing corporation appealed an order by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied its motion to quash a writ of attachment levied by plaintiff, state of California, against defendant's bank accounts in action by plaintiff to recover monetary penalties and enjoin defendant from defrauding public by means of false and misleading advertising and misrepresentation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, state of California, brought an action against defendants, leasing corporation and associated individuals, and sought to enjoin them from defrauding the public by false and misleading advertising and misrepresentation. Plaintiff sought to recover monetary penalties, pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17536, for each act of false and misleading advertising, and to recover civil penalties pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code §§ 6403, 6408, for carrying on unauthorized business in California. Plaintiff filed a declaration for attachment of defendant leasing corporation's funds pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(5), which was effected without prior notice and was levied upon its bank accounts. Defendant petitioned to quash the attachment and asserted that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(5) was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant appealed. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded to the trial court with directions to quash the writ because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(5) was unconstitutional as it authorized prejudgment attachment of defendant's' "necessities of life" without notice, for collection of money due on penalties imposed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied defendant leasing corporation's petition to quash a writ of attachment levied by plaintiff, state of California, and remanded the matter to the trial court with directions to quash the writ because statute that authorized prejudgment attachment of defendant's "necessities of life" without a hearing upon the validity of the claim was broadly drafted and violated due process.

City of Saratoga v. Huff


Civ. No. 30615


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


24 Cal. App. 3d 978; 101 Cal. Rptr. 32; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1323



April 14, 1972  
April 14, 1972


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 24 Cal. App. 3d 978 at 1006. Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 31, 1972. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Opinion of March 17, 1972. Reported at 24 Cal. App. 3d 978.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon the request of petitioner city, an alternative writ of mandate was issued and directed to respondent city clerk to show cause why a peremptory writ of mandate should not issue directing him to give notice of the sale of $ 2 million of special assessment bonds for an assessment for improvements to be undertaken under the Municipal Improvement Act of 1913 on certain lands of a community college district which were situated within the city.


OVERVIEW: The college district requested that the city take proceedings under the Act in order to finance certain improvements on the property of the district. The city authorized an assessment, which created a fixed obligation incurred for the direct benefit the college district, not the city, and payable over a 10-year period out of ad valorem taxes to be levied against all property within the college district. The court determined that the assessment violated Cal. Const. art. XII, § 40, which provided that the city could not incur indebtedness beyond projected revenues without the approval of two-thirds of the electors. The court held that although under proper circumstances the Legislature could provide for ad valorem taxes at rates in excess of limitations which it had itself fixed, such taxes could not be imposed to pay an obligation which violated the constitutional restriction. Finally, the court decided that because the proposed assessment was invalid, it was unnecessary to determine to what extent improvements could be constructed on land of another public entity in which the governing body ordering the improvements had merely an easement for construction.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ of mandate.

Penaat v. City of San Jose


Civ. No. 29559


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


24 Cal. App. 3d 707; 101 Cal. Rptr. 258; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1163



April 4, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 31, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 242836, O. Vincent Bruno, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff vehicle owner challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied his motion for a preliminary injunction restraining defendant city from towing vehicles from the tow-away zones in the city to any location other than as authorized by Cal. Veh. Code § 22850, and from entering automobiles which are about to be towed, without the permission of the owners thereof.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff vehicle owner's car was illegally parked at a location amply marked as a tow-away zone. City police cited and inventoried the car, and had it towed away by a private company. In affirming the decision of the superior court, which denied plaintiff an injunction to prevent defendant city from towing vehicles, the court held that there was no showing that the authority to negotiate towing contracts was not validly delegated by the city council. Absent such a showing, the presumption established by Cal. Evid. Code § 664 applied: It was presumed that official duty was regularly performed. The chief of police, a public officer engaged by the city council and entrusted with the enforcement of traffic regulations and ordinances, was a proper person to represent the government agency involved in designating the garage to which impounded vehicles were removed. The court further concluded that the tow-truck operator's entry for removal to storage was not an unconstitutional search as items were in plain view, and the officer authorizing the tow was not committing "auto theft" within the meaning of the code.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying plaintiff vehicle owner an injunction against defendant city. There was no showing that the authority to negotiate towing contracts was not validly delegated to the chief of police by the city council, and an inventory of items in plain sight was permissible and did not constitute an illegal search.

Escrow Institute of Cal. v. Pierno


Civ. No. 38786


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


24 Cal. App. 3d 361; 100 Cal. Rptr. 880; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1143



March 27, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 24, 1972, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 23, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles, No. 991297, Max Z. Wisot, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trade association and individuals sought a declaratory judgment that the Escrow Law, Cal. Fin. Code § 17000 et seq., was unconstitutional, particularly the exemptions specified in Cal. Fin. Code § 17006. When the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California) granted defendant corporations commissioner's motion for a judgment on the pleadings in its favor, plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant corporations commissioner, rejecting the contentions of plaintiff trade association and individuals that the Escrow Law, Cal. Fin. Code § 17000 et seq., violated their equal protection rights. Finding that the law, which sought to regulate escrow deposit by independent agents, was prompted by the need to protect the public against poor business practices, the court noted that those individuals exempted from it under Cal. Fin. Code § 17006, such as lawyers and real estate brokers, were members of already stringently regulated professions. Therefore, it concluded, because the classifications were reasonably related to a proper legislative objective, they were constitutional. It also found reasonable the provision of Cal. Fin. Code § 17200 that an independent escrow agent be a corporation, writing that incorporation would avoid the complications that an individual's death would cause and that it would allow defendant greater oversight over the qualifications of the corporate directors, would make escrow records more accessible, and would impose more effective bond requirements.


OUTCOME: Affirming the trial court's judgment on the pleadings denying the declaratory judgment sought by plaintiff trade association and individuals against defendant corporations commissioner, the court held that a law regulating escrow deposit by independent agents that exempted members of already regulated professions from its provisions was reasonably related to a proper legislative objective and thus did not violate the Equal Protection Clause.

West Valley Federation of Teachers, Local 1953 v. Campbell Union High Sch. Dist.


Civ. No. 28606


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


24 Cal. App. 3d 297; 101 Cal. Rptr. 83; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1136; 80 L.R.R.M. 2466



March 23, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 21, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 18, 1972. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 235630, Stanley R. Evans, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee organization appealed from order of Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), denying petition for writ of mandate ordering respondent school district to allow appellant to make direct presentations to its board instead of through negotiating council established under Winton Act, Cal. Educ. Code § 13080 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was one of a number of certificated employee organizations in respondent school district. Thus, it was subject to the Winton Act, Cal. Educ. Code § 13080 et seq., which provided that where there was more than one certificated employee organization, a negotiating council was to be appointed to represent all the certificated employees in the district. Appellant, who was not a participant on the negotiating council, tried unsuccessfully to make direct presentations at respondent's public board meetings. It then petitioned for a writ of mandate ordering respondent to allow direct presentations to its board. The trial court denied the writ. The court affirmed. The affirmative compulsion for employee organizations to meet with the negotiating council to confer and urge the adoption of their ideas was implicit in the requirement of section 13085 that employee organizations were required to exercise the rights given by the Act through the negotiating council. A minority employee organization such as appellant was not precluded from presenting proposals to the employer. Rather, such proposals were required to be referred to the negotiating council.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed denial of writ of mandate ordering respondent to allow direct presentations to its board. Implicit in Winton Act's requirement that employee organizations were required to exercise rights given by Act through negotiating council was requirement that employee organizations meet with negotiating counsel to urge adoption of their ideas.

Security Pacific Nat. Bank v. Goodman


Civ. No. 38505


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


24 Cal. App. 3d 131; 100 Cal. Rptr. 763; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1122; 10 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 529



March 17, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 883970, Bayard Rhone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as it determined plaintiff's right to recover possession of the boat. That portion of the judgment which denied relief on appellants' cross-complaint is reversed, for a retrial on the issue of damage only.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant purchasers challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondent bank on its complaint for the recovery of the boat. The trial court also entered judgment against appellants in their cross-complaints against respondent borrower for the recovery of damages.


OVERVIEW: Respondent borrowed from respondent bank a sum, and executed an agreement granting bank a security interest in a boat. Borrower delivered the boat to a dealer in boats and appellants, purchasers, bought and took possession of the boat. Due to default in payments, the bank repossessed the boat. Borrower paid the bank's charges in repossessing the boat, agreed to make up the delinquent installments, and the bank then delivered the boat to borrower, who paid off the loan and sold the boat. The court affirmed holding that the bank had a right to possession because borrower was not a dealer of boats and both dealer and appellants had notice of the security interest. The court reversed on the issue of damages to appellant, holding that the agreement between borrower and dealer, followed by the delivery of the boat to dealer, effectively transferred borrower's possessory interest to dealer despite noncompliance with the registration statute. The court held that appellants owned an interest in the collateral which the bank knew of, and bank and borrower were jointly and severally liable to appellants for any amount, equal to the excess of value over the note and security agreement.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in its determination that respondent bank had a right to possession of the boat. The court reversed the denial of relief on appellants' cross-complaint, and ordered a retrial on the issue of damage only, holding that respondent borrower was not entitled to possession of the boat when respondent bank returned it to him, because his rights had passed to appellants despite their noncompliance with the registration statute.

Estate of Rogers


Civ. No. 13238


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


24 Cal. App. 3d 69; 100 Cal. Rptr. 735; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1118



March 16, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 10, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 207338, Stanley W. Reckers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of the trial court are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff executrix appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that affirmed the report of the inheritance tax appraiser and denied plaintiff's petition for a refund of gift tax paid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff executrix appealed the trial court's order that affirmed the inheritance tax appraiser's report and denied plaintiff's petition for a refund of gift tax paid. The court affirmed the orders of the trial court. The court held that the proceeds that decedent received from a compromise settlement of a personal injury action were originally his separate property with tax consequences that resulted when he transmuted the settlement proceeds into community property and devised the remainder to the plaintiff. The court found that defendant controller's position was supported by both the case law and legislative history and indicated the court could not ascertain a practicable difference between an award and a compromise settlement under the circumstances. The court reasoned that the term "award" should not be construed so narrowly so as to apply only to judgments rendered after litigation, because it would unduly restrict the operation of the statute. The court also found that the statute was constitutional in that legislative classifications contained therein were based upon rational differences in relation to the subject matter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the trial court. The court held that the compromise settlement proceeds received by plaintiff executrix's late husband in settlement of a personal injury action were originally decedent's separate property with tax consequences that resulted when he transmuted the proceeds into community property and devised the remainder to plaintiff.

People v. Lachman


Crim. No. 19966


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 1094; 100 Cal. Rptr. 710; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1281



March 13, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 10, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 058738, Edward R. Brand, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from judgment of Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of felony drunk driving, contending use of presumption in Cal. Veh. Code § 23126, relating to level of alcohol in person's blood at which he was presumed to be under influence of alcohol, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony drunk driving. The record showed he had been in an accident after speeding on a city street. According to the arresting officer, defendant had strong alcohol on his breath and his speech was slurred. The police chemist who analyzed a sample of defendant's urine found a blood alcohol level of .14 percent and concluded that, depending on objective symptoms, defendant had probably been under the influence of alcohol. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. Cal. Veh. Code § 23126 provided that a person should be presumed to be under the influence of alcohol at the time of an offense if a chemical test revealed .10 per cent or more by weight of alcohol in the person's blood. This presumption did not violate the rule that a defendant was presumed innocent until proven guilty beyond a reasonable doubt. It merely allowed proof of an ultimate fact by permitting that fact to be presumed from proof of a preliminary fact. Also, the use of this presumption did not infringe upon due process because it could be said with substantial assurance that the presumed fact was more likely than not to flow from the proved fact on which it was made to depend.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed defendant's conviction. Presumption did not eliminate requirement that people prove defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt and it did not violate due process because it could be said with substantial assurance that presumed fact was more likely than not to flow from proved fact on which it depended.

Lefton v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 39647


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 1018; 100 Cal. Rptr. 598; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1276



March 6, 1972 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let the peremptory writ issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner couple filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to set aside its order granting the motion of real party in interest to be relieved of a stipulation regarding release of an attachment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner couple filed an action against real party in interest to rescind contracts for the purchase of stock and for money had and received. Petitioners attached a sum which real party had on deposit, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(1). Petitioners and real party in interest entered an agreement in which petitioners agreed to release the attached sum and not to initiate any further attachment and real party in interest agreed to deposit a sum in trust to secure any judgment petitioners might receive. Section 537(1) was ruled unconstitutional. Real party filed a motion to be relieved of its stipulation to keep money in trust on the ground that there had been a failure of consideration for the stipulation. Respondent trial court granted the motion. The court let a peremptory writ issue because it found that respondent had erred in granting the motion and that petitioners' agreement with real party in interest was supported by consideration, specifically their relinquishment of a legal right. Even though § 537(1) was found to be unconstitutional, petitioners had the right to attach under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(2).


OUTCOME: The court let a peremptory writ issue to compel respondent trial court to set aside its order granting the motion of real party in interest to be relieved of a stipulation regarding release of an attachment because it found that petitioner couple had relinquished a legal right, which constituted consideration for the agreement between petitioners and real party in interest.

Johnson v. Contra Costa County Fire Protection Dist.


Civ. No. 28907


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 868; 100 Cal. Rptr. 561; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1261; 37 Cal. Comp. Cases 918



February 28, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 115398, Joseph Genser, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted respondent fireman's petition for writ of mandate directing appellant to issue holiday pay as part of respondent's disability payments under Cal. Lab. Code § 4850.


OVERVIEW: Respondent fireman was injured in the course of duty, and received his full salary in lieu of temporary disability payments under Cal. Lab. Code § 4850. Appellant county deducted holiday pay from respondent's compensation, and respondent sought a writ of mandate ordering issuance of the holiday pay which was granted by the trial court. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed finding that compensation anticipated by § 4850 included all salary and benefits that respondent would have received as a shift worker if he was still working. Payment of holiday pay was part of worker's compensation even though respondent did not work the days required by the county ordinance to qualify for holiday pay. Mandamus was a proper remedy where the court's enforcement of appellant's compliance with § 4850 was ministerial and involved no discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed finding that respondent fireman was entitled to all salary and benefits without deduction for holiday pay that he would have received as a shift worker in lieu of temporary disability payments under state law.

Martinez v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 28739


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


23 Cal. App. 3d 679; 100 Cal. Rptr. 334; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1245



February 23, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1972. Tobriner, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 64174, Joseph P. Murphy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment is reversed without prejudice to petitioner's right to challenge the validity of the measure in any appropriate proceeding if it is enacted and goes into effect.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county board of supervisors and county clerk sought review of the issuance of a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) forbidding them to count the votes in an initiative defendant county board of supervisors called for concerning the need for a housing authority. Respondent indigent had brought suit to stop the initiative and compel start of a housing authority.


OVERVIEW: Respondent indigent filed for a writ of mandate to compel appellant county board of supervisors (board) to proceed to determine a housing authority was needed, rather than submitting the issue in an initiative. Because the issue had already been written onto the ballots, the superior court allowed the vote to take place, but later issued a writ forbidding appellant board and appellant county clerk from counting the votes on the housing authority initiative. Appellants appealed, but appellant board, before the court issued a decision, determined by resolution that there was a need for the housing authority to function. The court determined the issues were not moot because whether the votes cast ought to have been counted and the results announced and published remained. Reversing the decision, the court held that mandamus was not available to prevent the official recordation of the vote of people regardless of any apparent unconstitutionality of the measure.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the granting of respondent indigent's request for a writ of mandamus preventing the counting of votes cast on an initiative put on the ballot by appellant county board of supervisors and clerk to determine whether a housing authority was needed. The court explained that mandamus was not available to prohibit the counting of votes, regardless of any apparent unconstitutionality of the initiative.

People v. Grey


Crim. No. 19433


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


23 Cal. App. 3d 456; 100 Cal. Rptr. 245; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1228



February 10, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-109191, Eugene E. Sax, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  Defendant was fairly tried and justly convicted. The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of receiving stolen goods. Appellant alleged that the trial court erred in denying his motions to suppress evidence, to dismiss the prosecution, and to sever the consolidated information.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of receiving stolen goods. Appellant and another individual were engaged in stealing automobiles, stripping them, exchanging parts, repainting, and selling the stolen cars and their parts. Detectives went to the home of the other individual to investigate a "cut-up" automobile. During interrogation, that individual removed several papers from a briefcase that was in his possession. Subsequent to the individual's arrest, the police searched the briefcase and discovered documents that led them to connect the individual and appellant with theft of an automobile. The documents prompted a search of appellant's place of business of repairing and dealing in used cars. The court held that the search of the briefcase was a valid search incidental to an arrest and that the search of the business was permitted under Cal. Veh. Code § 2805. The court held that the state could require that a licensed business be open to orderly inspections.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellant's conviction of receiving stolen goods. The court held that the search of a co-defendant's briefcase was a valid, warrantless search incident to an arrest and that the search of appellant's automotive business was pursuant to statute of a licensed business. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying appellant's motions to dismiss or to sever the consolidated information.

 

890 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ortleb v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 39345


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 424; 100 Cal. Rptr. 471; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1222



February 9, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 6, 1972, and the petitions of real parties in interest GSC Development Corporation, Capistrano Highlands and Scripps Miramar Development Company for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied April 6, 1972.



DISPOSITION: The peremptory writ of mandate is granted, and respondent court is ordered to annul its orders quashing the writs of attachment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ of mandate to require respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, to vacate its orders quashing two writs of attachment and levies thereunder issued against nonresident corporate defendants pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 537(2), (3). The superior court ordered the writs and levies quashed on the ground of the unconstitutionality of Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 257(2).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed suit against nonresident corporate defendants, pleading multiple causes of action based on contract and tort. Subsequently, petitioners filed a declaration for attachment against all defendants under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 537(1) and caused writs to be issued and levies to be made on real properties in two counties. Property levied upon in Orange County was owned by a real party in interest, a California corporation, and property levied upon in San Diego County was owned by a real party in interest, also a California corporation. Both owners were wholly owned subsidiaries of one of the nonresident corporate defendants, and both intervened in the action below subsequent to the time of the levies. The court found that while, in a prior decision, it had invalidated Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 257(1), petitioners had amended their declarations, as allowed by Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 558, to invoke attachment under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 257(2), for the contract actions, and Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 257(3), for the tort actions, averring the nonresidence of the corporate defendants. The court found that Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 257(2) was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the peremptory writ of mandate and ordered the superior court to annul its orders quashing the writs of attachment.

 

891 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Property Research Financial Corp. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 39331


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 413; 100 Cal. Rptr. 233; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1221



February 9, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest GSC Development Corporation for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 6, 1972.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that subdivision 2 is valid under the due process and equal protection clauses of the state and federal Constitutions. The peremptory writ of mandate is granted, and respondent court is ordered to annul its order quashing the writ of attachment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner creditor requested a writ of prohibition or mandate to require respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to vacate its order quashing a prejudgment attachment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(2). The attachment was levied against the real property of debtor nonresident corporation, the real party in interest. Respondent declared § 537(2) unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner creditor filed suit on an alleged balance due on a promissory note against debtor nonresident corporation, the real party in interest. Petitioner filed a declaration of attachment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(1) and posted an attachment bond as required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 539. After the California Supreme Court declared § 537(1) unconstitutional, petitioner filed a second attachment declaration under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(2). A writ of attachment was issued against real property owned by the real party in interest. Respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County declared § 537(2) unconstitutional and quashed the writ and levy. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition or mandate requiring respondent to vacate its order. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate and ordered respondent to annul its order. The court held that § 537(2) did not violate federal or state constitutional due process and equal protection. Nonresidence was a justification for summary attachment because of a creditor's need to safeguard the collectibility of a judgment. A corporation incorporated in another state did not reside in California within the meaning of § 537(2).


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate and ordered respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to annul its order quashing petitioner creditor's writ of attachment. The court ruled that the statutory section was valid under federal and state due process and equal protection clauses.

People v. Schindler


Crim. No. 18581


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


23 Cal. App. 3d 369; 100 Cal. Rptr. 110; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1217



February 8, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied February 22 and 29, 1972, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 6, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-101669, Joseph L. Call, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of voluntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second degree murder for the death of his wife. On appeal the conviction was reversed, but by then defendant had spent considerable time in prison. After a second jury trial, defendant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter. On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court erroneously denied his motion to dismiss and his motion for acquittal, erred in refusing to dismiss on double jeopardy grounds and erred in making certain hearsay rulings. Defendant also argued that the trial court committed reversible error in refusing to admit certain exhibits into evidence, committed reversible error in instructing the jury, committed reversible error in explaining the verdicts to the jury, and erred in denying probation. After consideration, the court concluded that the court did misdirect the jury when it explained the various possible verdicts. The prejudicial effect of those instructions warranted a reversal. In addition, the court erred in denying probation to defendant. Thus, the trial court's judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that convicted defendant of voluntary manslaughter because the trial court misdirected the jury when it explained the various possible verdicts.

 

893 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Ball


Crim. No. 4959


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


23 Cal. App. 3d 380; 100 Cal. Rptr. 189; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1218



February 8, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 6, 1972. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applied for a writ of habeas corpus to challenge an order entered by the Orange County Superior Court (California), which affirmed his conviction for willfully and unlawfully entering land with the intention of interfering with lawful business carried on by the owner of such land, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his trespass conviction for willfully and unlawfully entering land with the intention of interfering with lawful business carried on by the owner of such land, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602, petitioner claimed that the words "property rights" in § 602 rendered the entire statute unconstitutionally overbroad and vague. The court affirmed the conviction. The court held that even when one portion of a penal statute was unconstitutionally invalid, a defendant's conviction would not be set aside if he were convicted under the remaining valid portion. The court found that the challenged words "property rights" played no part in petitioner's conviction. The court held that petitioner, by placing a table near the loading tram at Disneyland, in order to solicit funds for a cause, and causing the tram to be offloaded to another area, sufficiently demonstrated that he interfered with the owner's lawful business.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed petitioner's conviction for trespass by interfering with an owner's business. The court held that when one portion of a penal statute was unconstitutionally invalid, petitioner's conviction would not be set aside if he were convicted under the remaining valid portion.

Vandegrift v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 12627


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


23 Cal. App. 3d 228; 100 Cal. Rptr. 87; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1206



February 1, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 28, 1972, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 30, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 48490, Richard E. Patton, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Board of Supervisors, auditor, and treasurer, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), that issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding appellants to pay respondent former district attorney additional wages plus interest.


OVERVIEW: After resigning from office, respondent former district attorney brought action for writ of mandate to compel appellants, Board of Supervisors, auditor, and treasurer, to pay him the difference in pay due him under Cal. Gov't Code § 28126. Appellants alleged that respondent was properly paid the rate of pay established pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 28126 for his office at the beginning of his term and that, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XI § 5, his compensation could not be increased after his election or during his term of office. Respondent alleged that an amendment to Cal. Const. art. XI § 5 that permitted an increase in compensation during a time of war was applicable. The court held that respondent was entitled to an increase when the statutory rate increased because the United States was actually engaged in war in Vietnam during the time respondent was in office.


OUTCOME: Because respondent former district attorney held office during the Vietnam War, the court held that the constitutional amendment permitting an increase in pay during a term of office was applicable. The court affirmed the trial court's peremptory writ of mandate requiring appellants, Board of Supervisors, auditor, and treasurer, to pay respondent the additional wages to which he was entitled.

City of Los Angeles Fire & Police Protective League v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 37928


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


23 Cal. App. 3d 67; 99 Cal. Rptr. 908; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1191



January 21, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied, 23 Cal. App. 3d 67 at 78.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 960664, Harold F. Collins, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment is reversed in its entirety. Neither party shall recover costs in this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a police officers' league and police officers, challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied its claim against defendants, city and police chief, for full compensation for uniformed officers during their lunch break under Los Angeles, Cal., Ordinance 137,042 (1968).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a police officers' league and police officers, sought overtime pay for time the officers spent eating meals during their shifts, arguing that because they were required to wear uniform and gun, to respond to citizen requests, and to seek supervisory approval prior to taking meal breaks, they were on duty and entitled to overtime. The trial court found that plaintiff officers were entitled to one-half their usual pay for the meal period. The parties challenged, plaintiffs arguing that the officers were entitled to full pay, and defendants, the city and police chief, arguing the officers were not entitled to any pay. On appeal, the court held that the ordinance of a chartered city, to the extent that it did not conflict with state statutes, specified the limits of overtime pay for its employees. The court distinguished cases with similar factual backgrounds involving workers' compensation claims, where off-duty officers had been injured in responding to spontaneous calls, because those were recognized under state workers' compensation laws. The court held that the local ordinance specifying the conditions of overtime pay controlled, and reversed the order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiffs, a police officers' league and police officers, holding that the controlling municipal ordinance provided clear limitations on the use of overtime pay, and to award overtime pay to plaintiff officers would be an invasion of the province of the legislature, which could remedy any inequity with statewide statutory amendments.

Hensley v. Peace Officers Training Fund


Civ. No. 29717


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


22 Cal. App. 3d 933; 99 Cal. Rptr. 728; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1308



January 17, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 30, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 248322, John D. Foley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, convicted of various offenses, appealed an order of the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California), which sustained defendant peace officers training fund's demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' class action. Plaintiffs sought refunds of penalty assessments added to fines imposed on them after their convictions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were convicted of various misdemeanors, which carried a specific fine. Plaintiffs filed an action on behalf of themselves, as well as other people similarly situated, to obtain refunds of the penalty assessments added to those fines. They also filed a writ of prohibition staying all further penalty assessments pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 13521. Plaintiffs claimed that § 13521 was unconstitutional on its face as to the classifications. The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' class action suit and sustained defendant peace officers training fund's demurrer without leave to amend. The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint and ruled that the legislature had the power to add penalty assessments to the maximum fines set by a particular statute. The court held that § 13521 was constitutional and did not deprive plaintiffs of their equal protection under the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff convicts' action and ruled that the legislature had power to add penalty assessments to maximum fines set by statute. The court held that the statute was constitutional and plaintiffs were not deprived of their equal protection rights.

 

898 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jennings


Crim. No. 20281


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


22 Cal. App. 3d 945; 99 Cal. Rptr. 739; 1972 Cal. App. LEXIS 1310



January 17, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-171759, John A. Shidler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of his conviction from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County, (California) of assault with a deadly weapon, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). His motions for a new trial and probation were denied, and he was sentenced to state prison. On appeal, the court reversed the decision. It was erroneous and unfair to defendant to give the last instruction on simple assault as being lesser and included offense of assault with a deadly weapon. The jury had already begun deliberation. Purcell was distinguished because a new and unmentioned offense was not introduced in the midst of jury deliberation. In addition, it could not be said that § 245 did not require proof of an intent to kill, as did Cal. Penal Code § 217, thereby making it "lesser" in that respect. The Legislature might not have felt that the conduct denounced in § 245 was sufficiently more likely to occur than that denounced in § 217 and for that reason, a greater penalty was required as a deterrent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that convicted defendant of assault with a deadly weapon, and sentenced him to state prison, holding it was error to give the last instruction on simple assault as being lesser and included offense of assault with a deadly weapon. The court ruled that to give the instruction during deliberations interfered with the right of a defendant to a fair trial.
