Vincent v. State of California


Civ. No. 38012


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


22 Cal. App. 3d 566; 99 Cal. Rptr. 410; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1715



December 30, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 23, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 980180, Robert W. Kenny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a judgment in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, (California) that was based on a clerk's transcript, only. The state conceded there was no controversy as to the material facts, but stated that respondents, individually and as a class, were either totally disabled recipients, or their spouses who had given up other employment in order to serve as attendants to the recipients.


OVERVIEW: The state appealed from a trial court judgment for the plaintiffs. The plaintiffs, individually and as a class, were totally disabled recipients, or their spouses who had given up other employment in order to serve as attendants to the recipients. The recipients and the spouses lived together and the attendant spouse received attendant care payments from the state through its disability and aid to blind programs which were partly federally funded. However, payment for attendant services rendered by spouses was denied because of the changed provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 13700 and its companion regulation in the California Department of Social Welfare (SDSW) Eligibility Assistance Standards Manual. The trial court had granted the plaintiffs summary judgment on the statutory grounds that there was no defense to the action and that no triable issue of fact was presented, and struck as void SDSW regulations barring spousal attendant care payments. The court affirmed the lower court judgment finding it properly based upon application of the federal equal protection clause which did not allow arbitrary classification to deny rights to a class such as attendant care spouses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment against the state that struck as void the changed provisions of the California Department of Social Welfare Eligibility Assistance Standards Manual. The court noted that state laws created an arbitrary classification and denied equal protection to totally disabled recipients and attendant spouses by prohibiting payment for attendant care to a disabled recipient's spouse because they lived together.

 

900 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Reaves v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 13094, 13095, 13096


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


22 Cal. App. 3d 587; 99 Cal. Rptr. 156; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1717



December 30, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 26, 1972, and petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 23, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandamus issue directing the San Joaquin County Superior Court to cease and desist from following its present procedures outlined above and to implement new procedures consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, prison inmates in consolidated, class action cases, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California) to change its procedures in processing petitions for extraordinary writs. Petitioners claimed that the procedure of having the district attorney verify the facts in the petition and prepare the order violated Cal. Const. art. III by delegating judicial authority.


OVERVIEW: Following standard procedure, respondent, the trial court, sent the petitions for writs of mandate or habeas corpus filed by petitioners, prison inmates in consolidated, class action cases, to the district attorney who verified the facts in the petitions and prepared the orders. After their petitions were denied, petitioners sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to change the procedures. The court held that because petitioners sought to change an alleged unconstitutional procedure that would affect future filings of all prison inmates, the class actions for mandamus were proper. The court held that Cal. Const. art. VI, § 10 gave respondent original jurisdiction in proceedings for extraordinary relief, and that respondent's duty was to act as an independent arbiter and fact finder in those proceedings. Because the district attorney's primary function under Cal. Gov't. Code § 26500 was to act as the public prosecutor for the people, he was not independent, and respondent's current procedure was an improper delegation of respondent's authority in violation of Cal. Const. art. III. Therefore, the court issued the writ and ordered respondent to change its procedures.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ compelling respondent, a trial court, to change its procedure of having the district attorney verify the facts in petitions for extraordinary writs and prepare the orders on the petitions because the procedure was an improper delegation of judicial authority. The court held that the class action cases of petitioners, prison inmates, was proper because it affected future filings by all prison inmates.

 

901 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hoiland


Crim. No. 19819


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


22 Cal. App. 3d 530; 99 Cal. Rptr. 523; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1711



December 29, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 23, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 88916, John A. Westwick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgments from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara (California), which convicted defendants, on a jury verdict, of participating in a riot in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 405, and inciting to riot in violation of Cal. Penal Code §404.6.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted by a jury of participating in a riot. Defendants appealed asserting that the grand jury that indicted them was selected by a process which systematically excluded young people under the age of 30, and that persons between the ages of 18 and 20 were improperly excluded from the trial jury. The court held that the fact that persons under of the age of 21 were excluded from jury service was undeniable as it was mandated in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §198, that in order to be competent to act as a juror, a person had to be 21 years old. Additionally, the fact that that age was arbitrarily chosen did not mean it was unconstitutional, as once it was conceded, as defendants did here, that any classification could be properly based on chronological age, then the selection of the particular age was a matter of legislative, and not judicial, concern. Further, there was no evidence that individuals between 21 and 30 was a segment of the population that was a legally cognizable class requiring separate constitutional recognition, as people in that age group lacked the quality of distinctiveness required for creation of a legally cognizable class. Judgments affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgments of convictions affirmed where appellants failed to show that the exclusion of young people from the grand jury was unconstitutional as persons in the age group at issue did not constitute a legally cognizable group, and where persons under the age of 21 were properly excluded from jury service under statute.

 

902 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chain


Crim. No. 20162


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


22 Cal. App. 3d 493; 99 Cal. Rptr. 472; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1706



December 28, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 25, 1972, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 23, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A167640, Earl C. Broady, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) where he had been convicted of rape.


OVERVIEW: In appellant's challenge to his convictions for rape, he contended that the evidence was insufficient to support the conviction; the trial court erred in receiving evidence of uncharged prior sexual misconduct allegedly committed by appellant; and the trial court erred in permitting the jury to separate after the case was submitted to them. The court affirmed. While appellant argued that the testimony of his victim was inherently improbable, his support of that argument asked the court to reweigh the credibility of the witnesses at trial, which the court could not do. Appellant failed to object to the trial court's admittance of an alleged act of forcible oral copulation committed upon the victim, which was not the subject of a criminal charge. He was precluded from raising the issue on appeal, unless it was clear that the objection, if made, would not likely have erased the prejudicial effect of the evidence. Cal. Penal Code §§ 1121 and 1128 (1969) vested the trial court with discretion to separate or to sequester jurors without the qualification that they be permitted to separate only before the cause is submitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the trial court, upholding appellant's convictions for rape, because the evidence was sufficient to support his conviction. Any error in the trial court's admittance of an alleged act of forcible oral copulation was precluded from review on appeal because appellant had failed to object at trial. The trial court was, by statute, vested with the discretion to separate the jurors.

Breiner v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 38352


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


22 Cal. App. 3d 382; 99 Cal. Rptr. 180; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



December 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 982079, Abraham Gorenfeld, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that dismissed their complaints against respondents, City of Los Angeles and others, for declaratory and injunctive relief from an ordinance that could condemn appellants' property to expand the airport. Appellants contended that their properties were being taken not for physical occupation and to avoid inverse condemnation claims.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, property owners, brought complaints against respondents, City of Los Angeles and others, that sought declaratory and injunctive relief from an ordinance that could condemn appellants' property to expand the airport. The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed appellants' complaints after sustaining respondents' demurrers without leave to amend. The superior court correctly sustained the first demurrer because that court could not inquire into respondents' motive or the public necessity of the challenged ordinance because the issue was a political question. The court ruled that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1241 governed the necessity of condemnation. The court held that the necessity of the ordinance was not a justiciable controversy under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1060 and relevant precedent. The court rejected the contention of appellants' second complaint that an illegal election occurred because it lacked factual support. The court found appellants' third complaint was irrelevant and misplaced reliance on an assembly bill. The court found that the fourth complaint was fatally deficient because it raised a false issue of easement when appellants' fee simples were taken.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed the complaints of appellants, property owners, against respondents, City of Los Angeles and others, for declaratory and injunctive relief from the ordinance allowing respondents to condemn appellants' property to expand the airport. The court held that appellants' allegations about the properties being taken were not justiciable because they concerned the political question of public necessity.

 

904 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bowen


Crim. No. 9278


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


22 Cal. App. 3d 267; 99 Cal. Rptr. 498; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1692



December 22, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 17, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1972. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 45011, Harold B. Hove, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) sentencing him to state prison following a jury trial in which he was found guilty of murder in the second degree with five admitted prior convictions. Defendant claimed that the trial court denied him due process of law in refusing to allow him to interview or depose the victim's 4-year-old son and various errors regarding the prior felony convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for murder on the grounds that the trial court erred in refusing to allow him to depose the victim's four-year-old son, who was an eyewitness to the offense, in improperly instructing the jury regarding defendant's intoxication, and in the use of prior felony convictions. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that the trial court denial of defendant's motion to compel a meeting with the victim's son was proper because defendant was not prejudiced as a result of not meeting with the son, as the son did not testify at trial, defendant was free to subpoena him at any time, and there was no evidence that the prosecution withheld information. The court also held that the use of prior convictions was properly used to impeach defendant because the guilty plea to the prior conviction offense was entered voluntarily and there was nothing to indicate that defendant was deprived of an opportunity to consult with counsel regarding the plea. The court also held that the jury was properly instructed on the effect of defendant's intoxication and that the admission into evidence of the victim's bloody clothing was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction because the trial court properly instructed the jury regarding defendant's intoxication and properly denied his motion to compel a meeting with the victim's four-year-old son. The court held that defendant was free to subpoena the child and there was no indication that the prosecution withheld information. The court held that prior conviction evidence was properly utilized to impeach defendant.

Cucamonga County Water Dist. v. Southwest Water Co.


Civ. No. 10026


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


22 Cal. App. 3d 245; 99 Cal. Rptr. 557; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1691; 3 ERC (BNA) 2007



December 21, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 18, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CW-3393, Carl B. Hilliard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it denies acquisition of the Rochester system to the District and the trial court is directed to make necessary findings of fact and conclusions of law and enter judgment in accordance with the views herein expressed, including that upon payment of $ 249,000 there shall be condemned to the use of the District all of the property employed by Rochester in providing water service to that portion of the service area designated as proposed Assessment District No. 5, being water rights arising out of and the easements conveyed to Rochester by the deed from Charles W. Smith, dated February 20, 1889, together with the collection and diversion system, reservoir and reservoir site, and transmission and distribution facilities. The judgment is otherwise affirmed.

Respondents shall recover costs, by analogy to the rule that a successful condemner must nevertheless pay the proper costs of appeal incurred in good faith. (Sacramento Drainage Dist. ex rel.  [***2] State Rec. Bd. v. Reed, 217 Cal.App.2d 611, 612-613 [31 Cal.Rptr. 754].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a water district, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that denied appellant acquisition of respondent's privately owned water system and required just compensation be paid by appellant for an easement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a water district, filed suit to obtain ownership of certain property belonging to respondent, a privately owned public utility water company, after deciding to provide water services via the establishment of a public utility. The court entered judgment denying acquisition of respondent's water system to appellant, but providing for other just compensation to respondent for an easement. The court modified the judgment to provide that appellant was entitled to acquisition, under eminent domain, of respondent's water system after an additional just compensation payment was made. The court affirmed the remainder of the decision. The court held that legislation requiring the payment by appellant for the water system was constitutional. The court held that a misnomer regarding the type of water right possessed by respondent was harmless, as it did not affect a substantial right of either party.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the payment of just compensation by appellant, but modified the order to reflect that appellant was entitled, via eminent domain, to acquire respondent's private water system.

Garavatti v. Fairfax Planning Com.


Civ. No. 28533


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


22 Cal. App. 3d 145; 99 Cal. Rptr. 260; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1677



December 20, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, No. 54979, Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), that denied plaintiffs a writ of mandamus in an action against defendant, the city zoning commission, concerning the modification of a special use permit to operate a food store.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs received a special use permit to operate a food store during the day. After plaintiffs began to operate the store at night, defendant, the city zoning commission, amended plaintiffs' use permit, requiring them to be closed at night. Plaintiffs filed suit seeking to vacate said amendment of their use permit. The court affirmed the denial of plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandamus. The court held that the delegation of power to defendant was constitutionally valid with respect to the granting of a conditional use permit. The court further ruled that plaintiffs had been granted a special use permit that was properly subject to modification by defendant. The court found that defendant acted properly in amending plaintiffs' permit to protect the general health, safety, and welfare of the public, after numerous complaints had been registered as a result of the evening and early morning hours of plaintiffs' business.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandamus because defendant's modification of plaintiffs' special use permit to operate a food store was designed to protect the general health, safety, and welfare of the public.

 

907 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Turner


Crim. No. 9800


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


22 Cal. App. 3d 174; 99 Cal. Rptr. 186; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1681



December 20, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1972. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 13773, Thomas F. McBride, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her conviction in the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) for forgery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 470, and possession and uttering of a check with intent to defraud, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 475a. She asserted that the trial court erred in rejecting her proffered instructions on circumstantial evidence and in allowing the jury to receive new evidence after submission of the case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a judgment entered on a jury verdict finding her guilty of forgery, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 470, and possession and uttering of a check with intent to defraud in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 475a. The court affirmed the judgment. The court found that every element of the two offenses charged was shown by direct evidence, and that the proffered instructions on circumstantial evidence were properly refused. Further, defendant was not deprived of due process by being obliged to model a scarf and sunglasses similar to those worn by the woman in the video for comparison by the jury. In the absence of an objection from either side, it was permissible for the court to reopen the case after submission to the jury to have the defendant pose for the jury. Additionally, the jury's use of a magnifying glass was neither new evidence nor an experiment; the mere making of a more critical examination of an exhibit than was made during the trial was not objectionable. Finally, although it was error not to give a specific intent instruction, as it was inconceivable that the jury could have arrived at a contrary verdict, the failure to do so was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for forgery and possession and uttering of a check with intent to defraud because it was not error to refuse to give an instruction on circumstantial evidence when both offenses were proved by direct evidence. Furthermore, allowing the jury to make a more critical examination of an exhibit after submission of the case was not objectionable.

 

908 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Farr v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 38961


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


22 Cal. App. 3d 60; 99 Cal. Rptr. 342; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1667; 1 Media L. Rep. 2545



December 17, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 12, 1972, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 20, 1972.



DISPOSITION: The order imposing judgment of contempt is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner reporter sought a writ of review of an order of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which found him in contempt of court for failing to answer questions regarding the identities of persons who supplied him with confidential information for his newspaper article. In this action, petitioner received information in violation of respondent's order regarding potential witness testimony in a high publicity case.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) issued an order prohibiting the release of the content or nature of witness testimony. Subsequent to the issuance of this order, petitioner reporter received and publicized a witness' statement. Upon interrogation by respondent during the trial, petitioner refused to disclose his source. Following the conclusion of the trial, respondent held a hearing, wherein petitioner again refused to identify his sources. Respondent ordered petitioner be held in contempt of court. Petitioner sought a writ of review of respondent's order, and the court affirmed the judgment of contempt. The court rejected petitioner's contention that respondent had no jurisdiction and held that respondent had the power to punish for contempt occurring in an action, even if the principal action was terminated. The court also held that the protections conferred by Cal. Evid. Code §1070 were outweighed by respondent's duty to control its own officers and explore violations of its orders. Finally, the court held that respondent's interest in providing a fair trial outweighed any of petitioner's rights under U.S. Const., amend. I.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which imposed a judgment of contempt upon petitioner reporter. The court held that respondent retained jurisdiction over the matter, that petitioner was not entitled to immunity because the court was empowered to explore violations of its order by its own officers, and that the public interest in a fair trial outweighed petitioner's First Amendment rights.

 

909 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


O'Keefe v. Atascadero County Sanitation Dist.


Civ. No. 37237


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


21 Cal. App. 3d 719; 98 Cal. Rptr. 878; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1114



December 1, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 17, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 26, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 37155, Richard F. Harris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to overrule the demurrer as to the third cause of action, only, and allow respondents time in which to answer. Each side is to bear its own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff resident appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) that sustained defendant sanitation district and others' demurrer to plaintiff's suit. The suit alleged, among other things, that plaintiff was denied equal protection and a republican form of government by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 4730 and that defendants violated Cal. Health & Safety Code § 4792 and 4793 by diverting bond proceeds.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff resident appealed a judgment that sustained defendant sanitation district and others' demurrer to plaintiff's mandamus action. Plaintiff alleged, among other things, that he was denied equal protection and a republican form of government by operation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 4730 and that defendants violated Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 4792 and 4793 by diverting bond proceeds. The court affirmed and reversed in part the judgment. The court ruled that demurrer to plaintiff's allegations involving § 4730 was properly sustained. The court ruled that there was no prohibition, constitutional or otherwise, against elected officials being appointed to serve in other capacities. Thus, the fact that § 4730 permitted defendant's non-elected board of director's to be comprised of the elected county board of supervisors was not unlawful. The court ruled that the demurer to plaintiff's claim under §§ 4792 and 4793 was improperly sustained. The court ruled that plaintiff stated a cause of action in that it was alleged that defendants diverted bond proceeds to a project other than the project for which the underlying bonds were authorized and issued.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and reversed in part the judgment that sustained the demurrer to plaintiff resident's suit. The statute declaring defendant sanitation district's director to also be the county board of supervisors did not deny plaintiff equal protection or a republican form of government. The demurrer to the claim that bond proceeds were diverted to a project other than the one the bonds were authorized for should not have been sustained.

 

910 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Lewis v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 27528


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


21 Cal. App. 3d 339; 98 Cal. Rptr. 407; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1076



November 18, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 13, 1972. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 603476, John W. Bussey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the surviving husband and daughter of the deceased, challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed the wrongful death action as to defendant city.


OVERVIEW: The deceased was injured in a municipal bus accident on July 29, 1965. She filed a claim for personal injuries with defendant city and county. A statute mandated that any claims against the city must be filed within 100 days of the injury. She died on May 9, 1968, as a result of her injury. Plaintiffs, surviving husband and daughter of the deceased, filed a wrongful death action on May 8, 1969. Defendants demurred to the complaint, with leave to amend. After no amendment was filed, the trial court dismissed the wrongful death action as to the city. Appellants contended that the claims statute unconstitutionally denied equal protection of the law and the filing of the claim for personal injury put the city upon notice and substantially met the claims requirement. On appeal, the court affirmed, because it found no merit in the equal protection argument and found that the substantial compliance rule was only applied when some claim, even though technically defective, was filed. The court found that the claim for personal injuries could not be deemed substantial compliance with the claims statute and did not support a wrongful death action by the heirs.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the wrongful death suit as to defendant city was affirmed, because the deceased's claim for damages for personal injuries suffered in a bus accident cannot be deemed substantial compliance by her heirs with the claims statute, so as to support plaintiff, surviving spouse and daughter of deceased's, wrongful death action.

 

911 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Montalvo v. Madera Unified Sch. Dist. Bd. of Education


Civ. No. 1373


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


21 Cal. App. 3d 323; 98 Cal. Rptr. 593; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1075



November 17, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 15, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 13, 1972. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 16586, Jack L. Hammerberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor sought review of the decision by the Superior Court of Madera County (California), which held that respondent high school's regulation regarding the length of hair was valid and did not violate U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Appellant minor was prohibited from wearing long hair by respondent high school's regulation. Appellant filed a claim for declaratory relief against respondent. The trial court held that the regulation was valid. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court reasoned that respondent had the right to promulgate rules subject to constitutional protections. The court examined previous cases and determined that hair style was not a per se expression of speech. Professional educators testified that lack of control regarding the appearance of students would result in a deterioration of the educational atmosphere. Any protection granted to the decision of a hair style was found under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and absent a clear violation of the constitutional right, the regulation was presumed valid. Due to the evidence that wearing of long hair on males disrupted pupils, the regulation was upheld. The court also rejected appellant's equal protection argument as it had been raised in a previous case and disposed of.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to deny appellant minor's claim for declaratory relief because it found that hair length was not protected as speech by the First Amendment. There was evidence that hair length affected pupils' concentration so the regulation did not violate the Fourteenth Amendment.

 

912 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Schenley Affiliated Brands Corp. v. Kirby


Civ. No. 12893


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


21 Cal. App. 3d 177; 98 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1063



November 15, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 13, 1971, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 13, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue restraining the Department of Alcoholic Beverage control from enforcing subdivisions (f)(3) and (f)(4) of rule 100. The temporary stay issued by this court will be dissolved upon issuance of the peremptory writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant liquor wholesalers sought a preemptory writ restraining appellees, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and its director, from enforcing Cal. Code Regs. tit. XIV, § 100(f) (1970). Appellants claimed that the rule exceeded appellees' statutory power, and that it was enacted in violation of the rulemaking procedures.


OVERVIEW: Appellant liquor wholesalers claimed that an amendment to Cal. Code Regs. tit. XIV, § 100(f) (1970) violated the law because it was enacted improperly and it exceeded the statutory power of appellees, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and its director. Appellants said the rule punished smaller businesses, as only those businesses that purchased in large quantities would be able to obtain a discount. Appellees countered that the amendment put all purchasers on an equal playing field regarding discounts. The court agreed with appellants in part and held invalid the first paragraph of § 100(f)(3), which prohibited multiple discount rates to buyers, and the unit discount in § 100(f)(4). It said that those sections could be easily severed from the remainder of the rule. The court also held that no rulemaking violations occurred, and that appellants could not argue to change a rule and then cry foul when it was actually changed. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate restraining appellee from enforcing §§ 100(f)(3) and (4). The court also held that no constitutional violations were evident in the current rule after severability of the two sections were made.


OUTCOME: The court held that two sections of an administrative rule governing discounts were invalid as claimed by appellant liquor wholesalers. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate restraining appellees, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and its director, from enforcing the two offending sections. The court also held that appellant failed to rebut the presumption that the remaining sections of the rule were invalid.

Barger v. All-Coverage Ins. Exchange


Civ. No. 35622


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


20 Cal. App. 3d 675; 97 Cal. Rptr. 888; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1211

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer sought review from a decision of the California Superior Court finding that proceeds from an assessment conducted by respondent insurance commissioner under Cal. Ins. Code § 1062, in excess of the amount needed to pay all approved claims be refunded to policyholders. The insurer asserted that the excess funds should be used to restore its surplus.

The commissioner ordered that funds in excess of the amount needed to pay the approved claims be refunded to the insurer's policyholders. The trial court and court both upheld this decision, with the court noting that state control over insolvent insurers was important and that § 1062 granted insurance commissioners discretion when proceeding to make assessments of insurers. The court rejected the insurer's arguments that § 1062 contradicted with Cal. Ins. Code § 1391 in this regard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment.

In re Wells


Crim. Nos. 9292, 8712


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


20 Cal. App. 3d 640; 98 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1208



October 18, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 17, 1971, and the petitions of petitioner and appellant in Nos. 9292 and 8712 and respondent in No. 8712 for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied December 16, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition of petitioner and appellant should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 42287, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to take evidence on the motion to quash the indictment before reconsidering the ruling on the motion. If the motion is found to be good, the court shall dismiss the indictment and release appellant from custody. If the motion be denied, the court shall rearraign appellant for judgment and pronounce judgment on the jury's verdicts.

The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.

The purported appeal from the order denying probation is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction of assault from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California). Defendant asserted that certain demonstrative evidence should not have been admitted, he was denied a speedy trial, and the court erred when it failed to allow defendant discover how the grand jury was chosen.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction of assault with a deadly weapon and asserted that the trial court erred when it admitted certain demonstrative evidence, and denied defendant's motion for an indefinite continuance. Defendant also challenged the prosecutor's use of his preemptory challenges and asserted that the prosecutor purposely excluded negroes from the jury. The court reversed the judgment with directions that the trial court take evidence on defendant's motion to quash the indictment, then rule on the motion. Defendant was not denied a speedy trial because the delay complained of was reasonable under the circumstances. The trial court properly allowed the demonstrative evidence to clarify the circumstances of the charged crimes. Defendant was not prejudiced by adverse publicity because the jurors all indicated they would not be influenced by any such publicity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case with instructions to allow defendant to take evidence on how grand jurors were selected. The court found that the demonstrative evidence was properly admitted, and defendant was not denied a speedy trial.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus on the grounds that the California Criminal Syndicalism Act, Cal. Penal Code § 11401(3), was unconstitutional. Petitioner was charged with distribution of leaflets in violation of § 11401(3).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner defendant was charged with distribution of leaflets in violation of the California Criminal Syndicalism Act (Act), Cal. Penal Code § 11401(3). Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition, which was denied, by the appellate court. Petitioner sought relief in federal court and the U. S. Supreme Court held that they could not intervene except in special circumstances. The trial court denied petitioner's writ of habeas corpus. Petitioner sought review and filed another writ of habeas corpus. On review the court held that when they previously denied petitioner's writ of prohibition they were bound by a decision which had declared the Act constitutional. The court stated that the decision that they had previously followed had been expressly overruled and that because of that they were bound to grant his petition for a writ of habeas corpus and held that § 11401(3) was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner defendant's writ of habeas corpus for his distribution of leaflets charge. The court held that the section of the California Criminal Syndicalism Act, which he was charged under, was unconstitutional.

 

916 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


Crim. No. 18295


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


20 Cal. App. 3d 534; 97 Cal. Rptr. 883; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1198



October 14, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 248 143, James Greely Kolts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of voluntary manslaughter in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 192.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction and held that evidence of justifiable homicide was an issue of fact that was properly presented to the jury. Where appellant's self-defense claim was largely predicated on his own version of the incident, although there was considerable corroborating testimony, the jury was not required to accept appellant's version of the facts. The court held that the impeachment provision of Cal. Evid. Code § 788, had been uniformly upheld on a number of occasions and there was no new basis presented to reconsider the provision's validity. Appellant could be impeached by proof of a prior felony conviction, and such impeachment did not violate the due process clause of either the federal or state constitution. Finally, the court held that the trial court issued adequate instructions concerning the principles of law governing the case. The trial court was not required to instruct on specific points developed at trial absent a request.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of voluntary manslaughter because the court would not interfere with the jury's factual determination on the issue of justifiable homicide. The court also held that there was no basis to overturn the impeachment by prior conviction statute and that the trial court adequately charged the jury on the principles governing the case.

People v. Temple


Crim. No. 19771


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


20 Cal. App. 3d 540; 97 Cal. Rptr. 794; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1199



October 14, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-110482, Lloyd T. Hanson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is modified by striking the words "a felony." The conviction as modified is affirmed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for resentencing in accord with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of felony violation of Cal. Penal Code § 270 and sentenced him to probation for 12 years, conditioned on the payment of a fine and confinement in county jail on alternate weekends for one year.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a felony violation of Cal. Penal Code § 270, failure to provide support for his children. On appeal, the court modified the conviction to a misdemeanor, based on the California supreme court's determination that the felony provision of § 270 was unconstitutional. The court held that the imposition of a felony violation on a father who remained absent from the state for 30 days was a denial of equal protection. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support a misdemeanor conviction, as the evidence showed that defendant had lied to the California Bureau of Public Assistance regarding his ability to support his children. The court remanded to the trial court for resentencing consistent with a misdemeanor.


OUTCOME: The court modified defendant's conviction to a misdemeanor, in response to the California supreme court's determination that the felony provision of the delinquent child support statute was unconstitutional, and remanded to the trial court for resentencing consistent with a misdemeanor conviction.

People v. Wilson


Crim. No. 19568


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


20 Cal. App. 3d 507; 97 Cal. Rptr. 774; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1195



October 13, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 88718, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which convicted defendant of three counts of oral copulation and statutory rape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of three counts of oral copulation and one count of statutory rape. Defendant challenged his conviction for oral copulation and asserted that the evidence failed to support his conviction. On review the court affirmed and held that Cal. Penal Code § 288a, was violated by placing one's mouth on the genital organ of another and that the victims' testimony that defendant kissed them in the vaginal area with his tongue was sufficient evidence to support his conviction. The court held that the jury was properly instructed on the definition of an accomplice and the requisites of corroboration and that the testimony of the two victims coupled with the defendant's admission to the mother furnished adequate corroboration and supported the jury's findings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for oral copulation and statutory rape. The evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction and the jury was properly instructed on the definition of an accomplice and the requisites of corroboration.

Modern Mgmt. Method v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 39209


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


20 Cal. App. 3d 496; 97 Cal. Rptr. 791; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1194



October 12, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for alternative and peremptory writs of prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners company sought a writ of prohibition to prohibit respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), from execution of an order that dissolved attachment. Petitioners asserted that the decision that California attachment statute, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(1), violated procedural due process could not be applied retroactively to permit the dissolution of writs of attachment prior to the date of that decision.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners company sought a writ of prohibition to keep respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County from execution of an order that dissolved attachment. Respondent entered its order to dissolve attachment on the authority of the California Supreme Court, which declared that the California statute that authorized prejudgment attachment, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537(1), was unconstitutional. Petitioner asserted that the supreme court decision could not be applied retroactively to their writ of attachment that was levied prior to that decision. On review the court denied petitioner's writ. The court held that the motion to dissolve did not test the validity of the writ of attachment when levied and since no constitutional statute authorized attachment at the time respondent heard the motion to dissolve, respondent's action was the proper one.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners company's writ of prohibition that sought to stop respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County from execution of an order that dissolved attachment. California's attachment statute was declared unconstitutional prior to the time respondent heard the motion to dissolve.

 

920 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Elisabeth H.


Civ. No. 1520


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


20 Cal. App. 3d 323; 97 Cal. Rptr. 565; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1177



September 29, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 9208, Howard C. George, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which declared her to be a ward of the court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §602, because she had violated Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11556, being in a room or place where any narcotics are being used with knowledge that such activity is occurring.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared appellant minor to be a ward of the court because it found that she had violated Cal. Health & Safety Code §11556, by her presence in a car in which marijuana was found. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the evidence was sufficient to conclude that appellant knew illegal narcotic use was occurring in her presence, but concluding that in order for § 11556 to pass constitutional muster, more than mere voluntary presence was required for a conviction. The court stated that the supreme court would need to establish the additional elements, but that the evidence in this case did not support a finding of possession by appellant or that she aided or abetted the illegal drug use. The court held that evidence which raised a strong suspicion of appellant's involvement was not sufficient to support a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's declaration of appellant minor as a ward of the court for knowing presence in a place where narcotics were being used, because while the evidence was sufficient to conclude that appellant was voluntarily present in the vehicle and knew that illegal use of narcotics was occurring, the enforcement of the statute required more than mere presence and knowledge, in order to be constitutional.

 

921 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Golden


Crim. No. 17581


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


20 Cal. App. 3d 211; 97 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1167



September 22, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-230974, Malcolm M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), convicting defendant with possession of obscene matter with intent to distribute, based on evidence obtained from state's search warrant.


OVERVIEW: State charged defendant with burglary, receiving stolen property and possession of obscene matter with intent to distribute. The lower court acquitted defendant of all charges except possession and intent to distribute obscene material, the charges to which defendant had admitted. Defendant appealed the conviction claiming that state's search warrant was issued in violation of defendant's constitutional rights as it was issued prior to an adversary hearing and in the absence of probable cause. The court held that no prior adversary hearing was required because the it served no more than to establish the factor of probable cause for the warrant and defendant's admission established the necessary element. The court also held that U.S. Const. amend. I did not protect the distribution and sale of obscenity, but assuming it did, the court found that state satisfied the stronger showing of probable cause required before the issuing of the warrant. The court held that informant information corroborated by defendant's admission, clandestine transactions, conversations as to distribution, and FBI information was sufficient strong probable cause for the issuance of state's search warrant.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant with possession and intent to distribute obscene material was affirmed on the grounds that while defendant's actions were not constitutionally protected, the state satisfied a strong showing of probable cause in the issuance of its search warrant as required by the First Amendment.

Charles S. v. Board of Education


Civ. No. 28005


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


20 Cal. App. 3d 83; 97 Cal. Rptr. 422; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1152



September 20, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 19, 1971, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1971. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 609,313, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff suspended public school students appealed an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied declaratory relief under Cal. Educ. Code § 10607 and denied their petition for a writ of mandamus seeking termination of their suspensions pending a full due process hearing, expungement of the suspensions from their records, and additional educational programs to compensate for the suspensions.


OVERVIEW: A petition for a writ of mandate was filed on behalf of plaintiff suspended public school students seeking a court order terminating their suspensions pending a full due process hearing, expunging their records, and providing additional educational programs to make up lost school time. The trial court denied their petition and refused to declare that Cal. Educ. Code § 10607 required a due process hearing or was unconstitutional, and plaintiffs appealed. The court held that § 10607 did not violate due process and was not unconstitutional because it did not permit imposition of suspension without notice of charges and at least an attempt to ascertain accurately the facts involved and to give the student an opportunity to present his side of the case. The court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying mandatory and declaratory relief to plaintiff suspended public school students because the statute prescribing the procedure on suspension comported with state and federal constitutional requirements of due process of law.

 

923 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fuller


Crim. No. 18987


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


20 Cal. App. 3d 159; 97 Cal. Rptr. 455; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1161



September 20, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A-238 289 and A-236 816, Sherman W. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied probation and sentenced defendant to prison for the term prescribed by law based on the California Rehabilitation Center's decision that states that defendant was not a suitable subject for the program, giving as its basis for rejection defendant's excessive criminality.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to possession for sale of a narcotic, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500.5 and unlawfully selling, furnishing and giving away a narcotic, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501. Criminal proceedings were adjourned and defendant was referred to department 95, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051, which found that defendant, was a narcotic drug addict and committed him to the California Rehabilitation Center. The California Rehabilitation Center informed the trial court that defendant was not a suitable subject for the program, giving as its basis for rejection defendant's excessive criminality. The trial court denied probation and sentenced defendant to prison. The court affirmed the denial of probation and held that § 3051 was sufficiently clear to provide the reasonable certainty of definiteness required for due process. The court held that based on defendant's six prior felony convictions, the trial court could properly concluded that there was no abuse of discretion in the determination that defendant was not suitable for treatment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of probation and held that defendant's due process rights were not violated by being committed to the rehabilitation center for evaluation and that there was not abuse in discretion in determining that due to defendant's six prior felony conviction, he was not suitable for treatment because of excessive criminality.

 

924 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Bayside Timber Co. v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 28244


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


20 Cal. App. 3d 1; 97 Cal. Rptr. 431; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1144; 1 ELR 20425; 3 ERC (BNA) 1078



September 16, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 15, 1971, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 148093, Edmund Scott, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The superior court will take further proceedings not inconsistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant San Mateo County Board of Supervisors challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) which directed the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate requiring appellant to issue a road grading permit to respondent timber company on the basis that the Forest Practice Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 4521- 4618, preempted the field of logging and timber operations in California.


OVERVIEW: Respondent timber company obtained an operations permit under the Forest Practice Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 4521- 4618, but appellant board of supervisors refused to issue local permits. The trial court ordered appellant to issue the permits. On appeal, appellants raised for the first time the constitutionality of the act. The court agreed with the trial court that the act preempted the field of logging and timber operations in California and addressed the constitutionality of the act because of the great public importance of the issue. The court noted that the act had delegated to timber interests the power to formulate forest practice rules which, when adopted, had the force and effect of law, but had provided no guides or standards to prevent its abuse. Such a grant of authority was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. The court noted that the constitutional fault was compounded by the fact that the legislative authority had been delegated to persons with a pecuniary interest in the subject matter of the rules. The court therefore held that part of the act unconstitutional which provided for the promulgation of forest practice rules.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court because the statute on which it had relied, which dealt with the promulgation of forest practice rules, was unconstitutional.

 

925 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barton


Crim. No. 19758


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


19 Cal. App. 3d 990; 97 Cal. Rptr. 329; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1344



September 10, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 248951 and 248800, Sherman W. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to withdraw his guilty pleas entered to violations of grand theft and Cal. Veh. Code § 10851, on the basis that such pleas were obtained in violation of his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with grand theft, Cal. Penal Code § 487, and with violating Cal. Veh. Code § 10851. Defendant pleaded guilty to both charges and was later sentenced to prison as a result of such guilty pleas. Approximately nine years later, defendant was arrested and he sought to withdraw his earlier guilty pleas on the basis of judicial decisions that had been entered in the interim. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. The court held that defendant could not seek to change his guilty pleas solely because of case law that had been decided after his guilty pleas were entered. The court further held that defendant waived his right to lower court remedies when he admitted his guilt, and he did so under the law then existing. The court noted that defendant could question his guilty pleas only if he could show serious dereliction on the part of counsel that was sufficient to show that his plea was not, after all, a knowing and intelligent act. Defendant failed to meet such showing and the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that denied defendant's motion to withdraw his guilty pleas because his constitutional rights were not violated when he entered such pleas. The court held that defendant could not change his mind about earlier entered guilty pleas on the sole basis that subsequent judicial decisions would have prevented him from doing such had he known of their disposition at the time of his pleas.

 

926 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Madrid


Crim. Nos. 5666, 5736, 5775


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


19 Cal. App. 3d 996; 97 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1345



September 10, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 20, 1971, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1971.



DISPOSITION: Let the writs issue as prayed. Petitioners Madrid and Thomas are remanded to the Superior Court for the County of Los Angeles, and petitioner Compton to the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco, where they shall be permitted to withdraw their pleas of guilty and further proceedings shall be had in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three petitioners challenged their convictions from the Superior Court for the County of Los Angeles, and the Superior Court for the City and County of San Francisco (California), where petitioners pled guilty pursuant to plea bargains to kidnapping for the purpose of robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 209. The crimes committed were not connected; however, the court consolidated the matters because of the similarity of the legal issues involved.


OVERVIEW: Each of three petitioners entered a plea of guilty for kidnapping for the purpose of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 209, and challenged his conviction, claiming that his conduct was not within that proscribed by § 209, and sought relief on habeas corpus. Petitioners alleged that the compelled movement of the victims in each case was incidental to the robbery being committed and did not substantially increase the risk of harm to those persons. The court consolidated the matters solely because of the similarity of the legal issues involved. The court found that nothing foreclosed the granting of relief to those convicted for violating § 209 based upon a plea of guilty. The court held that the records provided a sufficient basis to conclude that in each case the compelled movement of the victims simply facilitated commission of the robbery and was thus incidental thereto and failed to "substantially increase the risk of harm." Accordingly, the court granted each writ and remanded petitioners to their proper jurisdictions to withdraw their pleas of guilty and to be prosecuted to the fullest extent of the law for the remaining crimes charged against them.


OUTCOME: The court issued writ of habeas corpus as to each of three petitioners to remand each petitioner to his jurisdiction to withdraw his guilty plea of kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and allow prosecution on the remaining charges. The court found that the compelled movement of the victims in each case was incidental to the robbery being committed and did not substantially increase the risk of harm to those persons.

 

927 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Silva & Hill Constr. Co. v. Employers Mut. Liab. Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 35699


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


19 Cal. App. 3d 914; 97 Cal. Rptr. 498; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1336



September 7, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 4, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 881754, Alfred P. Peracca, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of plaintiff insured in its action against defendant insurer, but disallowed recovery in the amount of $ 31,000 for an item of damage allegedly sustained by plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff insured was a construction company which contracted with the state to build and deliver a highway within a prescribed period of time, but failed to do so. Plaintiff then filed an action against defendant insurer to defend and pay claims as provided by the terms of a liability policy. Plaintiff appealed from a portion of the judgment disallowing recovery in the amount of $ 31,000 for an item of damage sustained by plaintiff. The court affirmed the decision, and the California Supreme Court transferred the matter back to the court for reconsideration in light of Cal. Gov't Code § 14376. Plaintiff contended that the trial court erred in characterizing the late charge as a penalty. The court affirmed the order and held that § 14376 was a legislative determination that late charges imposed on a construction company by a state contract fell within the provisions of Cal. Civ. Code § 1671 and were valid liquidated damages. The state never intended to require and defendant never intended to provide coverage for the damage suffered to the work. It followed that insofar at the $ 31,000 late charge was proximately caused by such damage, it was not covered by defendant's policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision in favor of defendant insurer and against plaintiff insured and held that the state never intended to require and defendant never intended to provide coverage for damage suffered to the work. Therefore, the late charge against plaintiff that was proximately caused by such damage was not covered by defendant's policy.

 

928 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nero


Crim. No. 4435


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


19 Cal. App. 3d 904; 97 Cal. Rptr. 145; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1335



September 3, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 20, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 27, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 20328, Leland C. Nielsen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of second-degree murder after he challenged the constitutionality of the selection of the grand jury.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was indicted by the grand jury and convicted by the lower court of second-degree murder. Appellant challenged his conviction, contending that the lower court erred in denying his motion to quash the grand jury indictment because potential jurors were unconstitutionally excluded, that substantial evidence did not support his conviction, and that the court abused its discretion in imposing his sentence. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment. Potential jurors were not excluded on an impermissible basis, as classifications based on wealth or youth were not protected. Therefore, appellant was not entitled to information as to jurors' net worth because such information was irrelevant. Substantial evidence showed that appellant acted with malice to support his conviction for second-degree murder, and the lower court did not abuse its discretion in not sentencing appellant to probation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, because appellant failed to show that potential jurors were excluded on an impermissible basis, substantial evidence supported appellant's conviction, and the lower court did not abuse its discretion in sentencing appellant.

 

929 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. McCullin


Crim. No. 19176


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


19 Cal. App. 3d 795; 97 Cal. Rptr. 107; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1326



August 31, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A008051, Hampton Hutton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order specifying the minimum term of imprisonment under Penal Code section 1202b is reversed with directions to the trial court to vacate the sentence and take up the matter of probation and sentence anew in light of the views set forth in our foregoing opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which specified a minimum sentence under Cal. Penal Code § 1202(b) after defendant was convicted of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187.


OVERVIEW: The people and defendant entered into a stipulation that if defendant was convicted of first degree murder, the people would not seek the death penalty. Defendant was convicted. The trial court specified a minimum sentence under Cal. Penal Code § 1202(b) over the people's objections. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. It found that § 1202(b) was not applicable because the offense defendant was charged with was punishable by death. The court construed "punishable by death" to mean an offense for which the statute authorized the death penalty as a possible maximum punishment regardless of whether it was imposed in a particular case. The court reasoned that its construction of the phrase comported with the commonly acknowledged meaning of the word "punishable" as used in criminal statutes in general and with the purpose for which § 1202(b) was enacted. The court remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to vacate the sentence and take up the matter of probation and sentence anew.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case with directions to vacate the sentence and take up the matter of probation and sentence anew. The court held that the offense that defendant was convicted of was an offense punishable by death and therefore the statute allowing the trial court to specify a minimum term of imprisonment did not apply.

County of Alameda v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 26558


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


19 Cal. App. 3d 750; 97 Cal. Rptr. 175; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1320; 48 A.L.R.3d 332



August 30, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 60398, Lincoln F. Mahan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, City and County of San Francisco and its tax collector, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which granted judgment to plaintiffs, neighboring counties, in plaintiffs' action alleging that a tax defendants sought to impose on commuters was illegal and void.


OVERVIEW: Defendant City and County of San Francisco enacted an ordinance imposing a tax on the adjusted gross incomes of persons who practiced a trade in San Francisco but resided elsewhere. Plaintiff neighboring counties filed suit alleging that the tax was illegal and void. The trial court found that the tax was illegal and void and granted plaintiffs a preliminary injunction. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. It held that the provisions of the California Constitution and U.S. Constitution that guaranteed equal protection proscribed local taxes which operated to unfairly discriminate against intercity business. The court noted that defendants' ordinance did far more than require commuters to pay their fair share of the cost of services provided by defendants. The court found that the formula adopted by defendants to compute the tax was arbitrary and could not be deemed a valid or reasonable method of apportioning the cost of services.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. It found that the license tax on commuters which defendants, City and County of San Francisco and its tax collector, had enacted was illegal and void. The court held that the provisions of the state and federal constitutions which guaranteed equal protection of the laws proscribed local taxes which operated unfairly to discriminate against intercity business.

In re Brown


Crim. No. 9078


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


19 Cal. App. 3d 659; 97 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1313



August 26, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order of the superior court vacating the judgment and commitment made and entered December 12, 1969 by the superior court in Sacramento County in action No. 32366 is reversed, and the case is remanded to the trial court with directions to ascertain the date upon which petitioner was granted probation in that action, and to make its order directing the Adult Authority to deem the commitment contained in the aforementioned judgment as dated as of the date on which such probation was granted, and to consider the term of imprisonment under that commitment as commencing upon the date upon which petitioner was delivered to prison under commitment from Solano County for his subsequent offense. The court is further directed to otherwise deny the petition for writ of habeas corpus and discharge the order to show cause.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from an order by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which vacated one of two commitments under which petitioner was serving concurrent terms, in a proceeding instituted by a petition for writ of habeas corpus. Petitioner contended that the trial court lost jurisdiction to pronounce the judgment in question under the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged the revocation of probation and imposition of a concurrent sentence on a prior offense following his conviction on a subsequent offense. The trial court vacated the commitment on the ground that it lost jurisdiction to pronounce judgment when it failed to act within 30 days of receipt by the probation officer of petitioner's waiver of counsel and personal appearance in revocation proceedings under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.2a. The people appealed. The court reversed, finding that the delay was due to the probation officer's error in misplacing the waiver and that the trial court acted within 30 days of actual notice of the waiver. The court held that the trial court was not to be divested of jurisdiction absent a showing that petitioner has been prejudiced by the errors or omissions of the state. The court held that petitioner was not prejudiced by the delay because the sentence imposed on the prior offense was concurrent and deemed to commence when he was first delivered to prison under commitment for the subsequent offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order, which vacated the commitment, and remanded to the trial court to enter an order directing that the term of commitment for the prior offense should be deemed as having commenced on the date upon which petitioner was delivered to prison under commitment for his subsequent offense.
