Coast Bank v. Holmes


Civ. No. 10509


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


19 Cal. App. 3d 581; 97 Cal. Rptr. 30; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1305



August 24, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 16, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 21, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 148804, William S. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is modified by striking therefrom that portion decreeing reformation of the promissory note and, as so modified, the judgment is affirmed and the matter is remanded with directions to the trial court to set a time and place for a hearing to determine a reasonable attorney fee for services rendered by defendant's counsel on appeal and to add such amount to the fee previously awarded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lender appealed a judgment in favor of defendant borrower on defendant's cross complaint by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), in an action for recovery on a promissory note, plus attorney's fees. Plaintiff claimed that the parol evidence rule barred defendant's affirmative defenses, and that the award of attorney's fees was an invalid retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 1717.


OVERVIEW: Defendant borrower executed a promissory note in favor of plaintiff lender. Plaintiff brought suit to recover principal, interest, and attorney's fees. Defendant filed a cross-complaint for reformation. The trial court found in favor of defendant on all affirmative defenses except for illegality, adjudged that plaintiff take nothing, decreed reformation, and awarded defendant attorney's fees. Plaintiff appealed, alleging that the parol evidence rule barred defendant's affirmative defenses and that the trial court erred in awarding attorney's fees when it applied Cal. Civ. Code § 1717 retroactively. The court held that the trial court properly admitted evidence showing a failure of consideration and fraud, and that its findings on those claims were supported by substantial evidence. The court affirmed, but modified by striking the portion of the judgment that ordered reformation of the note to add varying terms because that extrinsic evidence was irrelevant and did not support the judgment. It held that defendant was entitled to attorney's fees under Cal. Civ. Code § 1717, because the application of § 1717 to pre-existing contracts was a reasonable exercise of governmental power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, but modified the judgment to strike the portion that ordered reformation of the promissory note between plaintiff lender and defendant borrower. The court held that the trial court had properly admitted defendant's evidence of fraud and failure of consideration. The award of attorney's fees to defendant was proper because the trial court's application of a new statute to a pre-existing contract was reasonable.

Estate of Gill


Civ. No. 37537


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


19 Cal. App. 3d 496; 96 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1300



August 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 10, 1971. Files, P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 21, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 956254, Ben Koenig, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, state controller for decedent's estate, sought review of a judgment in which the lower court refused to impose an inheritance tax upon an inter vivos transfer of real property by decedent to respondent, decedent's daughter under Cal. Rev. and Tax. Code §§ 14550, 14551.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, state controller for decedent's estate, challenged the lower court's judgment which refused to impose an inheritance tax upon an inter vivos transfer of real property by decedent to respondent decedent's daughter. The trial court held that the transfer was not subject to inheritance tax, as it constituted a gift given without consideration. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and held that the retrospective application of an amendment to the inheritance tax statute, Cal. Rev. and Tax. Code § 13644, was constitutional. The statute established a conclusive presumption of a reservation for life of interest in property transferred where the transferor retained possession or enjoyment of income or interest in the property transferred until his death. The court found that the presumption was not applicable. Respondent held fee simple title to the property vested by the deed, and decedent's occupancy of a portion of the house subsequent to the transfer was due to respondent's implied invitation and was not due to decedent retaining possession or reserving any interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment in which it refused to impose an inheritance tax upon an inter vivos transfer of real property by decedent to respondent, decedent's daughter, as a gift transfer was not subject to inheritance tax.

People v. Jones


Crim. No. 4317


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


19 Cal. App. 3d 437; 96 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1296



August 20, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-21753, Raymond Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), convicting him of lynching in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 405(a) (1933) and incitement to riot in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 404.6.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed a conviction for lynching in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 405(a) (1933) and incitement to riot in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 404.6, contending that § 405(a) was unconstitutionally vague and uncertain, that the trial court erred in not instructing the jury sua sponte on the elements of lynching and incitement to riot, and that the admission of notes seized at the jailhouse constituted prejudicial error. The court on appeal affirmed, holding that § 405(a) was clear sufficiently clear on its face to put defendant on notice of what conduct was prohibited, that the language of § 405(a) used terms with ordinary definitions such that the trial court was not required to define specific terms, and that defendant had no right of privacy in jail such that confiscation of his letters could not constitute an illegal search and seizure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of lynching and incitement to riot, holding that the lynching statute presented no vagueness problem, that the statutory language was sufficiently clear such that it was permissible to use it to instruct the jury, and that defendant had no privacy rights in jail such that his letters could be confiscated.

 

935 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Uribe v. Howie


Civ. No. 10718


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


19 Cal. App. 3d 194; 96 Cal. Rptr. 493; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1271



August 10, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of respondents Howie and Washburn & Bell for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 6, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 96676, John Neblett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of the trial court are reversed and the cause remanded for further considerations consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), that denied petitioner farm worker's writ of mandate for disclosure of pesticide spray reports filed with respondent agricultural commissioner pursuant to Cal. Agric. Code § 11733, on the basis that the reports were exempted by Cal. Gov't Code § 6254 and nondisclosure was justified under Cal. Gov't Code § 6255.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner farm worker had sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent county agricultural commissioner to allow inspection and copying of pesticide spray reports filed with respondent by pesticide control operators pursuant to Cal. Agric. Code § 11733. The writ was denied on the basis that although Cal. Gov't Code § 6253 provided that all public records were open to public inspection, the reports contained trade secrets exempted by Cal. Gov't Code § 6254, and nondisclosure was justified under Cal. Gov't Code § 6255. The state, intervenor in the petitioner's action for a writ of mandate, challenged the decision. The court reversed, holding that the exemption for trade secrets in § 6254 was not absolute, that the exemption for records compiled by licensing agencies in § 6254 applied only when the prospect of enforcement proceedings were concrete and definite, that disclosure of the reports was in the best interests of the public, and that the reports were not exempt from public disclosure under the governmental privilege provisions of Cal. Evid. Code § 1040.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of petitioner farm worker's writ of mandate for disclosure of pesticide spray reports filed with respondent county agricultural commissioner, holding that disclosure of the reports was in the public's best interests even if the records contained trade secrets, and the disclosure exemption for records compiled for licensing purposes was only applicable if there was a definite prospect of enforcement proceedings.

 

936 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clay


Crim. No. 4387


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


18 Cal. App. 3d 964; 96 Cal. Rptr. 213; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1449



July 27, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 6, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 23185, James E. Cunningham, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the sentence she received for her conviction for second degree murder, entered in the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), asserting that the requirement of district attorney consent to her probation under Cal. Penal Code § 1204 violated the separation of powers principles of Cal. Const. art. III.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of second degree murder. The trial court reduced her offense to non-statutory voluntary manslaughter and sent her to prison. She was returned to court under the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1168 with a recommendation from the prison authorities that she be placed on probation. The probation officer's supplemental report also recommended probation. The superior court denied probation because the district attorney would not grant his consent, which was mandatory under Cal. Penal Code § 1203. The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded the case for the superior court to exercise its discretion in deciding defendant's application for probation without reference to any mandatory concurrence by the district attorney. Such a mandatory requirement violated the doctrine of separation of powers, Cal. Const. art. III, because it threatened the independent power of the judiciary in sentencing defendants. Because the mandatory consent provision was severable, the court permitted the rest of the statutory provision to stand.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded her case with directions that the superior court exercise its discretion in determining whether she would receive probation, without regard to whether the district attorney concurred. The mandatory concurrence provision of the statute offended constitutional principles of separation of powers.

 

937 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re George S.


Civ. Nos. 37353, 37354


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


18 Cal. App. 3d 788; 96 Cal. Rptr. 203; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1431



July 19, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 21, 1971. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Juvenile Court Nos. 397533, 397534, Marvin A. Freeman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In each case the order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that her children were dependent children, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a). Appellant contended that she was entitled to appointed counsel and that dependency proceedings required a proof beyond a reasonable doubt standard.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother's children were adjudicated dependent children under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600(a). The court affirmed. First, the court held that the evidence adequately supported the finding that the welfare of the children required that their custody be taken from appellant. Second, appellant contended that she was entitled to the appointment of counsel. The court held that undoubtedly, with counsel, the record would have been different. However, the court could not agree with the implication that a different decision might have been made if appellant had had the aid of counsel. Third, appellant contended that the statute under which the juvenile court acted was unconstitutional because Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 701 authorized a finding made upon a preponderance of the evidence and that the constitutionally required standard was proof beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that the custody of minor children was determined upon the civil standard of proof by a preponderance of evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, and held that there was adequate evidence to support the dependency adjudication. The court held that appellant mother was not entitled to the appointment of counsel. The court further held that the custody of minor children was determined upon a preponderance of evidence.

 

938 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Candelaria


Crim. No. 17894


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


18 Cal. App. 3d 754; 96 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1428



July 16, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 3, 1971.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which committed him to a drug rehabilitation center under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3100.6 after determining he was an addict.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested by police officers for driving under the influence of narcotics and was taken to the jail infirmary. The prosecution filed a petition for commitment under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3100.6. The trial court committed appellant. Appellant challenged the commitment, contending that his detention and arrest were unlawful and that the procedures under Cal. The court held that the statute was not constitutionally defective for having not time limit, and appellant was alleging no violation. The court also held that the state legislature had broad discretion and § 3100 was not unconstitutional because it prescribed different procedures for different involuntary commitment statutes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that appellant drug addict's detention, arrest, and subsequent involuntary commitment were constitutional.

 

939 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Shipstead


Crim. No. 8549


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


19 Cal. App. 3d 58; 96 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1259



July 8, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 30, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, Conrad B. Reisch, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of conviction of possession of a restricted dangerous drug in violation of section 11910 of the Health and Safety Code is reversed. The judgment of conviction of violation of section 11911 of the Health and Safety Code is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings under the direction and in conformity with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment entered in the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California) convicting him of possession of a restricted dangerous drug for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11911 and simple possession of such a drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code §1190.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of a restricted dangerous drug for sale and simple possession of such a drug, and he appealed. The court reversed the conviction of simple possession because it was a lesser included offense of possession for sale. If the testimony concerning the informant's declarations was properly before the court, there was probable cause to arrest defendant. There was sufficient evidence to sustain the conviction of possession for sale, and defendant received effective assistance of counsel. However, the court reversed defendant's conviction of possession for sale because the trial court erred in denying his motion for the informant's identity. There was no claim that the informant was reliable, and defendant introduced testimony which indicated that the declarations were obtained by means of illegal eavesdropping, which would have affected the ruling on the motion to suppress. Thus, the court remanded the case with instructions to set aside the conviction of simple possession and to set aside the conviction of possession for sale only if the evidence sought indicated that the information was secured in violation of defendant's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction of simple possession of a restricted dangerous drug because it was a lesser included offense of possession of such a drug for sale. The court also reversed defendant's conviction of possession for sale because the trial court erred in denying his motion for the informant's identity. The case was remanded for further proceedings consistent therewith.

Governing Board v. Brennan


Civ. Nos. 26958, 27201


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


18 Cal. App. 3d 396; 95 Cal. Rptr. 712; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393



June 24, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 23, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 8, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, Nos. 49814, 51339, Samuel W. Gardiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondent school district and denied appellant's writ of mandate. Appellant filed a petition for a writ of mandate, seeking an order compelling respondent to reemploy her for the 1968-1969 school year.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teacher made and executed an affidavit, telling of her long and beneficial use of marijuana to support another person's motion for an arrest of judgment. Respondent school district suspended appellant and then recommended she not be re-employed for the upcoming school year. Appellant contended that the term immoral conduct as applied in this case was unconstitutionally vague. The court affirmed the judgments. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which entered a judgment in favor of respondent school district and denied appellant teacher's writ of mandate. The court found that the term "immoral conduct" was not unconstitutional in itself, and because there was evidence of unfitness to teach, it was not unconstitutional as applied.

Freeman v. Contra Costa County Water Dist.


Civ. No. 28717


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


18 Cal. App. 3d 404; 95 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1394



June 24, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 113193, S. C. Masterson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which sustained a regulation instituted by respondent water district. The regulation required appellants to install and pay for a device used to protect the water supply. Appellants asserted that the regulation unreasonably required them to install and pay for a device to protect the public and violated takings and due process clauses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant residents challenged a judgment that upheld a regulation instituted by respondent water district, which required appellants to install and pay for device used to protect the water supply. Appellants asserted that the regulation unreasonably required them to install and pay for a device to protect the public and violated the takings clause and the due process clause. Each appellant maintained a well for gardening and washing cars. Appellants' wells weren't connected to the public system, but respondent threatened to terminate water service until appellants installed a device. The court held that it was not unreasonable for respondent to require appellant residents to install and pay for a device to protect against potential water contamination and that water pollution prevention was a legitimate governmental objective. The requirement did not infringe on the right of appellants to use their property, as it involved the state's exercise of police power. The requirement is authorized by Cal. Water Code § 31024 and Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 203, 208. There was no due process violation, as there was a hearing prior to adoption of regulation. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that it was not unreasonable for respondent water district to require appellant residents to install and pay for a device to protect against potential water contamination. The court found that the requirement did not infringe on the right of appellants to use their property, as the requirement involved the state's police power. There was no due process violation, as there was a hearing prior to adoption of regulation.

Horack v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 10443


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


18 Cal. App. 3d 363; 95 Cal. Rptr. 717; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1390



June 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 18, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. M-1358, Robert L. Corfman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order granting petition for writ of mandate reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the California Franchise Tax Board, appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), that granted petitioners' request for a writ of mandate directing defendant to return to petitioners certain monies which had come into defendant's possession by reason of the arrest of petitioners on a narcotics offense.


OVERVIEW: Police seized a quantity of drugs and money from petitioners. The search was ultimately held to be without probable cause. Defendant, the California Franchise Tax Board, which was notified of the seizure, concluded that petitioners had income which they failed to report and issued a jeopardy assessment for personal income tax and orders directing the police to turn the money over to it. The police complied. Petitioners filed protests with defendant. Petitioners also filed a petition for writ of mandate in the superior court, which was granted. Upon defendant's, the court reversed because petitioners failed to exhaust their administrative remedies, and they were not relieved of the obligation to pursue administrative remedies by the fact that the money was the product of an illegal search. In addition, Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 15 and Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 19081 both prohibited the use of mandate to enjoin collection of personal income taxes. A suit to recover alleged overpayments was the exclusive means of obtaining judicial review of tax proceedings.


OUTCOME: The order granting the petition for writ of mandate directing defendant, the California Franchise Tax Board, to return petitioners' money, obtained by reason of petitioners' arrest on a narcotics offense, was reversed because petitioners failed to exhaust their administrative remedies, and because the use of mandate to enjoin collection of personal income taxes was prohibited by the California Constitution and by statute.

Fortenberry v. Weber


Civ. No. 26498


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


18 Cal. App. 3d 213; 95 Cal. Rptr. 834; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1377



June 21, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 21, 1971, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 18, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 530481, C. Harold Caulfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the new trial is affirmed.

The appeal from the judgment is dismissed.

Plaintiff to recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant stockbrokers appealed from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) granting plaintiff client's motion for a new trial on her claim to recover damages for mismanagement of a securities account. Plaintiff appealed the judgment in defendants' favor.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff client filed an action to recover damages from defendant stockbrokers based on mismanagement of her securities account. The jury returned a verdict for defendants. The trial court granted plaintiff's motion for a new trial. Defendants appealed the order granting a new trial, and plaintiff cross-appealed the judgment. On appeal, the court determined that the entry of the order granting the new trial was made within the 60-day period as required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 660. Although the trial judge did not abuse his discretion by allowing the amendment to the answer and the instruction on contributory negligence was proper, the order granting a new trial was supported by the trial court's statement that there was insufficient evidence to justify the verdict and its specifications as to where evidence was lacking. In addition, the instruction on the defense of ratification was improper because it failed to state plaintiff had to have been found to have had notice of all the transactions before she could be found to have ratified the activities of defendants. The order granting the new trial was affirmed; the appeal from the judgment was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting plaintiff client's motion for a new trial on her claim that defendant stockbrokers mismanaged her securities account. The trial court's order granting the motion for a new trial was supported by its statement that there was insufficient evidence to justify the verdict and its specifications as to where evidence was lacking. The court dismissed plaintiff's appeal from the judgment.

People v. Chapman


Crim. No. 9131


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


17 Cal. App. 3d 865; 95 Cal. Rptr. 242; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1536



May 26, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 13, 1971. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Fremont-Newark-Union City Judicial District of Alameda County, No. 12662, Roy B. Pucci, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment reversing the municipal court order which denied the motion to suppress certain allegedly obscene books and magazines is reversed. The order of the municipal court directing the return of such obscene material as was not named in the search warrant is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Municipal Court for the Fremont-Newark-Union City Judicial District of Alameda County (California), which denied defendant's motion to suppress allegedly obscene books and magazines, alleging that a prior adversary hearing should have been conducted before a search warrant was issued. Defendant was prosecuted under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2.


OVERVIEW: A search warrant was issued authorizing the search of defendant's bookstore to seize allegedly obscene materials. Defendant was charged with two misdemeanor counts of distributing obscene materials. Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence and for restoration of property seized under the search warrants. The municipal court ordered that the books seized that were not listed in the warrant be returned, and denied the motion to suppress. The appellate department affirmed the order of the municipal court as to books and magazines purchased by the officer from defendant and alleged to be obscene, and reversed the order as to the allegedly obscene books and magazines seized under a search warrant issued ex parte. Defendant contended that a prior adversary hearing should have been conducted before the search warrant was issued for a seizure of allegedly obscene books and magazines, when such publications were available for sale. The court reversed the order that reversed the municipal court order denying the motion to suppress, holding that an adversary hearing was not required prior to issuance of the search warrant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment reversing an order to deny defendant's motion to suppress certain allegedly obscene books and magazines, and the order directing the return of such obscene material not named in the search warrant was vacated. An adversary hearing was not required prior to issuance of a search warrant, where seized items were available for sale.

People v. McFarland


Crim. No. 8097


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


17 Cal. App. 3d 807; 95 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1530



May 24, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 73032, Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and the juvenile court's transfer order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a jury verdict finding him guilty of murder from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), and the prior proceedings transferring him from the juvenile court, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, on the basis that the transfer caused jeopardy to attach and that evidence of a witness's testimony was improper and prejudicial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of murder. Appellant challenged his conviction and the prior proceedings transferring him from the juvenile court, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, on the basis that the transfer caused jeopardy to attach and that evidence of a witness's testimony was improper and prejudicial. The court affirmed. The court held that appellant had previously been a ward of the juvenile court and had spent time at juvenile hall on a number of serious charges. The court held that the seriousness of the offense after a period of apparent rehabilitation, appellant's delinquent behavior pattern, and his apparent failure to respond to the programs offered by two stays at juvenile hall, reasonably supported the conclusion that appellant was not a promising candidate for further commitment under the juvenile court law. Additionally, the court held that sufficient evidence connected appellant with the crime to reasonably satisfy the jury that his coconspirator was telling the truth about appellant's commission of the murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because appellant's pattern of delinquent behavior and his failure to respond to the programs offered by two stays at juvenile hall supported the conclusion that appellant was not a promising candidate for further commitment under the juvenile court law. Evidence connecting appellant with the crime was sufficient to show that his coconspirator's testimony was truthful.

Mitcheltree v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 36491


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


17 Cal. App. 3d 791; 95 Cal. Rptr. 76; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1528



May 21, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 18, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 995 921, Harold F. Collins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted a writ of mandate to respondent landowners in their action to set aside the city planning commission's grant of a conditional land use permit under Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 12.24.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landowners sought a writ of mandate from the trial court to set aside a conditional use permit granted to appellant city by the planning commission which authorized the construction of airplane hangars on residentially zoned land. The trial court granted the writ and ordered the permit to be set aside. The court reversed, finding that although appellant had not yet adopted a land use plan as contemplated in Los Angeles Municipal ordinance § 12.24-B1, the planning commission did not exceed its legislative authority in making its decision. The court held that a conditional use permit could be granted if it was essential or desirable to the public convenience or welfare under the general welfare standard of an ordinance. Such standards were generally upheld. Further, such general welfare standards for determining an application for a conditional use permit were sufficiently specific to provide constitutionally adequate guidelines and to support the planning commission's decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the writ of mandate that had set aside a conditional use permit obtained by appellant city. The court held that the municipal code provided a sufficient standard on which the city planning commission could act.

People v. Martin


Crim. No. 5406


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


17 Cal. App. 3d 661; 95 Cal. Rptr. 250; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1513



May 19, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 37150, Leonard J. Dieden, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The sentencing court is directed to exercise its discretion with respect to the effect of the prior conviction, pursuant to People v. Tenorio, supra, and the judgment is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted appellant of possession of heroin and possession of heroin for sale. Appellant alleged errors in the admission of a co-defendant's extrajudicial confession, in the failure of the trial court to sever appellant's trial from that of his co-defendant, and in his conviction for both possession and possession for sale.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession of heroin and possession of heroin for sale. Appellant challenged his conviction on a variety of issues. The court found that it was not error to allow expert opinion testimony merely because the testimony coincided with an ultimate issue of fact and that it was not error to admit evidence of appellant's financial condition at the time of the alleged offense. The court determined that admission of a co-defendant's extrajudicial confession was not prejudicial error when there was no reasonable possibility that a result more favorable to appellant would have been reached absent the error. Similarly, the court determined that failure to sever appellant's trial from that of the co-defendant was not prejudicial when there was no reasonable probability that appellant would have obtained a different verdict at a separate trial. The court also decided that, under the circumstances, appellant had engaged in two separate acts, which supported appellant's conviction for both possession and possession for sale. The court affirmed but ordered the trial court to exercise its discretion with respect to the sentencing effect of a prior conviction.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction was affirmed, but the trial court was directed to exercise its discretion with respect to the sentencing effect of a prior conviction. There was no error in admitting the testimony of a police officer as an expect on an ultimate issue of fact, the trial court was not compelled to grant severance on its own motion, and appellant was properly convicted, under the circumstances, of both possession and possession for sale.

 

948 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Martin v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 29026


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


17 Cal. App. 3d 412; 95 Cal. Rptr. 110; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1489



May 10, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 7, 1971.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a peremptory writ of prohibition is denied; the alternative writ is discharged. The "Interim [Orders] re Contempt" in Martin v. Martin, superior court No. 203840, filed September 11, 1970, and Kerley v. Davis, superior court No. 165314, filed September 21, 1970, are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which held them in contempt for noncompliance with child support orders under the prima facie standards of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209.5.


OVERVIEW: The trial court, in separate proceedings, held petitioner parents in contempt under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209.5 for failing to comply with support orders. The court denied petitioners' request for a peremptory writ of prohibition, discharged the alternative writ, and affirmed the contempt orders. The court found that § 1209.5 was constitutional because it could have reasonably been said that the presumed facts under that section, i.e., an ability and willful failure to pay support, was more likely than not to flow from the proof that the trial court made a support order, thus finding an ability to pay at the time of the orders, and that petitioners knew of the orders. The court also determined that it seemed reasonable to infer that it was more likely than not that such an ability would continue. The court additionally found that the above elements established § 1209.5's prima facie evidence of contempt. The court also held that § 1209.5 did not violate U.S. Const. amend. V because it only fixed the quantum of evidence that, until overcome, established petitioners' guilt, and that it did not compel them to be witnesses against themselves.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner parents' requested peremptory writ of prohibition, discharged an alternative writ, and affirmed orders under a constitutional law that not complying with its terms prima facially proved contempt. The court held that it was reasonable to state that willfully not paying support was more likely than not to flow from an ability to comply with orders that petitioners knew of when they were issued.

 

949 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Gray v. Whitmore


Civ. Nos. 27565, 28540, 28319


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


17 Cal. App. 3d 1; 94 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1459



April 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of the appellants in Nos. 27565 and 28540 and petitioner in No. 28319 for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, Edmund Scott, Judge. Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in Gray v. Whitmore (1 Civ. 27565) is affirmed.

The judgment in Dionio v. Carberry (1 Civ. 28540) is affirmed.

In Bryson v. Carberry (1 Civ. 28319) the alternative writ of mandate directed to the sheriff is discharged and the petition is denied. With respect to Moalem, petitioner is not entitled to the complete relief sought, but to relief limited in its scope. Petitioner is entitled to a writ of mandate directing Moalem to: (1) refrain from demanding or requiring, as a condition of the redemption provided for in section 1174, that petitioner pay the judgment in Moalem's favor obtained in Moalem v. Bryson, No. 641169, in the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco; and (2) refrain from applying in payment of said judgment any proceeds remaining from the money realized from the sale, if any, of petitioner's personal property to [***2] satisfy Moalem's costs in storing and selling such property. Petitioner is not entitled to a writ of mandate under the rationale of this opinion with respect to the other grounds for relief. As to these grounds, the petition is denied and the alternative writ is discharged. Let the writ issue accordingly. Petitioner shall not recover any of her costs from Moalem and Moalem shall not recover any of his costs from petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases petitioner evicted tenants challenged orders from the Superior Court of San Mateo County and Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied them preliminary injunctions and peremptory writs of mandate to compel respondent sheriffs to accept their claims of exemption for property held by real party in interest landlord, claiming that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner evicted tenants sought to compel respondent sheriffs to accept their claims of exception, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690, for personal property seized and delivered to real party in interest landlord for safekeeping pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174. The court denied the writs sought and found that respondent was not in possession or control of the property and had acted lawfully. The court held that when the property was taken under § 1174, a bailment was created for the benefit of petitioners. The court held that the landlord had a duty to store the property for at least 30 days unless it was sooner redeemed by petitioner. The court found that the landlord could later sell the property to satisfy storage costs only. The court found that the part of § 1174 that allowed the landlord to satisfy any money judgment rendered in a forcible detainer action violated substantive due process. The court held that the class actions were proper, because evicted tenants whose personal property had been stored pursuant to writs of possession and who claimed the property was exempt under § 690 constituted an ascertainable classification.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the portion of the statute which required petitioner evicted tenants to satisfy a money judgment before redemption of personal property, or which subjected the proceeds of the sale of that property to the satisfaction of the judgment, violated substantive due process. The court denied the writs of mandate against respondent sheriffs, but granted a writ of mandate against real party in interest landlord.

 

950 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mittelman v. Seifert


Civ. No. 26494


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


17 Cal. App. 3d 51; 94 Cal. Rptr. 654; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1462



April 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition for a rehearing was denied May 21, 1971. Taylor, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 23, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Marshall S. Hall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, with directions to the trial court to enter its minute order establishing defendant's liability, and thereafter, to proceed to try the remaining issue of damages, instructing the jury accordingly. ( Lauderdale v. U & I Equip. Co. (1969) 271 Cal.App.2d 140, 143 [76 Cal.Rptr. 483].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor surviving children challenged the verdict returned in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that denied their recovery in a wrongful death action against respondent, administratrix of her pilot husband's estate. Appellants claimed that the deceased pilot's misconduct was wilful, and that at the time of their deaths their parents were passengers, not guests, in the aircraft, as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant surviving children alleged wilful conduct in a wrongful death suit after their parents were killed in an airplane that crashed while piloted by their attorney, the husband of respondent administratrix. The court ruled that Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 21406 that prohibited a non-paying guest in any aircraft from any action for damages against a pilot, was not unconstitutional for want of due process and equal protection, and was not in conflict with 14 C.F.R. § 91.9 that forbade careless and reckless operation of aircraft. The court found that appellants' parents were passengers, not guests, at the time of the crash because of the expectation of future remuneration created by the attorney-client relationship, and that death proximately resulting from the pilot's intoxication removed the guest statute limitation. The court found that under the aviation guest statute, the pilot's violations of statutes and regulations constituted wilful misconduct as a matter of law. The court reversed the judgment and held that respondent had not rebutted the inference of negligence arising under res ipsa loquitur nor the presumption of negligence arising from flight regulation violations.


OUTCOME: The court found that the trial court erred when it denied appellant surviving children's motion for a directed verdict on the issue of liability in their wrongful death action against respondent, administratrix of her pilot husband's estate. The court reversed the judgment and ordered the trial court to enter its minute order establishing defendant's liability as a matter of law, and to try the remaining issue of damages accordingly.

 

951 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Redmond


Crim. No. 17639


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


16 Cal. App. 3d 931; 94 Cal. Rptr. 543; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1652



April 21, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-229198, Charles H. Church, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of commitment is reversed and defendant is ordered remanded to the superior court, with directions to that court to set aside the verdict finding defendant insane at the time he committed the offense and to reconsider defendant's motion to withdraw his insanity plea in light of the views set forth above in this opinion; attempted appeal from nonexistent judgments is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of simple assault and ordered him committed to a state hospital upon a finding that he was insane at the time he committed the offense, after rejecting defendant's motion to withdraw his insanity plea.


OVERVIEW: The trial court rejected defendant's motion to withdraw his plea of not guilty by reason of insanity and after a finding by a jury that defendant was insane at the time he committed the offense, he was ordered committed to a state hospital. The court reversed and remanded the order that committed defendant with directions to the trial court to set aside the verdict finding that he was insane when he committed the offense and to reconsider his motion to withdraw his insanity plea. The court held that the trial court should have determined that defendant was sane when he sought to withdraw his insanity plea and was capable of understanding the nature of the proceedings against him, and he was able to assist his attorney in the conduct of his defense. If he was found to have been presently sane, then a series of questions should have been propounded to defendant and his counsel and answers made part of the record. If the court found that defendant was sane, then he should have been permitted to withdraw his plea. If the trial court felt that defendant was a danger to society, it should have instituted civil involuntary commitment under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order of commitment with directions to the trial court to set aside the verdict finding defendant insane at the time he committed the offense and to reconsider defendant's motion to withdraw his insanity plea because he was presently sane, and should have been allowed to withdraw his plea of insanity. If he was a danger to society, the trial court should have instituted an involuntary civil commitment.

 

952 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Western Mobilehome Assn. v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 10385


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


16 Cal. App. 3d 941; 94 Cal. Rptr. 504; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1653



April 21, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 4, 1971, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, William H. Macomber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to enter judgment in favor of County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants county and its officials appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs mobilehome association and its members and declared that the ordinance passed by defendants attempted to impose additional requirements in an area preempted by the Mobilehomes Park Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 18200- 18700.


OVERVIEW: Defendants county and its officials enacted a county ordinance that established operating permit fees for mobilehome parks. San Diego County, Cal. Code § 56.107.Plaintiffs mobilehome association and its members filed a complaint that sought declaratory relief to enjoin enforcement of § 56.107. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs based on the Mobilehome Parks Act, Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 18200- 18700, preempting defendant from enforcing the county ordinance. The court reversed the summary judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs, mobilehome association and its members with directions to the trial court to enter judgment in favor of defendants county and its officials because Cal. Health & Safety Code § 510 empowered defendant to enact local regulation to determine its own costs of enforcement and to fix fees. The court held that although the state had assumed original jurisdiction under § 510 in the matter of regulating mobilehomes and mobilehome parks, it permitted local governments at their option to undertake the full duties of enforcement, to collect operating permit fees, to issue such permits and to control them under the power to suspend them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs mobilehome association and its members with directions to enter judgment in favor of defendants county and its officials because the state statute empowered defendants to enact local regulation to enforce, to collect operating permit fees, and to issue such permits for mobilehome parks.

 

953 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carnesi


Crim. No. 18863


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


16 Cal. App. 3d 863; 94 Cal. Rptr. 555; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1645



April 20, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-163483, Earl C. Broady, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant probation following his conviction for possession of a restricted dangerous drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910.


OVERVIEW: Three juveniles, including defendant, were arrested after police noticed the three drinking from alcoholic beverage containers in a public place in violation of Los Angeles Municipal Code § 41.27(c). Defendant was searched, and four capsules were removed from defendant's pocket. The trial court convicted defendant of possession of a restricted dangerous drug in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910. Defendant alleged that the beer can and its contents were never produced, thus there was no proof that it contained the requisite amount of alcohol, and that the state proved neither the capsule's chemical nature nor defendant's knowledge of the capsule's chemical nature. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the officers reasonably assumed that the can labelled as a well-known brand of beer contained beer and that its alcohol content was that of ordinary beer. Though there was no testimony concerning the chemical nature of the capsules, there was effectively a stipulation that the capsules examined were secobarbital. The court also found that defendant's knowledge of the chemical nature of the compound was not an element of the § 11910 offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and probation, finding that that the officers reasonably assumed that the defendant's beer can contained ordinary beer, that the chemical nature of the capsules found was effectively stipulated, and defendant's knowledge of the chemical nature of the capsules was not an element of the offense.

 

954 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gibbs


Crim. No. 17204


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


16 Cal. App. 3d 758; 94 Cal. Rptr. 458; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1635



April 19, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Max F. Deutz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of possession of marijuana for sale after his motion to suppress evidence was denied and he entered a plea of guilty to the offense. Defendant thereafter sought review of the trial court's order denying the motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to possession of marijuana for sale after the trial court denied his motion to suppress evidence of narcotics found in his car by a police officer after a traffic stop. On appeal, defendant challenged the legality of the officer's initial observation of a marijuana cigarette on the front seat of the car, claiming that the officer did not have probable cause to stop his car. Affirming the denial of the motion, the court ruled (1) that the officer had probable cause to justify stopping the car for excessive exhaust smoke under Cal. Veh. Code § 27153 whether or not § 27153 was constitutionally infirm, (2) that § 27153 was constitutional, and (3) that the officer's entry into the car for the purpose of looking for the registration slip and to demonstrate the fact that defendant's car was emitting excessive smoke did not violate defendant's rights under the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The court concluded that the marijuana cigarette was in plain view after the officer entered the car and that the search and seizure triggered by the observation was also lawful.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence.

 

955 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Young v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 12688


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


16 Cal. App. 3d 766; 94 Cal. Rptr. 331; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1636



April 19, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1971. Burke, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded with directions for the issuance of a writ of prohibition as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of prohibition to bar his prosecution under Sacramento, Ca., Mun. Ord. 409 on the ground that the ordinance was unconstitutionally overbroad.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, while selling periodicals at a busy intersection, was arrested for violating Sacramento, Ca., Ordinance No. 409, which prohibited peddling or hawking goods "along or upon" any public road or highway in the unincorporated area of the County of Sacramento. Defendant contended that the ordinance was unconstitutionally overbroad under U.S. Const. amend. I, as applied to the states by U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Therefore, defendant sought a writ of prohibition to foreclose his prosecution. After the superior court denied the writ, the appellate court reversed. Historically, streets, sidewalks, and parks have been associated with exercise of the rights of communication protected by U.S. Const.,amend. I; access to those fora for the purpose of exercising those rights could not be denied broadly and absolutely. Because the ordinance had a restrictive effect on the protected activity of distribution of printed matter "upon or along" the public streets, the ordinance had to be narrowly drawn to achieve a legitimate government end. The ordinance was overbroad, as it proscribed both activities which threatened public safety by impacting traffic flow and those which did not.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied defendant's petition for a writ of prohibition and remanded with directions that the writ issue because the municipal ordinance which defendant allegedly violated was unconstitutionally overbroad.

 

956 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Stevens v. Watson


Civ. No. 36878


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


16 Cal. App. 3d 629; 94 Cal. Rptr. 190; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1619



April 13, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 9, 1971. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained respondent county assessor's demurrer to appellant's petition for mandate and declaratory relief against respondent. Appellant's action challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 2.6 relating to property taxation of non-profit golf courses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers petitioned for mandate and declaratory relief against respondent assessor, challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 2.6 relating to property taxation of non-profit golf courses. The lower court sustained respondent's demurrer and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the judgment and held that § 2.6 conformed to the requirements of equal protection because a number of conceivable states of fact supported its provisions. The court concluded that the language of the § 2.6 was not vague or uncertain, and the standards for property tax assessment were sufficiently definite. The operation of the golf courses was not in any sense connected with a government function thus California could not be charged with any responsibility for the private conduct of the owners of the golf courses. The fact that it may have incidentally benefitted someone who practiced discrimination was immaterial, as § 2.6 was in furtherance of a proper public purpose. If the legislation proved unwise, then appellant's remedy lied with the electorate at the polls, not with the courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sustaining respondent county assessor's demurrer to appellant taxpayers' petition for mandate and declaratory relief against respondent. The court concluded that preferential tax treatment for certain nonprofit golf courses conformed to the requirements of equal protection, was not too vague, not connected to a government function, and that California could not be charged with the conduct of the private owners.

 

957 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


Crim. No. 8869


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


17 Cal. App. 3d 388; 95 Cal. Rptr. 80; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1487



April 12, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, Conrad B. Reisch and Allison M. Rouse, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied his motion requesting commitment to a narcotic rehabilitation program under the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051 after he pled guilty to possession of heroin.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's motion requesting commitment to a narcotic rehabilitation program under the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051 and sentenced him to state prison for possession of heroin with an admitted prior conviction of the same offense and a second prior conviction for robbery. On appeal defendant contended that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3052 which precluded drug addicts convicted of certain crimes from rehabilitation programs was unconstitutional. The court held that the legislature could reasonably exclude addicts with a history of violent crimes. The court disagreed with defendant's contention that a provision of § 3052 that permitted district attorney to investigate facts relevant to commitment was unconstitutional as it did not curb judicial power. The court held that rehabilitation center director did not abuse his discretion when he testified that defendant was unfit for commitment as the record established that defendant was prima facie ineligible for the program based on continued addiction and criminal activity and because he violated parole during prior commitments in the program. Accordingly the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because defendant's record of criminality together with his demonstrated inability to profit from the narcotic rehabilitation program amply supported the trial court's ruling denying defendant's motion for commitment.

 

958 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Newton v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 28883


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


16 Cal. App. 3d 499; 94 Cal. Rptr. 120; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1605



April 1, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied; the alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his motion to substitute State of California bonds in lieu of cash on deposit with the court as his bail in an underlying case in which defendant was charged with manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with manslaughter and following his arrest, his bail was fixed at $ 50,000.00. Defendant posted that amount and thereafter filed a motion for an order substituting State of California bonds in lieu of cash on deposit with the court as his bail. The bonds defendant sought to substitute had not reached maturity and thus only had a face value equal to the amount of defendant's bond. The trial court denied his motion and he appealed, contending that under Cal. Penal Code § 1298, such a substitution was allowed. The court affirmed, holding § 1298 could be construed as providing that bonds of the face value of the cash deposit required could be deposited and treated in the same manner as a deposit of money, when their actual value was commensurate with a proper amount of cash bail, however, the statute did not allow defendant to post state bonds whose face value was less than the amount of his bail. The court denied defendant's petition for a writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that defendant could not substitute state bonds in lieu of cash on deposit with the court as his bail because the face value of the bonds did not equal the amount fixed as his bond.

Ames v. Hermosa Beach


Civ. No. 34817


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


16 Cal. App. 3d 146; 93 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1571



March 19, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, John A. Shidler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly the order dismissing the action is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views herein expressed; the order denying preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in which respondent city's single general demurrer was sustained and the action dismissed and preliminary injunction denied in an action seeking to declare certain loitering and housing laws of the City of Hermosa Beach unconstitutional on their face and therefore void.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer brought action pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526(a) to enjoin respondents, city and others, from expending public funds and from using public property to administer or enforce certain provisions of its Municipal Code, Hermosa Beach, CA, §§ 20-10, 20-11, relating to loitering, and § 18-14, regulating the rental of housing accommodations to minors. Although the complaint set forth two causes of action, respondents filed a single demurrer to the entire complaint which was granted and the action dismissed and order for a preliminary injunction was denied. The court affirmed the denial of the preliminary injunction and reversed the order dismissing the action and remanded. The court held that the loitering ordinance was overbroad and therefore void on its face. The housing ordinance was valid; it was not improper to classify minors or juveniles differently from adults. A taxpayer could file suit and seek an injunction where government funds were employed so as to deprive others of constitutionally secured rights or to enforce laws which were themselves unconstitutional and therefore void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the action and remanded the cause because the general demurrer was to the entire complaint. The loitering ordinance was overbroad and facially void. The housing ordinance was not unconstitutional and the complaint challenging this ordinance stated a valid cause of action. The denial of the preliminary injunction was affirmed.

Antonello v. San Diego


Civ. No. 10200


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


16 Cal. App. 3d 161; 93 Cal. Rptr. 820; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1573



March 19, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 13, 1971. The United States Supreme Court denied a writ of certiorari on October 26, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Eli H. Levenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business owners sought review from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), in which a city ordinance regulating the construction and operation of peep show establishments enacted by defendant city was declared valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, business owners, operated arcades in the City of San Diego, known as peep show establishments, exhibiting motion pictures observed in a viewing booth, through a small aperture, upon deposit of a coin. Defendant City Council of San Diego, enacted a ordinance which prescribed standards of construction, maintenance and operation of peep show places of business. Plaintiffs challenged the validity of the ordinance alleging it was unconstitutional and the real purpose was to close down their businesses. The court upheld the validity of the statute holding that a court may not examine the motives influencing legislators in voting on the passage of a statute and that municipalities could impose regulations upon the construction and maintenance of business premises reasonably necessary to protect the health, safety, and general welfare of patrons of the business and members of the public.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the ordinance regulating the construction and operation of peep show establishments because the classification in question was not unreasonable, arbitrary or discriminatory and the object of the statute was one for which police power could legitimately be invoked and the statute had a reasonable and substantial relation to the object sought to be attained, health and safety of patrons and the public.

Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought review from the Sacramento County Superior Court (California), in which it granted a post-administrative discovery hearing to real party in interest in a action against petitioner requiring questions to be answered. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent court from enforcing its order.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners are doctors employed by the state of California and sought to remove respondent seller's prescription drugs from petitioner state's formulary alleging the prices respondent charged for the drugs were discriminatory. At the administrative hearing the director found that respondent's marketing practices violated state law and declared that its prescription drug products were ineligible for purchase under the medical assistance program. Respondent filed a second complaint and the court trial court issued a order staying the effective date of the administrative decision. Respondent then gave notice of taking depositions of petitioner doctors who refused to answer discovery questions on advice of counsel. Respondent then sought a order granting him discovery against petitioners and requiring them to answer certain questions which was granted by respondent court. The court let a writ issue restraining respondent court from enforcing its order holding that a trial court could admit relevant evidence not adduce before a administrative hearing only where such evidence could not have been produced there in the exercise of reasonable diligence.


OUTCOME: The court let a writ issue restraining respondent court from enforcing its order and directing petitioners to answer questions because respondent real party did not show that evidence sought by discovery was improperly excluded or could not have been produced at the administrative hearing.

Wong v. Regents of University of California


Civ. No. 27857


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


15 Cal. App. 3d 823; 93 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 950



March 5, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 29, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Henry R. Rolph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The superior court will overrule the general demurrer and thereafter take proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant medical student challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate by which he sought to compel respondent university regents to reinstate him as a fourth year medical student and sustained respondent's demurrer without leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought by petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent university regents to reinstate him as a fourth year medical student. Respondent filed a general demurrer and answer alleging that appellant was scholastically deficient and was dismissed upon his own request. The trial court entered a decision in favor of respondent denying the peremptory writ based upon findings of fact and conclusions of law. Thereafter the trial court granted appellant's motion for reconsideration and set aside its prior decision. At the subsequent hearing the court denied the petition for writ of mandate and sustained respondent's demurrer. The court held that appellant's allegations reasonably evidenced that respondent acted arbitrarily and capriciously in his dismissal, and that he exhausted his administrative remedies. The court held therefore that the petition for mandate stated a cause of action, and as such the trial court's judgment dismissing the mandate proceedings upon sustaining respondent's general demurrer was error. Accordingly the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying appellant's writ of mandate was reversed because his allegations reasonably established that respondent's actions were arbitrary and capricious and that he exhausted his administrative remedies. As such the trial court erroneously dismissed mandate proceedings upon sustaining respondent's general demurrer.

Cristmat, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 37107


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


15 Cal. App. 3d 590; 93 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 926



February 24, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 11, 1971, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 22, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff cocktail lounge sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which denied its request for a preliminary injunction preventing defendant county from enforcing its ordinance prohibiting the possession or sale of alcoholic beverages in model studios, and which granted defendant's motion for a judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff cocktail lounge was licensed by the state to serve alcohol and was licensed by defendant county to operate a model studio on the same premises. Defendant issued citations and otherwise enforced its ordinance forbidding the use of alcohol at a model studio. Plaintiff filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief, claiming that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The trial court denied plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction and granted defendant's motion for a judgment on the pleadings. The court held that the ordinance did not violate Cal. Const. art. XX, § 22 because it regulated model studios, not the sale and possession of alcohol generally. The court found that the state had not impliedly preempted the field of regulation of alcoholic beverages. The court held that the ordinance did not violate equal protection of the laws because there was a rational basis for it. The court found that the regulation of this commercial activity did not violate any rights of freedom of expression. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of plaintiff cocktail lounge's motion for a preliminary injunction and grant of defendant county's motion for a judgment on the pleadings because the ordinance was a valid exercise of defendant's police power.

People v. Harris


Crim. No. 18116


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


15 Cal. App. 3d 498; 93 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1725



February 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 22, 1971.
