DISPOSITION: The purported appeal from sundry orders is dismissed; the judgment is modified by deleting all of the reference therein to alleged prior convictions and substituting in place thereof the following: "one prior conviction having been found true, as alleged, to wit: Violation of section 11502, Health & Safety Code, a felony, Superior Court of the State of California, Los Angeles County, November 28, 1958"; as so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction of heroin possession, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500, arguing that it was error for the lower court to have denied him the right to represent himself, that the evidence against him was illegally obtained, and that the lower court did not give the jury the instruction proposed by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review from his conviction of possession of heroin, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500, contending that the lower court erroneously denied defendant the right to self-representation, that the evidence against him was unlawfully obtained and thus his motion to suppress should have been granted, and that the lower court erred in failing to give a requested jury instruction. On appeal, the conviction was affirmed. In support of its ruling, the court held that it could not say, based upon the record, that the lower court abused its discretion in failing to grant defendant's right to represent himself as the evidence showed that defendant was not sufficiently competent. The court further held that the evidence obtained against defendant was lawfully obtained as officers were entitled to stop and question defendant, and because police had not illegally compelled defendant to disclose the evidence. The court also found that the jury instruction that was given by the lower court was proper in that it included much of the contents contained in defendant's proposed instruction.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed, as the lower court did not abuse its discretion in failing to permit defendant to represent himself, the police were entitled to stop and question defendant, and because the instruction given to the jury contained much of what defendant requested.

 

965 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


National General Corp. v. Dutch Inns of America, Inc.


Civ. No. 36941


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


15 Cal. App. 3d 490; 93 Cal. Rptr. 343; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 916



February 23, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 22, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Max F. Deutz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant borrower challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied its motions to discharged the attachment on its interest in real property, arguing that attachment of its property was not permitted under California law and that, if such attachment was authorized, it violated its rights as a non-resident to equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff lender brought suit against defendant borrower to rescind the parties' loan agreement and to restore funds. Plaintiff simultaneously filed a declaration for attachment against a non-resident in accordance with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537. The trial court issued a writ of attachment and directed the county sheriff to attach defendant's interest in real property. Defendant moved to discharge the attachment and appealed the order denying its motion, arguing that California law did not permit the attachment of a non-resident's property in an action upon a secured contract and that, if the law did permit such a result, it was invalid because it denied equal protection of the laws to a non-resident. In affirming the order issuing the writ, the appellate court concluded that an attachment would lie in all actions upon a contract when the defendant was not a resident of the state. Further, there was no equal protection violation where the remedy bore a rational relationship to the objective of Cal. Civ. Code § 537 by giving the courts power to render a personal judgment that could be satisfied out of the property attached.


OUTCOME: The order denying defendant borrower's motion to discharge the attachment was affirmed, as California law permitted the attachment of in-state property in actions based upon a contract where the defendant was not a resident of the state. Further, there was no equal protection violation where a rational basis existed for the different treatment of residents and non-residents in the attachment of property.

 

966 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Bartlett


Crim. No. 4327


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


15 Cal. App. 3d 176; 93 Cal. Rptr. 96; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 885



February 11, 1971 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The writ of habeas corpus is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to release him from prison after the 10 year maximum sentence for a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500 was served.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was sentenced for a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11500. A year earlier, petitioner was convicted on a misdemeanor narcotics charge, a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11721. The records pertaining to the earlier conviction were destroyed. After serving more than 10 years, petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus for release from prison. At the time of petitioner's second conviction, part of § 11721 was found unconstitutional. The court found that petitioner's misdemeanor conviction could be used to aggravate a sentence where the complaint charged both use of narcotics and addiction. Copies of records of the prosecutor indicated petitioner was charged with both use of narcotics and addition, and it was presumed petitioner pled guilty to both charges. The court granted petitioner's request for a writ of habeas corpus because there was no record evidencing petitioner knowingly and intelligently waived his right to counsel at the time of earlier conviction. Because there was no waiver of counsel, the earlier misdemeanor conviction could not have been used to enhance second sentence, and the maximum sentence petitioner was required to serve was 10 years.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner's request for habeas corpus relief because petitioner did not make a knowing and intelligent waiver of his right to counsel in a prior narcotics charge. Thus, the prior conviction should not have been used to impose an enhanced sentence, and petitioner had already served the maximum sentence for the crime he was convicted of.

 

967 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Watson


Crim. No. 16380


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


15 Cal. App. 3d 28; 92 Cal. Rptr. 860; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 871



February 4, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 3, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 1, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, William B. Keene, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeals from "orders" and "the order after judgment imposing a fine and penalty assessment" are dismissed. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant former city harbor commissioner appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which convicted him of conflict of interest under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 1090 and 1097, imposed a fine, and forever barred him from holding office in California.


OVERVIEW: While in office, defendant former city harbor commissioner voted to approve a lease of city harbor facilities to an enterprise which he helped finance. A jury found him guilty of conflict of interest under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 1090 and 1097. The trial court imposed a fine and forever barred defendant from holding office in California. On defendant's appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. Rejecting defendant's constitutional challenge, the court held that § 1090's prohibition against conflict of interest was explicitly restricted to financial interest and was therefore neither vague, uncertain, nor indefinite. There was sufficient evidence, the court found, to support a finding that defendant possessed a creditor-debtor relationship with the enterprise when he voted to approve its lease. In the absence of a timely application by defendant to modify or vacate a presumptively valid gag order and in the absence of any specific showing that defendant was prejudiced from a denial of that application, the court held that defendant was precluded from challenging the validity of the gag order on appeal.


OUTCOME: Upholding the constitutionality of California's conflict-of-interest statute and finding substantial evidence to support defendant former city harbor commissioner's conviction thereunder, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

968 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Mannino


Crim. No. 9005


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


14 Cal. App. 3d 953; 92 Cal. Rptr. 880; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1044; 45 A.L.R.3d 996



February 1, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 1, 1971. Elkington, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 31, 1971. Wright, C. J., McComb, J., and Burke, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The writ is granted and the Superior Court of San Mateo County is directed to modify its order granting probation in accordance with the views set forth above. In all other respects the order to show cause is discharged and the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief from conditions of probation ordered by the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.1. Petitioner claimed that certain of the conditions were unreasonable infringements on his rights under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: During an unauthorized demonstration on a college campus, petitioner kicked another man in the face. A jury found him guilty of assault with force likely to produce great bodily harm. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.1, the sentencing court granted five years' probation on certain conditions. One condition prohibited petitioner from becoming a member, either actively or passively, of any political or other organization that participated in or advocated any form of protest or change in existing conditions. Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief, claiming that this and other conditions were unreasonable and violated his rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court concluded that this condition was an overly broad infringement on petitioner's freedom to associate. The court upheld the validity of other conditions, however, such as a prohibition against petitioner's active participation in demonstrations, finding them reasonably related and sufficiently narrowly drawn to serve the state's interest in providing for petitioner's reform and rehabilitation. Accordingly, the court granted the writ of habeas corpus to the extent the sentencing court was required to revise its order.


OUTCOME: The court granted a writ of habeas corpus and directed the sentencing court to modify its probation order to the extent that certain of the conditions of probation were held to be invalid.

 

969 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Villegas


Crim. No. 18776, Civ. No. 37280


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


14 Cal. App. 3d 700; 92 Cal. Rptr. 663; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1027



January 26, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Francis J. Garvey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is reversed. The alternative writ of mandate is discharged. The peremptory writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of California challenged the Superior Court of Los Angeles County's (California) ruling that defendant was entitled to probation for the offense of voluntary manslaughter where a deadly weapon was used, arguing that it was error for the lower court to have failed to make a determination of unusual circumstances and failed to obtain the prosecution's concurrence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant State of California sought review of the lower court's ruling that defendant was entitled to probation for the offense of voluntary manslaughter where a deadly weapon was used, contending that the lower court failed to make a determination as to the existence of unusual circumstances and failed to seek the prosecution's concurrence. On appeal, the determination of probation was reversed and remanded. In support of its ruling, the court held that the order of the lower court in question was appealable as it was unlikely that the legislature intended that there should be a distinction, regarding the appealability of such an order, that depended on whether it was the imposition of the sentence or its execution that was suspended. The court further held that the lower court could not have granted probation without obtaining the prosecution's concurrence and making a requisite finding that defendant's case was unusual.


OUTCOME: The granting of probation was reversed and remanded as the court agreed that the lower court was required to have obtained the prosecution's concurrence and to have made a determination as to unusual circumstances.

 

970 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Carlin v. City of Palm Springs


Civ. No. 9833


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


14 Cal. App. 3d 706; 92 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1028



January 26, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 10, 1971, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 26, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, Richard M. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the owners of a motel, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which ruled that an ordinance of appellee city barring "rate signs" was constitutional and enforceable as applied to appellants' business sign.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city argued that its ordinance barring "rate signs" was necessary to protect the aesthetics of the community and prevent overcommercialization and, thus, the ordinance was properly held enforceable as applied to the business sign of appellant motel owners, whose business name incorporated a rate. The court disagreed, ruling first that a rate sign could not be classified separately from a permitted nonrate sign on the basis of public health or safety. Moreover, the ordinance's separate classification could not be justified on aesthetic reasons, as there was no distinguishing feature between a business sign classified as a rate sign and a business sign characterized as a nonrate sign. That the ordinance permitted rates to be posted in the interior of a facility was also insufficient to uphold the ordinance, as such posting would force a prospective customer into the motel and discourage his departure if the rates appeared high. Nor did the potential for deceptive advertising justify the ordinance, as misrepresentation of prices was never alleged. And economics was no justification, as customers understood that luxury hotels offering more services would be more expensive.


OUTCOME: Judgment ruling that ordinance of appellee city barring outside "rate signs" was constitutional and enforceable as applied to appellant motel owners' business sign reversed, because separate classification between rate signs and permitted nonrate signs was not justified on the basis of health, safety, aesthetics, the potential for deceptive or fraudulent advertising, or on economic grounds.

 

971 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lynch


Crim. No. 8004


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


14 Cal. App. 3d 602; 92 Cal. Rptr. 411; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1022



January 22, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 19, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Redmond C. Staats, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The appeal from the order denying motion for a new trial is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) convicting him of two counts of forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code §261 and one count of kidnapping under Cal. Penal Code §207, and denying his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction of two counts of forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261 and one count of kidnapping under Cal. Penal Code § 207, on the grounds that he was denied a right to a trial by an impartial jury in the light of its racial, economic and educational composition and that the trial court erred in denying his request for a continuance to secure a witness. The trial court denied his motion for a new trial. On appeal, the court affirmed appellant's convictions holding that appellant was convicted by an impartial jury where the record failed to establish an underrepresentation of black persons and it could not be said that an unconstitutional discrimination on racial or economic grounds occurred in selecting jurors from voter registration lists. The court held that the denial of the continuance motion was not an abuse of discretion, because there was no showing of due diligence and no attempt to subpoena a witness until after the close of the prosecution's case. The court held that the jury instruction on the law of kidnapping was adequate, and the failure to instruct the jury on the proper weight of expert witness testimony was not prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for forcible rape and kidnapping. The court found that appellant was convicted by an impartial jury and received effective assistance of counsel. The court held that the trial court properly denied his motion for a continuance and for a new trial, gave proper instruction on the kidnapping law, and that its failure to instruct on the proper weight of expert witness testimony was not prejudicial error.

Simpson v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 12616


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


14 Cal. App. 3d 591; 92 Cal. Rptr. 417; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1021



January 21, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1971. Mosk, J., did not participate therein. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant picketer sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of prohibition restraining his prosecution for a misdemeanor charge of picketing within the state capitol building in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 171f(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellant picketer was charged with picketing within the state capitol building in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 171f(3). Asserting the statute's unconstitutionality, appellant petitioned the trial court for a writ of prohibition restraining his prosecution. Appellant's petition was denied. Appellant challenged the trial court denial of relief, contending that the ban on picketing within the state capitol building violated the freedoms of speech and petition secured to him by the First Amendment and protected against state invasion by U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 9 and 10. The court affirmed the trial court's decision holding that the statute was an adequately narrow regulation designed to protect a substantial state interest. That interest was to protect the state's constituents from the pressure of picketing patrols in the capitol halls because to enforce appellant's freedom of speech in disregard of the rights of others would be harsh and arbitrary in itself. The court held that because the ban was no broader than necessary to meet a legitimate governmental need, the statute did not violate appellant's right to freedom of speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment denying appellant picketer's petition for a writ of prohibition to restrain his prosecution for picketing within the state capitol. The court held that the statute banning picketing within the capitol building was an adequately narrow regulation designed to protect a substantial state interest and did not violate appellant's right to freedom of speech.

People v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 10181


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


14 Cal. App. 3d 362; 92 Cal. Rptr. 248; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 1000



January 14, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, William H. Macomber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) denying its petition for a writ of mandate to direct the municipal court to vacate an order dismissing charges under Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) after defendant was allowed to withdraw a plea bargain incorporating this dismissal.


OVERVIEW: A negotiated plea bargain was entered in the municipal court allowing defendant to plead guilty to possession of obscene materials under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2 while misdemeanor charges of soliciting a lewd act under Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) were dismissed. At the probation hearing, the judge allowed defendant to move to set aside his guilty plea because he concluded that private possession of obscene material was no longer a crime. Appellant people sought review of the order permitting the withdrawal of the guilty plea, but the appeal was dismissed as nonappealable, and the superior court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to direct the municipal court to vacate its order dismissing the soliciting charges. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellant was not entitled to a writ of mandate. The court found that after the municipal court denied appellant's motion to vacate the order of dismissal, appellant should have appealed from the order of denial under Cal. Penal Code § 1466. No appeal was taken from this order; thus, appellant lost its remedy by failing to exercise the right to it. This loss did not require the granting of a writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant people's petition for a writ of mandate to direct the municipal court to vacate an order dismissing charges after defendant was allowed to withdraw a plea bargain incorporating this dismissal because appellant failed to seek the remedy of appeal.

Levy v. Crocker-Citizens Nat. Bank


Civ. No. 36482


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


14 Cal. App. 3d 102; 94 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1971 Cal. App. LEXIS 979



January 4, 1971 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Bayard Rhone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant trustor sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent bank in appellant's suit to terminate two trusts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought to terminate two trusts under which he was the trustor and beneficiary of the net income from both trusts for life. The trial court found appellant lacked the power to terminate the two trusts and entered judgment in favor of respondent, a bank serving as trustee for the two trusts. The terms of the trust permitted appellant to distribute the corpus of the trust under an exercise of general powers of appointment by will, or, in default, the corpus was to go to any surviving lawful issue. The appellate court found, however, that appellant was not the sole beneficiary of the trust because the trust corpus was to go to any lawful surviving issue or distributed by appellant's general power of appointment. Ostensibly, appellant had control over the trust corpus via the general power of appointment, but the appellate court found that he had no power to terminate the trusts. As such, the court affirmed the lower court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment in favor of respondent bank, finding that appellant trustor did not have power to terminate trusts because he was not the sole beneficiary.

 

975 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jackson


Crim. No. 17930; Civ. Nos. 35709, 35693


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


14 Cal. App. 3d 57; 92 Cal. Rptr. 91; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1203



December 31, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied January 28, 1971, and the petitions of respondents Rainey, Allen, Johnson and Harris for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied February 24, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, Floyd C. Dodson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a writ of prohibition by defendant Robert Emory Allen is denied.

The petition for a writ of prohibition by defendant Andrew James Jackson is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) that, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995, dismissed certain felony counts against defendants that were based upon searches of three residences and seizure of stolen property, weapons, and narcotics therefrom. Defendants sought writs of prohibition to prevent their trial on those counts that the trial court refused to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Defendants and several co-defendants were charged with numerous counts of burglary, receiving stolen property, and possession of deadly weapons and drugs. The charges arose after three residences were searched pursuant to search warrants. Defendants were either present when the searches were conducted or occupied the premises. The stolen goods were the result of a series of eight burglaries. The state appealed the trial court's dismissal, under Cal. Penal Code § 995, of certain counts against defendants; defendants sought a writ of prohibition to prevent trial on the counts not dismissed. The court denied defendants' requested relief and reversed the order dismissing the felony counts against defendants. The court ruled that the evidence of each defendant's occupancy of and presence at one of the residences, together with the conduct of the various defendants, was sufficient to warrant the magistrate's conclusion that each defendant was in possession of all of the items found at such location. From that, there was probable cause to believe that each defendant committed the crimes of burglary and receiving stolen property. The searches were reasonable and the evidence admissible.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' petitions for writs of prohibition to prevent trial on felony counts that the trial court refused to dismiss when it dismissed other counts against them and reversed orders dismissing other counts against defendants and several co-defendants. The searches of various residences were reasonable under the Fourth Amendment and provided sufficient evidence to support defendants' prosecutions for the charged crimes.

 

976 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mesaris


Crim. No. 18630


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


14 Cal. App. 3d 71; 91 Cal. Rptr. 837; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1204



December 31, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 24, 1971. Burke, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Alfred J. McCourtney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted him probation after he was found guilty of possession of marijuana for sale, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530.5.


OVERVIEW: A repairman contacted police and advised them that the refrigerator he was repairing contained bags of marijuana. The officers went to defendant's home and examined the bags. As defendant entered the residence, he was placed under arrest. A search of defendant revealed marijuana debris. Defendant was convicted of possession of marijuana for sale and sentenced to probation. Defendant contended that he was subject to an unconstitutional search and seizure. On appeal, the court reversed the order granting probation. In order for the officers to gain access to the residence without a search warrant, they had to have valid consent. The officers did not have valid consent because they entered the house alleging that they needed to speak with the repairmen. The officers only obtained entry by trickery and subterfuge, so the search and seizure was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting defendant probation decision because the evidence used against him was the product of an unconstitutional search and seizure. Police officers did not have valid consent to enter defendant's residence because they obtained entry through subterfuge. Based on such unlawful entry, the subsequent search and seizure was likewise unlawful.

People v. Brown


Crim. No. 8034


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


13 Cal. App. 3d 876; 91 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1296



December 29, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 15, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 24, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile offender sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him of murder in the first degree. Appellant contended that his protection against double jeopardy under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV was violated because he was subjected to both juvenile and criminal court proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile offender was convicted of murder in the first degree after he was found to be not a proper subject for juvenile court treatment under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707. On appeal, appellant contended that his protection against double jeopardy under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV was violated when the juvenile court entertained proceedings on the juvenile court petition, and appellant was thereafter prosecuted as an adult. The court rejected this contention and affirmed the trial court's decision. The court determined that the criminal prosecution did not expose appellant to double jeopardy because the determination of appellant's unfitness under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 was made during the juvenile court jurisdictional hearing. The court determined that appellant's claim of lack of written notice of the fitness hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 was without merit because appellant did not object on that basis below. The court further found that ample evidence supported the order of transfer to adult court and restriction on the impeachment of a witness did not prejudice appellant at his criminal trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant juvenile offender's conviction of murder in the first degree. The court found that the procedure by which appellant was found to be not a proper subject for juvenile court treatment was made during the juvenile court's jurisdictional hearing, therefore the subsequent criminal prosecution did not violate double jeopardy.

People v. Superior Court

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


13 Cal. App. 3d 672; 91 Cal. Rptr. 651; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1278

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of an order from the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which dismissed the indictment against defendant under Cal. Penal Code § 1385.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that the indictment against him was improper because the grand jury was selected in an unconstitutional manner that violated his due process and equal protection rights. The trial court dismissed the indictment. On appeal, the district attorney contended that the trial court was not authorized to dismiss the action under Cal. Penal Code § 1385. The court agreed with the district attorney's contention, finding that the dismissal was not authorized because no reasons were set forth as to why the dismissal was in the furtherance of justice. The court noted that a protracted hearing on defendant's grand jury challenge was not necessary if the motion was based on discrepancies in the grand jury composition because it appeared such a challenge was not a justiciable claim. Accordingly, the court issued a writ of mandate requiring the trial court to vacate its order of dismissal.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the trial court to vacate its order that dismissed the indictment against defendant. The court held that dismissal of the indictment was not authorized by the relevant statute because the trial court did not set forth the reasons why the dismissal was in the furtherance of justice.

Trustees of Cal. State Colleges v. Local 1352, S. F. State Etc. Teachers


Civ. No. 28101


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


13 Cal. App. 3d 863; 92 Cal. Rptr. 134; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1294; 76 L.R.R.M. 2265



December 18, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 11, 1971, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 17, 1971. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, J. Everett Barr, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teachers' union sought review of an order by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment for respondent state college trustees in respondent's action to enjoin a strike and related activities. Appellant claimed that the judgment unlawfully enjoined its right to strike.


OVERVIEW: Respondent state college trustees filed an action against appellant teachers' union to enjoin a strike and related activities at one particular college and were granted summary judgment. On appeal, appellant contended that its right to strike was unlawfully enjoined. The court rejected this contention. The court found that as public employees, appellant did not have the right to strike because there was no statutory grant allowing appellant to do so. In so holding, the court determined that the judgment did not deny appellant equal protection of the laws. The court further reasoned that the judgment did not prohibit a withdrawal of labor in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIII because appellant's members could have quit their employment. The judgment was not unconstitutionally overbroad because the right to picket was subject to limitation where legitimate interests of the state required such action, and the judgment enjoined only picketing in support of an actual strike and at one college itself. Accordingly, the court upheld the grant of summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment for respondent state college trustees in its action against appellant teachers' union to enjoin a strike and related activities. The court found that appellant did not have a right to strike without a statutory grant. The judgment was constitutional in that it did not violate equal protection, it was not a curtailment of appellant's right to withdraw labor, and it was not overbroad.

 

980 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Booloodian v. Ohanesian


Civ. No. 1208


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


13 Cal. App. 3d 635; 91 Cal. Rptr. 923; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1274



December 17, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 13, 1971, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 24, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, George A. Hopper, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtor sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which denied appellant's motion for an order discharging a writ of attachment in respondent creditor's action on a promissory note. Appellant contended that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 542b required the funds held by a sheriff pursuant to a writ of attachment to be released to appellant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent creditor filed an action against appellant debtor on a promissory note and levied a garnishment on payments due to appellant from a non-party garnishee pursuant to a writ of attachment. After judgment for respondent was entered, appellant presented a motion for an order requiring the sheriff to deliver to appellant funds held pursuant to the writ of attachment. The trial court denied the motion. On appeal, appellant contended that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 542b mandated the release the funds to appellant. The court rejected this contention. The court found that the requirement under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 542b for the release of attached funds was suspended because the garnishee filed a petition for bankruptcy. The bankruptcy petition had the effect of tolling Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 542b, therefore the sheriff was under no obligation to return the funds to appellant and correctly delivered the funds to the bankruptcy referee and the garnishee pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 544. Accordingly, the court affirmed the denial of the motion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant debtor's motion for an order discharging a writ of attachment in respondent creditor's action on a promissory note. The court found that the applicable statute requiring return of funds held by the sheriff pursuant to a writ of attachment was tolled because the party upon whom the writ of attachment was executed filed a bankruptcy petition.

 

981 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Patton v. Board of Harbor Commissioners


Civ. No. 35027


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


13 Cal. App. 3d 536; 91 Cal. Rptr. 832; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1265



December 16, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 10, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since the complaint does not state facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action against either Caughlin or the Board, 7 the order of dismissal is affirmed.



7 The reasons stated in the demurrers as to why the complaint did not state a cause of action are not the reasons on which our holding is based. However, since a general demurrer need not specify particulars in which the complaint fails to state facts constituting a cause of action ( Burke v. Maquire, 154 Cal. 456, 462-463 [98 P. 21]), the fact that certain particulars were stated does not limit us to a consideration of them. The demurrer was sustained on the ground that "the Court does not believe that a controversy is asserted between the demurring defendant on the one hand and plaintiff on the other. Plaintiff can get all of the relief he asks for, if he proves to be entitled thereto, against the City of Los Angeles which is a party and has not demurred." Code of Civil Procedure, section 472d provides that the court must include in its decision a statement of its grounds for sustaining the demurrer. However, it is the validity of the lower court's action in sustaining the demurrer which is reviewable and not the court's statement of reasons for its action. ( Apelian v. County of Los Angeles, 266 Cal.App.2d 550, 554 [72 Cal.Rptr. 265].) A failure to state reasons is reversible error only if there is an affirmative showing of prejudice. ( Pactor Corp. v. Manpower Inc., 252 Cal.App.2d 1032, 1034 [60 Cal.Rptr. 878]; Schuetram v. Granada Sanitary Dist., 229 Cal.App.2d 25, 31 [39 Cal.Rptr. 919].) Plaintiff has not been prejudiced in his appeal in the instant case because arguments have been submitted by both sides to this court, not only on the question of whether plaintiff's cause of action extended to these two defendants, but as to whether plaintiff had a cause of action at all.

In light of our ruling we need not discuss the claim that the Board and Caughlin are necessary parties.

We presume parties remaining in the litigation will pursue courses consistent with this opinion.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant Board of Harbor Commissioners and general manager of Harbor Department's demurrer without leave to amend. Plaintiff challenged the constitutionality of Los Angeles, Cal., City Charter § 112(c) because it did not provide for a hearing on plaintiff's suspension from employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee brought an action for declaratory relief against defendant Board of Harbor Commissioners (board), general manager of the Harbor Department (manager), and City of Los Angeles after he was suspended from work for insubordination. Defendants board and manager brought a demurrer that the trial court granted. On appeal, plaintiff contended that Los Angeles, Cal., City Charter § 112(c) violated procedural due process of law as guaranteed by Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it did not provide for a hearing on his suspension. The court rejected this contention. The court reasoned that Los Angeles, Cal., City Charter § 112(c) was constitutional because plaintiff's interest was not sufficient to require notice and hearing at the administrative level as a constitutional right. The court further determined that the statutory procedure for suspension under Los Angeles, Cal., City Charter § 80(a) was followed and this provision was not an unconstitutional delegation of power to suspend. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of defendant Board of Harbor Commissioners and general manager of Harbor Department's demurrer in plaintiff employee's declaratory action arising out of his work suspension. The court found that the city charter provision that provided for plaintiff's suspension without a hearing was constitutional because plaintiff's interest was not sufficient to require notice and hearing.

 

982 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Vidaurri v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 10668


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


13 Cal. App. 3d 550; 91 Cal. Rptr. 704; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1267



December 16, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner marijuana growers filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) to require it to suppress illegally obtained evidence. Petitioners asserted that the evidence was tainted as a result of an illegal search.


OVERVIEW: An agriculture department inspector entered petitioner marijuana growers' backyard without a warrant and discovered marijuana. He called the police, who also entered the yard without a warrant and arrested petitioners. Petitioners asserted that the evidence should have been suppressed by respondent Superior Court of San Diego, since it was illegally obtained. The court found that under Cal. Civ. Proc. §§ 1822.50-1822.56 inspection by a health officer was permitted only upon issuance of a warrant. The court further found that because the evidence came to light as a result of the inspector's illegal search, the evidence was tainted and inadmissible. Furthermore, the court stated that because petitioners' backyard was fenced and entry limited by a gate, the area was protected from unreasonable government intrusion. The court held that petitioners did not give up their reasonable expectations of backyard privacy simply because a trespassing police officer might stand at the end of the driveway and look over the gate into their yard, at what he had already been told was there. Thus, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate ordering the trial court to suppress the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a preemptory writ of mandate compelling respondent, the Superior Court of San Diego County, to suppress the evidence obtained against petitioners marijuana growers. The court held that the evidence had been illegally obtained as a result of a warrantless search.

 

983 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Eller Telecasting Co.


Civ. No. 9907


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


13 Cal. App. 3d 296; 91 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1239; 20 Rad. Reg. 2d (P ; F) 2131



December 4, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 28, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, Victor A. Gillespie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed; the case is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter judgment for the defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant telecasting company sought review of the judgment entered by the Superior Court of Imperial County (California) for respondent state of California, which granted an injunction prohibiting appellant from transmitting advertisements for optical services into California.


OVERVIEW: Appellant telecasting company was restrained by from transmitting advertisements for optical services or glasses at a stipulated price into respondent state of California. The advertisements were not directed at California residents and no services were offered by the advertising company in California. The trial court based its decision on Cal. Bus. & Prof.. Code § 3129, which prohibited advertisement of optical services at a stipulated price. The court concluded that the injunction prohibiting the broadcast constituted an undue burden on interstate commerce under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court reasoned that § 3129 by regulating optometry in California was a legal exercise of police power, but that applying § 3129 to proscribe acts completely legal in Arizona created a conflict and disruption casting the undue burden upon interstate commerce. The variety of restrictions and requirements that could result from prohibiting the transmission created the undue burden. Additionally, respondent's injunction imposed a direct and discriminatory burden upon appellant because it was engaging in interstate commerce. The trial court was directed to enter judgment for appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of an injunction to respondent state of California that prohibited appellant telecasting company's transmissions because the ads were not directed at California residents and were completely legal in Arizona, the state of origin, and prohibiting the transmissions created an undue burden on interstate commerce.

 

984 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


C.R.W. v. Orr


Civ. No. 12212


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


13 Cal. App. 3d 70; 91 Cal. Rptr. 446; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1219



November 24, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, Robert P. Sullivan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal from the writ is dismissed (see fn. 2). The judgment appealed from is reversed with directions to the trial court to deny the petition, vacate the writ issued and dismiss the proceedings. Defendant is to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, director of the department of motor vehicles, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate in a proceeding challenging the validity of appellant's revocation of respondent minor driver's license pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13355 and from the writ itself.


OVERVIEW: Respondent minor driver was involved in a motor vehicle accident and was charged with felony drunk driving, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23101. The juvenile court found that respondent had violated misdemeanor drunk driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23102, and respondent was placed on probation. The juvenile court forwarded an abstract of judgment to appellant, department of motor vehicles. Respondent did not appeal the juvenile court order. Appellant revoked respondent's license without hearing. The superior court issued a peremptory writ of mandate preventing the revocation. On appeal, the court reversed, citing that essentially respondent was challenging the proceedings of the juvenile court. The court noted that appellant was neither obligated nor authorized to review the factual and procedural background of abstracts filed with it. The court found that Cal. Veh. Code § 13355 stated that revocation was mandatory upon receipt of a report from a juvenile court that the minor had committed one of the offenses enumerated therein. The court opined that the superior court's judgment ordering a writ of mandamus against appellant was without jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of a peremptory writ of mandate. The court found that once the juvenile court convicted respondent minor driver for misdemeanor drunk driving appellant, department of motor vehicles, properly revoked respondent's license without a hearing pursuant to statute. The court noted that respondent's appropriate recourse was to appeal the juvenile conviction, but he failed to do so.

 

985 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re David S.


Civ. No. 27650


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


12 Cal. App. 3d 1124; 91 Cal. Rptr. 261; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



November 23, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 21, 1971. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, Raymond J. Sherwin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Solano County (California) which adjudicated appellant to be a ward of the juvenile court as a person beyond the control of his parents, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 601, and remanded to the custody of respondent probation officer for placement in his own home on probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile left home with his mother's consent to spend the weekend with his friends. However, appellant went to San Diego, where he was apprehended attempting to cross the border into Mexico. Appellant admitted that he had deliberately lied because he knew his mother would not give him permission for the trip. Appellant was found to be a person beyond the control of his parents and, therefore, subject to the jurisdiction of the court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 601. Appellant was declared a ward of the court and remanded to the custody of respondent probation officer for placement in his own home on probation. On appeal, the courts found that appellant was beyond the control of his parent and, therefore, subject to the jurisdiction of the court. The court articulated that the legislature envisioned that while the first sentence of the statute required repeated refusals on the part of the minor to obey his parents, a single act, if sufficiently serious, would indicate that the minor was beyond control. The court opined that the juvenile court properly found that appellant was beyond the control of his parents, and subject to its jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's determination that appellant juvenile was to be a ward of the juvenile court as a person beyond the control of his parents and placed with respondent probation officer. The court noted that a single act, sufficiently serious, indicated that the minor was beyond control of parent and subject to be held a ward of the court.

 

986 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Savoy Club v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 35549


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


12 Cal. App. 3d 1034; 91 Cal. Rptr. 198; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1691



November 19, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 15, 1970, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 28, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Richard Schauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant petitioners challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellants' request for a writ of mandate directing respondents, Welfare Commission and Board of Supervisors, to set aside a decision to revoke a license issued to appellants to conduct a card club.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Welfare Commission (commission) revoked appellant petitioners' license to conduct a card club after a hearing conducted without appellants and appellants' attorney who had been notified of the hearing. Appellants filed an action against respondents, Board of Supervisors and commission, seeking a writ of mandate directing respondent commission to set aside its decision. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the writ. Respondent commission's denial of a continuance was not arbitrary or capricious because the commission had granted appellants at least three continuances and appellants' self-incrimination privilege was not a proper basis for a continuance as it could not be asserted prior to testifying. The court held that the hearing should not have been abated pending the outcome of the criminal action pending against appellants based upon the same facts because the hearing was not criminal in nature and any discovery that the prosecution would gain from the hearing was allowed. Respondent commission's failure to make written findings did not prejudice appellants because any findings would have necessarily been against them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant petitioners' request for a writ of mandate directing respondents, Welfare Commission and Board of Supervisors, to set aside the revocation of appellants' license to conduct a card club. Respondent commission's denial of appellants' request for a continuance was not arbitrary or capricious and the failure to make written findings did not prejudice appellants.

G & D Holland Construction Co. v. City of Marysville


Civ. No. 12699


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


12 Cal. App. 3d 989; 91 Cal. Rptr. 227; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1687



November 17, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 15, 1970, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 14, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yuba County, Richard W. Abbe, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss the appeal is denied. The summary judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner construction company appealed an order of Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent city in petitioner's action for a building permit. Respondent filed a motion to dismiss the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner construction company sought a construction permit from respondent city for a project to be built on a block previously rezoned to allow the intended buildings. Respondent denied the permit and rezoned the property as an urgency measure. Petitioner filed suit alleging discriminatory purpose. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent. Petitioner filed for a writ of mandate to compel the permit to be issued and respondent filed a motion to dismiss the appeal. The court reversed the summary judgment, holding that where respondent failed to show how its denial of the permit and subsequent rezoning were relevant to any existing plan or purpose and not solely discriminatory, triable issues of fact existed requiring reversal. The court denied respondent's motion to dismiss on the grounds that it was an effort to seek affirmance of summary judgment by factual claims not made in trial court, and because the rezoning ordinances were susceptible to petitioner's charge of discriminatory purpose.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment in favor of respondent city in petitioner construction company's discrimination action. Where respondent city failed to show how its denial of a construction permit and subsequent rezoning of disputed area was relevant to any existing plan or purpose, triable issues of fact existed as to whether those actions were for a discriminatory purpose. Respondent's motion to dismiss the appeal was denied.

 

988 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miller


Crim. No. 8484


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


12 Cal. App. 3d 922; 91 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1680



November 12, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 6, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Frederick M. Van Sicklen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted defendant of two counts of rape by force and violence and threats of bodily harm, two counts of robbery, and two counts of simple kidnaping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant represented himself in his criminal trial and then contested his conviction. The court affirmed defendants conviction and found that the trial court did not deny defendant of his constitutional right to counsel by dismissing the public defender because defendant repeatedly refused to be represented by a public defender, explicitly stated that he was making arrangements to hire counsel, displayed a high degree of intelligence and a knowledge of criminal law and procedure, indicated a complete awareness of the seriousness of the charges against him, and understood the consequences of dismissing the public defender if he could not retain counsel. The court also found that there was sufficient evidence to support a separate conviction for kidnapping because defendant transported his victims to remote areas where he could safely commit his other crimes. The movement was not merely incidental to his plan to rape and rob.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction because defendant made a knowing and intelligent waiver of his right to counsel when he demanded the dismissal of his public defender before hiring his own counsel.

 

989 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. House


Crim. Nos. 17842, 18093


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


12 Cal. App. 3d 756; 90 Cal. Rptr. 831; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1666



November 6, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 30, 1970. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Bernard S. Selber and D. Sterry Fagan, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in A056766 is affirmed. The judgment in A227715 is affirmed. The writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a conviction of voluntary manslaughter in Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Defendant claimed that prior felony convictions were admitted into evidence when he testified, defendant's trial counsel failed to impeach certain witnesses, and defendant was excluded from lawyer conferences in the judge's chambers, violating U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought reversal, including habeas corpus, of a voluntary manslaughter conviction in the trial court. Defendant claimed that prosecution introduced defendant's prior convictions when defendant testified, defendant's trial counsel failed to impeach witnesses, and defendant's right to confrontation under U.S. Const. amend. VI was denied when defendant was not allowed in meetings in the judge's chambers. Defendant's conviction and sentencing for assault with a deadly from another trial court was consolidated by the court. The court affirmed both convictions and denied defendant's habeas corpus, holding that under Cal. Evid. Code § 788, the prior convictions were admissible for impeachment when defendant testified and that the trial court gave an instruction informing the jury that prior convictions could only be used to determine a witness' credibility, which cured any prejudice. The court further held that U.S. Const. amend. VI, only required defendant to be present during presentation of evidence and the decisions of defendant's trial counsel regarding impeaching witnesses was a discretionary trial tactic decision and not grounds for reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants convictions, because prior convictions could be used by prosecution to impeach defendant when he testified, particularly with a limiting instruction to the jury by the court, and defendant was not denied his right to confrontation of witnesses by being excluded from conferences in judge's chambers.

 

990 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Herrera


Crim. No. 840


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


12 Cal. App. 3d 629; 90 Cal. Rptr. 802; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1654



November 2, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, Paul R. Borton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Kern County (California), for possession of marijuana for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11503.5 and possession of restricted dangerous drugs for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11911 after he was stopped and his vehicle was searched by the border patrol.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was stopped by the border patrol and his car searched, without a warrant, for illegal aliens. The border patrol found large quantities of marijuana, secobarbital and amphetamine sulphate in appellant's trunk and he was convicted of possession of marijuana for sale and possession of restricted dangerous drugs for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11503.5, 11911. The trial court refused to suppress the evidence found in appellant's trunk and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the border patrol had the authority to stop appellant and search his car for illegal aliens pursuant to the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.S. § 1357, within the 100-mile limit established in 8 C.F.R. 287.1(a). The court found that, once the trunk was opened to search for illegal aliens, the officers were not required to blind themselves to the drugs in plain sight and that they were not required to Mirandize appellant before asking him what was in the box in his trunk. Because appellant was not in custodial interrogation at the time and was simply being detained, his U.S. Const. amend. V rights were not violated by the lack of Miranda warnings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for possession of marijuana for sale and possession of restricted dangerous drugs for sale because evidence seized by the border patrol was properly admitted under federal law and the United States Constitution.

People v. Cohen


Crim. No. 15353


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


12 Cal. App. 3d 298; 90 Cal. Rptr. 612; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1629



October 26, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 24, 1970, and the petition of appellant Theodore Cohen for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 14, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction of appellant Susan Cohen is reversed. The judgment of conviction of appellant Theodore Cohen is modified to read as follows: "Whereas the said defendant having been duly found guilty in this court of the crime of violation of section 182.1 and 4, Penal Code, a felony, as charged in count 1 of the indictment, and grand theft (Sec. 487. 1 PC), a felony, as charged in each of counts 2, 3. 4, 5, 8, 9, 11, 12, and 14, it is therefore Ordered, Adjudged and Decreed that said defendant be punished by imprisonment in the State Prison for the term prescribed by law on said counts. Execution of the sentence on counts 2, 3, 4, 5, 8, 9, 11, 12, and 14 is stayed, the stay to become permanent upon the satisfaction of the sentence on count 1." The judgment as modified is affirmed as to Theodore Cohen.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband sought review of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of conspiracy to commit grand theft and forgery and to obtain money by false pretenses in addition to nine counts of grand theft. Defendant wife sought review of her conviction of one count of grand theft.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were involved in a scheme to defraud an insurer. Defendant husband was an attorney who conspired with an insurance agent and others to present false claims to an insurer. A false claim was submitted on behalf of defendant wife. Defendant wife was convicted of one count of grand theft, but the court reversed her conviction because the record did not contain a waiver of her right to separate counsel from her pro se defendant husband. Defendant husband was convicted of conspiracy to commit grand theft and forgery and to obtain money by false pretenses, as well as nine counts of grand theft. Defendants made many challenges to their convictions including the grand jury procedures, continuances, their pro se representation, discovery motions, and the charges of which they were convicted. Defendant husband did not establish any grounds for reversal of his convictions, but the court modified the judgment due to a clerical error and a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 654, prohibiting sentences which impose punishment for conspiracy to commit illegal acts, in this case grand theft, and for the illegal acts if the conspiracy had no objective other than the specific acts charged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant husband because he did not establish any grounds for reversal, but punishment modification was necessary to remedy a clerical error and to eliminate the punishment for conspiracy only to commit grand theft when he was already being punished for grand theft. The court reversed the judgment convicting defendant wife because the record lacked an effective waiver of her right to separate counsel.

Campbell Chain Co. v. County of Alameda


Civ. Nos. 26094, 26418


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


12 Cal. App. 3d 248; 90 Cal. Rptr. 501; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1625



October 23, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 20, 1970, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 21, 1971. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Monroe Friedman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments in favor of respondents are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tax payers sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which rendered judgment in favor of respondent counties in an action to recover property taxes.


OVERVIEW: Respondent counties applied different discount rates to different classes of property in order to arrive at an assessment value. Appellant paid property taxes under protest and filed an action claiming respondents assessments were unfair and discriminatory. The court affirmed the trial court judgment in favor of respondents and found that respondents were within their discretion to apply different discount rates to different classes of personal property because the inherent differences in personal property and business personal property required respondents to use slightly different formulas to fairly measure the full cash value of the property. Once full cash value was determined, respondent applied the same assessment ration to each class of property to get the assessed value. The court also found that the trial court did not err in excluding the state board ratio or depositions of the Assessor and his deputy from the trial because the state board ratio was not final and not available to individual taxpayers and the appellants were not prejudiced by the exclusion of the depositions when they had opportunity to take testimony from the Assessor and his deputy but chose not to.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent counties in appellant taxpayers' action to recover property taxes because applying different formulas to determine full cash value of the property was not inherently unfair.

 

993 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Johnston v. Cunningham


Civ. No. 12305


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


12 Cal. App. 3d 123; 90 Cal. Rptr. 487; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1613



October 20, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sutter County, Edmund T. Manwell, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to determine title in accordance with the provisions of section 689, Code of Civil Procedure, and to take such other proceedings consistent with this opinion as may be appropriate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff creditor appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Sutter County (California), which sustained a release of attachment by the constable under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 689 and allowed the claim of third party claimant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff creditor attached defendant debtor's prune crop. Third party claimant delivered his claim to the constable who mailed a copy to plaintiff. The claim contained no written demand for an undertaking. Plaintiff petitioned for a hearing to determine third party's title to the prunes, but prior to the hearing, the constable released the attachment. The prunes were sold. At the hearing, the trial court declared that there had been "substantial compliance" with the statute by the constable. On plaintiff's appeal, the court held that, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 689, a written demand was a prerequisite to the creditor's obligation to post an undertaking. Because the constable had no authority to release the attached property except by compliance with § 689, and because there was no written demand, the court held that title should have been determined. The court also rejected third party's contention that sale of the prunes had mooted the matter and stated that, if the title determination were adverse to third party, remedies could then be considered. The court reversed the trial court's order and remanded with directions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which had sustained a release of attachment by the constable in favor of a third party claimant, and remanded to the trial court with directions to determine title as requested by plaintiff creditor.

Knudsen Dairy Products Co. v. State Board of Equalization


Civ. No. 35310


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


12 Cal. App. 3d 47; 90 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1606



October 19, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 17, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Martin Katz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporations sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that plaintiffs were not entitled to a refund of sales and use taxes that were assessed by defendant board of equalization in a sales transaction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation, acquired ownership of the outstanding stock of a food company that was indebted to its parent corporation. The food company transferred all of its assets to plaintiff at the direction of the parent corporation, in exchange for a promissory note. The parent corporation reduced the food company's indebtedness, in the amount of the value of the assets transferred. During an audit of the food company by defendant board of equalization, an auditor discovered that the food corporation owed additional taxes as a result of the sale of assets and plaintiff paid the taxes under protest. Plaintiffs brought an action to recover the taxes and the lower court held that plaintiff was liable for the taxes as a purchaser. Plaintiff sought review. On review the court affirmed. The court held that the execution of the promissory note was sufficient to show that plaintiff was a purchaser of the food company even though it received the assets of the transaction at the direction of its parent corporation. The court held that plaintiff was a successor to the food company in the transaction and was liable for its tax liability pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 6811,6812.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was affirmed, because the lower court did not err when it held that plaintiff corporation was a purchaser in a sales transaction and therefore liable for the outstanding tax liability of the seller.

Keane v. Mihaly


Civ. No. 28707


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


11 Cal. App. 3d 1037; 90 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1799



October 17, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A peremptory writ will issue commanding respondent registrar of voters to register petitioners as voters.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorneys filed a petition for a writ of mandate with the Supreme Court of California, which assigned the case to another court. The case involved a one year residency requirement for voting set forth in Cal. Const. art. II, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney's filed a writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of the one-year residency requirement for voting in California. Petitioners were attorneys who were rejected for voter registration because of the one year requirement. On appeal, the court let the peremptory writ issue. The court found that the issue was not moot and applied a compelling interest standard in reviewing the issue. The court held that the one-year residency requirement for voting, Cal. Const. art. II, § 1, did not meet the standard of necessity to enforce a compelling state interest and was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court let a peremptory writ issue in petitioner attorneys' challenge of the constitutionality of a one year residency voting requirement. The court held that the requirement did not meet a compelling interest.

In re Thomas G.


Civ. No. 26932


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


11 Cal. App. 3d 1193; 90 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1809



October 15, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, John J. Purchio, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from, described as "judgment" in the notice of appeal, are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the orders of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which found that he had violated the provisions of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910, declared him a ward of the court, and placed him under the supervision of the court's probation officer. Defendant argued that the conduct of school officials leading up to the discovery of contraband on his person was violative of his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to have violated the provisions of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11910 and therefore to be a person described by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, declared to be a ward of the court, and placed under the supervision of the court's probation officer. Defendant contended that the conduct of school officials leading up to the discovery of contraband on his person was violative of his U.S. Const. amend. IV rights. Although the court stated that it was impelled to conclude that the conduct of the officials was reasonable and without amend. IV taint, it reversed the orders of the trial court. The court observed that the trial court had applied the preponderance of the evidence standard and that proof beyond a reasonable doubt had been held to be a U.S. Const. amend XIV due process requirement applicable against state juvenile courts. The court deemed it proper to reverse the trial court's decision and remand the cause to the trial court for reconsideration.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the cause to the trial court for reconsideration. The court found that while the trial court had applied the preponderance of evidence standard to defendant's allegations that his Fourth Amendment rights had been violated, the appropriate standard was proof beyond a reasonable doubt.
